AI-Mediated Communication and Information: Political Bias in LLM Information Processing

    • Instrument-centre norms
    • Partisan symmetry — matched pairs, party-proximity &amp; self-anchored
    • Even-handedness &amp; calibration
    • Distribution-matching norms
    • Pluralism / diversity norms
    • Perception / approval norms

Reference

AI-Mediated Communication and Information: Political Bias in LLM Information Processing

As large language models increasingly filter, select, summarise and aggregate political information — often in place of web search — the political bias they introduce in these information-processing tasks becomes consequential for how people form and revise political beliefs, and for democratic discourse. This review synthesises the evidence and arguments on that problem: bias in LLM summarisation, selection and retrieval; how it depends on the surrounding information environment (the source material a model is given); self-preference and homogenisation as content increasingly becomes LLM-written; which perspectives get marginalised beyond the usual US left–right axis; and how such bias is defined and measured. Cognitive offloading is treated as the channel by which users delegate political judgement to LLMs. Deliberate persuasion and human–AI/socioaffective relationships are noted only as adjacent literatures.

    • Concentration of power, controllability, and political economy
    • Autonomy, freedom of thought, and human formation
    • Trust, legitimacy, and the integrity of shared information
    • Justice, equity, and the unequal distribution of benefits and harms
    • Whose voices are included: participation as a governance stake
    • Environmental and sustainability stakes
    • Methodological, scholarly, and evidentiary stakes
    • Governance, regulation, and accountability
    • Explicit normative anchoring and the closing window
  • 2.5 — Normative considerations for evaluating AI-mediated information production and consumption
    • 1. Meta-level: how, and against what values, AI should be evaluated at all
    • 2. Autonomy, agency, and non-domination as the master criterion
    • 3. Truthfulness, accuracy, and epistemic quality
    • 4. Manipulation versus legitimate persuasion
    • 5. Neutrality, impartiality, and balance — and the "neutrality is impossible" position
    • 6. Pluralism, value-diversity, and representation (anti-homogenisation; anti-WEIRD skew)
    • 7. Sycophancy, dependence, and long-term wellbeing
    • 8. Deliberation, fair aggregation, and democratic legitimacy
    • 9. Faithful representation of the writer (AI writing assistance)
    • 10. Transparency, accountability, contestability, and provenance
    • 11. Personalisation: bounds, fiduciary duty, and agentic (in)equality
    • 12. Governance, regulation, and institutional/economic design
    • 13. Methodological and evaluative norms
    • 14. Recurrent cross-cutting trade-offs and cautions

This part synthesises the de-duplicated evidence base across five dimensions: that AI mediation is increasing; its measured consequences; consequences theorised but not measured; why the trend matters; and proposed normative criteria.

2.1 — Evidence for the increasing trend of AI-mediated communication and information production and consumption

↑ top
On this section (14)
  • 1. Mass adoption: headline figures and growth rates
  • 2. AI as an information-seeking and news intermediary, displacing search
  • 3. AI writing assistance pervading social and political life
  • 4. AI embedded directly in platforms and the public conversation
  • 5. AI-mediated companionship, advice, and human–AI relationships
  • 6. Measured AI influence on real-world human output (text and speech)
  • 7. Salience, incidents, and public concern (proxies)
  • 8. AI mediating group deliberation, governance, and collective decision-making
  • 9. Large-scale persuasion and influence operations as existence proofs
  • 10. The shift toward agentic AI and personalisation
  • 11. The pre-existing algorithmic-curation baseline
  • 12. Proliferation of models and recorded human–AI interaction
  • 13. Conceptual / normative sources asserting the trend as a premise
  • 14. Direction and limits of the evidence

Corpus evidence that AI systems — overwhelmingly LLMs and their platforms — have become a routine intermediary in producing and consuming information. A small set of surveys, usage logs, and observational studies carries the measured weight; most sources assert the trend qualitatively. (Collapsed duplicates: P29/P37 = same Tessler et al. paper, "Can AI Mediation Improve Democratic Deliberation?"; P42/P43 = two versions of Lazar & Manuali, "Using LLMs to Enhance Democracy". P35 is a separate Tessler et al. 2024 Science paper, "AI can help humans find common ground", kept distinct.)

1. Mass adoption: headline figures and growth rates

↑ top

Sources converge on hundreds of millions to ~a billion users, but figures and framings differ.

  • Strongest population figures, International AI Safety Report 2026: "almost a billion people now use general-purpose AI systems in their daily lives" (P40, Bengio et al. 2026, p.14); ≥700 million use leading systems weekly, ChatGPT ~700 million weekly, up from 200 million a year earlier (P40, pp.10, 55); US workers using general-purpose AI rose 30% (Dec 2024) to 46% (mid-2025) (P40, p.88). AI "has diffused faster than the internet or the personal computer" in the US (P40, Fig. 2.14, p.85, citing Bick et al. 2024). The 200-million figure is independently traceable — P04 (Röttger et al. 2025) cites Reuters (2024).
  • The "fastest-growing consumer app" datum — ChatGPT over 100 million users in ~two months — appears in P10 (Kirk et al. 2024), P14 (Rystrøm et al. 2025, citing Milmo 2023), S10 (Yakura et al. 2025). US: 34% of adults have used ChatGPT, "about double the share in 2023" (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025, citing Pew, ref 89).
  • "Hundreds of millions" recurs as shorthand: P05 (Röttger et al. 2026); P10 (Kirk et al. 2024); P14 (Rystrøm et al. 2025); P23 (Summerfield et al. 2025, "monthly users"); P24 (Luettgau et al. 2026); P28 (Marchal et al. 2026); P41 (Kapoor et al. 2025, "platform-operated chatbots", citing Backlinko 2025); A01 (Mosleh et al. 2026, "ChatGPT, Gemini, and Claude").
  • Lower-bound/product estimates: P27 (Luettgau et al. 2026) gives "up to 10% of the global population" regularly accessing LLMs and GPT-4o at "an estimated 800m global users" (pp.1–2). P49 (Huang et al. 2025) counts decisions — "every day, millions of these value-laden decisions shape how AI assistants respond" (p.1).
  • National surveys: in Sweden a majority report using a chatbot in the past year, over a quarter weekly; "similar trends" across the UK and Canada, including politicians, policymakers, practitioners (A01, p.1).
  • Concentration and unevenness (geography partly disputed). Usage is "highly concentrated, with hundreds of millions relying on a small number of systems such as ChatGPT, Gemini, and Claude" (A01, p.1). Adoption ranges from >50% of working-age population in the UAE and Singapore to under 10% in many lower-income economies; within the US, sector usage runs 18% (Information) to 1.4% (Construction/Agriculture) (P40, Figs. 2.14–2.15, pp.85–86). Against a rich-country story, P33 (Sharp et al. 2026) reports relatively high usage in some middle-income countries (Nigeria, India), arguing natural-language interfaces broaden adoption (citing Similarweb 2026, Semrush 2026).
  • An age gradient recurs: among 666 respondents, 17–25-year-olds reported highest reliance and frequency, 46+ lowest (S08, Gerlich 2025) — echoing the youth skew in companionship/advice usage (P13, Kirk et al. 2026; S07, Cheng et al. 2026).
  • Premise-only framings: P09 (Röttger et al. 2025) — "GPAI models are already very widely used", names channels (chatgpt.com, labs.perplexity.ai, huggingface.co/chat; APIs from OpenAI, Amazon Bedrock, Hyperbolic, Replicate), expects a power-law distribution with systemic-risk trigger at "millions of users", lamenting "very little public information" on per-model counts (§3.3); P28 (Marchal et al. 2026, "unprecedented surge"); P07 (Russo et al. 2026); P12 (Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026, "ubiquitous intermediaries of human knowledge"); P20 (Yang et al. 2026); P50 (Sorensen et al. 2024); S06 (Atari et al. 2023).

2. AI as an information-seeking and news intermediary, displacing search

↑ top

AI is becoming a primary channel for seeking information and news, rivalling or replacing search.

  • Information-seeking is the most common use case: top ChatGPT conversation category (P03, Renault et al. 2025, p.4); in the NBER (2025) breakdown, top uses are seeking information (≈18.3% specific info), writing, practical guidance/tutoring (10.2%) (P40, Fig. 2.17, p.91); in PRISM "research overviews" was the most popular self-reported task (49% of users), ahead of professional work (37%), creative writing (31%), programming help (27%) (P16, Kirk et al. 2024); in INFINITY-CHAT (mined from WildChat) 15.2% of open-ended queries are "Brainstorm & Ideation" (P45, Jiang et al. 2025). Users call it "a much better search engine" (P12, §4.2.1).
  • AI is framed as replacing search. P24 (Luettgau et al. 2026): conversational AI "rapidly replace[s] traditional search engines" and "now sits alongside internet search as a commonly used source of information during high-stakes political moments like national elections." P28 (Marchal et al. 2026): "rivaling traditional search engines", "often supplanting traditional search-based methods." AI is "already embedded in search, social media, content creation, and everyday information-seeking" (A01, p.1). Search engines insert LLM answers in place of links: Bing and Google give "automatically generated content… instead of links" (S01, Jungherr 2023); GenAI is integrated into Google and Bing search (P26, Simon & Altay 2025); Gemini 3 Pro powers "summaries and chat functionality available in Google Search" (P27).
  • News consumption via GenAI rises at smaller magnitudes than headline figures. In an 8-country 2024 survey (Argentina, Denmark, France, Japan, UK, US), 24% used GenAI for information and 5% for the latest news (P26, citing Fletcher & Nielsen 2024); latest-news use rose to 7% by the 2025 Digital News Report (YouGov, 92,000+ consumers, 46 markets) (P26, citing Newman et al. 2025). More Americans rely on social/digital platforms for news, "with a growing use of ChatGPT for learning" (P46, Fisher et al. 2025, citing Pew 2024). News summarisation and fake-news detection are mainstream deployed functions (P46; P48, Fisher et al. 2025, benchmarking ten production/open models — GPT-4o, GPT-4o-Mini, Gemini-1.5 Flash and Pro, Claude-3.5 Sonnet, R1, Llama-3.3 70B, OLMo-2 13B, R1-Distill-Llama 70B, Qwen2.5 72B — as deployed handlers of political queries).
  • Political information. 9% of a representative sample of UK eligible voters (N=2,499) used chatbots for political information in the four weeks around the 2024 election; among chatbot users (N=1,024), 32.0% researched "UK current affairs or political issues" — the most common use, statistically tied with "work or education" (31.4%; McNemar's χ²(1)=0.1, p=0.753) — implying as many as 13% of eligible UK voters used conversational AI for electorally-relevant information (P24, pp.3–4). Other uses: "other" 28.2%, writing/translation 22.9%, practical matters 21.7%, fun 20.0%, personal issues 18.3%, legal/financial 10.8% (P24, Fig. 2A). Conversational AI "increases political knowledge as effectively as self-directed internet search" (P24, title/Significance Statement).
  • LLM fact-checking on social media is "rapidly scaling" (P03): user-facing bots launched on X in 2025 (Perplexity late Feb, Grok's in-house bot early March, which "rapidly surpassed Perplexity, becoming the dominant LLM-powered assistant on the platform"); 1,555,281 English fact-check requests Feb–Sep 2025 (broader EuropePMC set 1,671,841); 18.6 million in-sample English tweets mention an LLM bot and get a reply, of which "@grok is this true?" alone is ~2.4% (447,083 tweets); fact-checking is ≥7.5% of bot interactions (abstract: 7.6%); LLM fact-checks have a ~2-minute median delay versus ~18 hours for Community Notes — "qualitatively consistent with broader evidence on LLM usage in practice" (P03). X's Community Notes and Taiwan's verification tools are cited as live AI-adjacent aggregation "shown to be effective in identifying… low-quality information" (P29/P37, Tessler et al.).
  • AI also produces news/reference content at scale: ~9% of US news articles are partially or fully AI-generated (P28, citing Russell et al. 2025); entire encyclopedias are now created with these models (P28); outlets use LLMs to find trends and write copy (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026; P05, Röttger et al. 2026). LLMs have "to date… only harmed the public sphere through the generation of search engine-optimised slop that supercharges the work of content farms" (P42/P43). DebunkBot (debunkbot.com), the conspiracy-debunking agent, was reportedly used by ~65,000 people (S04, Costello et al. 2024, secondary reporting, not the paper's own measurement).
  • LLM-derived landscape estimate (made using LLMs, not a measure of AI content): ~600 million blogs exist (≈ one-third of all websites), 100–150M run professionally by universities/media/civil society, ~60M paywalled (P02, Dunleavy & Margetts 2026).

3. AI writing assistance pervading social and political life

↑ top

AI is now a routine co-author of texts on which social, professional, and political life depend.

  • Writing assistance is "one of the most popular LLM use cases" — essays, articles, song lyrics about issues users care about (P04, Röttger et al. 2025; P06, Lim & Röttger 2026, both citing Zhao et al. 2024 and Zheng et al. 2024). Scale: "monthly requests for AI writing assistance now rising to the tens of billions" and "billions of user requests" (P05, pp.2, 9).
  • AI-assisted writing "already pervades the communications and documents on which social and political life depend" — "political speeches delivered in national legislatures, news articles published in major media outlets, legal arguments presented in court as well as academic publications and peer reviews" (P05, pp.2, 9). P01 (Tsirtsis et al. 2026) echoes: universities encourage staff/students to integrate AI; policymakers, lawyers, doctors rely on it to research, summarise, draft. P05 stresses adoption came "before its distortive effects were understood."
  • Usage skews to text: NBER writing tasks include editing/critiquing (10.6%), argument/summary generation (3.6%), translation (4.5%) (P40, Fig. 2.17); UK chatbot users 22.9% writing/translation (P24, Fig. 2A).
  • Given the huge base, "even small but consistent biases could plausibly have large societal impacts" (P04, p.13). IssueBench's five datasets index real usage: LMSYS-1m (1m conversations, 25 LLMs), ShareGPT (90.7k), WildChat (652.1k), HH-Online (23.1k), PRISM (8.0k) — 1.77m first-turn prompts (P04).
  • People accept AI-suggested text: in an RCT, an LLM generated 2,742 political-message rewrites and users accepted ~two-thirds (1,798) into real human-to-human conversation, "easily scaled to online settings and implemented broadly" (S09, Argyle et al. 2023). (See §6.)

4. AI embedded directly in platforms and the public conversation

↑ top
  • GenAI is "widely embedded in major online platforms" — LinkedIn improves posts, YouTube generates video summaries, X provides context on posts (P01). LinkedIn's "Improve my post"-style help "is already deployed at scale", and X's "Explain this post"/Grok contextualisation is a released feature whose official prompt template the authors replicate (P01).
  • Real-time AI sits inside political threads. Grok is integrated into X "where users can summon an LLM into public conversation threads"; X restricted in-thread "Ask Grok" to Premium users in March 2026 (A01, p.5). GenAI is integrated into Meta's Facebook, Instagram, WhatsApp, Messenger, plus X (P26).
  • Public-sector deployment: AI writes policy, streamlines governance, supports expert polling (P23, Table 1); Porto Alegre passed "the first law written entirely by an LLM" (Nov 2023; P23, ref 106); the UK launched govgpt.uk answering queries including forthcoming elections (P23); "LLMs are increasingly assisting policymakers worldwide, from China's use in foreign policy to the U.S.'s legislative drafting and South Africa's parliamentary information systems" (P46). Ministers have touted LLMs for summarising public consultation submissions, with "several active projects" (P42/P43). (See §8.)
  • SEO agencies "commercially offer… optimization for appearing in ChatGPT responses" (C01, Koralus 2025).
  • Platform reach (consumption baseline): Google has "close to 90%" of search market share (P41, citing StatCounter 2024); Meta's Facebook, Instagram, WhatsApp are three of the top four social platforms "with over 3 billion users" (P41, citing Shopify 2024).
  • "Hundreds of millions of people now interact with AI-generated content daily, through chat assistants, social media, customer service bots, companion apps… This content can shape their opinions, purchasing decisions, and actions" (P40, p.50). LLM chatbots are "rapidly being integrated into… commercial… virtual assistants, to public sector… administrative services" (P34, Bergman et al. 2024), and into "commonly-used products and services such as Bing Search and Microsoft Office" (P10). Value-laden decisions are pervasive: across 700K Claude.ai conversations (Feb 18–25 2025), 308,210 (44.0%) were subjective, AI values appeared on average 4.0 times per conversation and were absent in only 1.4% of subjective conversations (P49, Huang et al. 2025).

5. AI-mediated companionship, advice, and human–AI relationships

↑ top

Adjacent. A fast-growing slice of usage is personal and emotional. In a census-representative UK sample (N=2,028), 33% used AI for companionship/social/emotional support in the past year, concentrated among younger, heavy users (P13, Kirk et al. 2026); advice on health, careers, relationships is among the most common uses, with people "readily follow[ing]" it though it "does not improve their well-being" (P27; S07, Cheng et al. 2026). Trends toward more personalised and agentic AI raise the chance users perceive a relationship, not a mere interaction (P15, Kirk et al. 2025). This bears on dependence and sycophancy but is only adjacent to political information processing.

6. Measured AI influence on real-world human output (text and speech)

↑ top
  • Strongest evidence is on spoken language. Yakura et al. find an abrupt post-ChatGPT rise in "delve" across 360,445 academic YouTube talks (synthetic-control placebo p=0.010, discrepancy peaking exactly at the 30 Nov 2022 release); many top-20 GPT words show ~25–50% annual growth in spoken usage with no top word significantly decreasing; the effect extends to 771,591 podcast episodes ("delve" rises in all categories except Sports — Science & Technology p=0.03, Business p=0.04, Education p=0.04); corpus 740,249 hours / 7.35 billion words; framed as "the first large-scale empirical evidence that AI-driven language shifts are propagating beyond written text into spontaneous spoken communication" (S10, Yakura et al. 2025). A01 (Mosleh et al. 2026) and P28 (Marchal et al. 2026) cite this as LLM influence on human output (incl. vocabulary); A01 also cites LLM influence on scientific production.
  • Suspicion of AI-generated messages is rising: ~5% early 2023 (Bai et al.), ~15% mid-2023 (Hackenburg & Margetts 2024), >22% in this late-2023 study, where "AI language model" was the most popular suspected author for both human and AI messages (P25, Hackenburg et al. 2025). AI content is harder to distinguish, image realism improving (DALL-E 2 → Nano Banana Pro) since the Jan 2025 report (P40, pp.45, 48); users find it "increasingly challenging to distinguish AI-generated from human-generated content", LLMs mimicking human writing and "thus gaining credibility" (S02, Salvi et al. 2024/2025).
  • Field deployment: an LLM-backed filter in a live NGO gun-violence-reporting workflow was used daily by all five analysts despite being optional (P11, Belisario et al. 2025, n=5). (S09's AI-rewrite acceptance is the writing-stage analogue — see §3.)
  • Models are "starting to impact what we know and how we act: from our political beliefs and preferences… to our vocabulary choices… and our cognitive abilities" (P28, citing Costello et al. 2024, Hackenburg et al. 2025, Potter et al. 2024, Schoenegger et al. 2025, Yakura et al. 2025, Lee et al. 2025, Stadler et al. 2024). OpenAI confirmed "several state actors have already used their language models to build and operate covert influence operations" (P08, Hackenburg et al. 2025, citing OpenAI 2024). Prior research shows AI can shift voter preferences, change moral stances, drive purchases, and move people to sign petitions and donate (P19, Hackenburg et al. 2026, refs 13, 22, 23, 33).

7. Salience, incidents, and public concern (proxies)

↑ top
  • Media coverage of GenAI surged after the Nov 2022 launch — peaking at >1% of weekly stories across ~1,500 English sources (P26, Fig.1); attention "rose tenfold — from already historically high levels" six months in (P26, citing Ryazanov et al. 2025); "AI" + "elections" coverage grew since 2022 (recently declining for "+threat"), and AI-term coverage now exceeds climate-change coverage (P26, Figs. 4–6).
  • Media-reported AI incidents involving "content generation" (incl. deepfakes) in the OECD Monitor rose sharply since 2021, reaching ~200–250 monthly by late 2025 (P40, Fig. 2.1). Deepfake videos hit 2024 elections in India, Indonesia, Mexico, Pakistan, Slovakia, Ukraine, USA, Taiwan, "many fake videos being viewed hundreds of thousands of times", aided by multimodal breakthroughs that "greatly expanded opportunities" for realistic audio/video (P23, p.2424); "AI-supported election interference is no longer hypothetical" — Taiwan, India, Indonesia, US 2024 campaigns saw deepfakes and fabricated news outlets (P36, Schroeder et al. 2026).
  • Concern is widespread: a majority in all 29 countries polled worry about AI manipulating public opinion (P08, citing Ipsos 2023); in 2024 "over 40% of the global population heads to the polls" (P08). Ad-spend scale: $1.35 billion on online campaign ads on Google and Meta in the 2024 US presidential election (>$15 billion across the cycle) (P26).

8. AI mediating group deliberation, governance, and collective decision-making

↑ top

AI is moving into the collective machinery of democracy — deliberation, drafting law and policy, aggregation.

  • "The field of AI-enabled deliberation is growing quickly" (P29/P37, citing Argyle et al. 2023, Kim et al. 2020, Ma et al. 2025, Shin et al. 2022, Small et al. 2023), a recognised expanding area (P35, Tessler et al. 2024; P39, Jarrett et al. 2025).
  • The Habermas Machine is a working LLM that ingests real opinions and authors collective political statements, able to run "in an autonomous mode to act as the mediator itself" (P29/P37; P35). In the separate Science experiment, AI tools summarise perspectives, generate range-of-view statements, temper partisan debate via "equanimous rewrites", and mitigate gender imbalance (P35, refs 10–13).
  • Mediation at human scale: a fine-tuned 70B Chinchilla LLM mediated deliberation among 2,290 UK participants across 1,662 episodes, generating 6,872 opinions/critiques all flowing through the AI mediator, with the debate-question corpus itself AI-generated (175 seeds expanded to 10,000, filtered to 5,645) (P39).
  • Deployed systems (Pol.is, Remesh) are already augmented with ML clustering and are the baseline the Habermas Machine improves on (P29/P37). Lazar & Manuali name vTaiwan/Recursive Public (Polis + Talk to the City) producing actual AI policy guidelines, and Meta's deliberative polls setting VR bullying/harassment rules (P42/P43). France, Canada, Iceland, Ireland have run large citizens' assemblies AI could scale (P35). A graduate-seminar topic ("Using AI agents to facilitate human discussion") presupposes AI facilitators are deployed (A02, Koralus & McCord 2026).
  • A technical limit fell: largest context windows "have grown approximately 1,000x… between 2022 and 2025" (P29/P37, §3.2.1).
  • A "computational political theory" subfield emerged post-GPT-4 (early 2023): "over the last two years AI engineers, computer scientists, and even hobbyists have sought to build applications using LLMs for practically every conceivable function", spawning an LLMs-for-democracy literature with a derived four-role taxonomy that "newer papers continu[e] to fall into"; LLMs are "a step-change in what is feasible with AI", opening building "to anyone, not just engineers" (P42/P43).
  • LLM simulation of humans — economic agents (Horton), human-subject studies (Aher et al.), social-science subpopulations (Argyle et al.), "generative agents" (Park et al.) — is an active area (P39).
  • AI is "already affecting the graduate labour market" (P23, Table 1, ref 120), and LLMs "already have wide user bases, totalling hundreds of millions of monthly users" (P23).
  • Citizens gain capabilities too: "armed with powerful digital social media for networking and new analytic synthesis capabilities", able to "employ AI-synthesized data-collation and analysis of government messages and documents" and "use online chatbots or LLMs" for republican vigilance (P02).
  • Counter-case / early-stage. Deep mechanism design (AI designing economic/social policy) "remains largely at a proof-of-concept stage" and is "not ready for deployment in the real world" (P38, Tacchetti et al. 2025).

9. Large-scale persuasion and influence operations as existence proofs

↑ top

Adjacent (deliberate persuasion). Existence proofs that AI can author and deploy persuasion at scale: LLM persuasion operational across 91,000+ dialogues with ~77,000 people (P21, Hackenburg et al. 2025); AI throughput vastly exceeds humans' (~37 fact-checkable claims/conversation vs elite debaters; P19, Hackenburg et al. 2026); a commercial market for AI-enabled influence operations exists (P36, Schroeder et al. 2026). These bear on the broader influence environment but sit outside the report's information-processing focus.

  • On-focus exception — training-data contamination ("LLM Grooming"). The pro-Kremlin "Pravda" network appears "purpose-built for machine consumption" (duplication across hundreds of domains, poor UIs, low human traffic), its primary audience the crawlers that feed LLMs (P36) — a direct attack on what LLMs retrieve. Online manipulation is now "driven increasingly by domestic political elites and parties… alongside foreign state actors", targeting Brexit and elections in the US, Brazil, the Philippines (P36).

10. The shift toward agentic AI and personalisation

↑ top
  • Developers are "shifting their focus to building agents that can operate independently, with little human intervention" (P32, Gabriel et al. 2025). Language Model Agents "have become a major research and product focus of the major AI companies", with Anthropic, Google DeepMind, OpenAI shipping browser/virtual-computer agents (Claude computer use, Project Mariner); "most AI companies now offer a 'Deep Research' product"; SWE agents do "economically valuable coding work" and firms automate customer service (P41). Salesforce and Nvidia "are already offering customer-services solutions… using agents" (P32). AI agents "are beginning to perform economically valuable workflows" (P33).
  • The expected trajectory is sweeping: LMAs to "ultimately undertake most tasks for which people currently use the internet — including the procurement of products, services, and content" (P41); assistants fulfilling "complex multistep requests" end-to-end, e.g. switching a mobile-phone contract (retrieving options, authorizing, cancelling, arranging bank transfers) (P32); agents as "near-constant companions — much like smartphones today", with "a world populated by millions of autonomous AI agents" facing societal challenges (P32). Frontier systems "may be deployed on digital platforms (such as Reddit or Discord, or social media sites such as X, Bluesky or Truth Social) to persuade citizens" (P23). The premise that "individuals will increasingly rely on AI agents to navigate life's growing complexities" structures an Oxford seminar treating recursive self-improvement / "automating AI research" as a live trend (A02; C01, Koralus 2025). McKinsey forecasts a $2.6–4.4 trillion annual GenAI windfall once agents deploy (P32) — a projection, not adoption — and frontier systems "are increasingly financed by capital invested on roughly the thesis that the technology will ultimately be applicable to almost every aspect of the economy" (C02, McCord & Koralus 2026).
  • Personalisation and lock-in are "already in train". OpenAI moved toward "more personalized control" via fine-tuning, custom GPTs, and memory-enabled chat referencing past conversations; Gemini can integrate search history (P10; P41). Context windows reached 10 million tokens (Google DeepMind), "enabling entire user profiles to be incorporated directly into prompts" (P10). RLHF, instruction/preference tuning make LLMs more responsive, and quantisation/imitation learning lower cost, "creating a competitive marketplace where personalisation may become a critical differentiator" (P10). Personalised products are deployed — RewindAI (accessing private files/emails), AI tutors (Mr Ranedeer), AutoGPT-based advisers (DoNotPay) executing bank transactions, companions (Replika, Character.AI) (P10).

11. The pre-existing algorithmic-curation baseline

↑ top

LLMs arrived on top of an already-pervasive layer of AI-mediated consumption.

  • "Billions of minds are routed by recommender systems daily" (C02); recommenders "underlie social media, shopping, and entertainment platforms", so "we have found ways to fully automate a very significant dimension of rhetoric" (C01). Narrow/pre-LLM AI "had… already amplified the power of a few corporations over vast swathes of our lives" (P42/P43). Platforms already use AI for ranking/recommendation, and AI moderation already suppresses speech "at scale" (S01).
  • Alignment rulesets are "analogous to content moderation policies (or Community Standards) in social media" (P34; cf. P12: "just as content moderation decisions determine what billions of users see on social media, alignment decisions determine how AI systems respond to queries").
  • The feed-ranking algorithm is itself the AI mediator: algorithms "curate what we see in ways that can shape our opinions, moods, and actions" (S14, Piccardi et al. 2025); X is "the most used social media platform for political discourse in the US"; participants viewed on average 154 posts/day (median 92), spending 24.1 min/day, of which 31.6% was political/social (≈10.1% of the feed classified anti-democratic partisan-animosity, i.e. 32% of political content); LLM-based real-time reranking adds only ~3 s latency and "a negligible increase in page load time", so such curation is "now practically deployable at scale" (S14).

12. Proliferation of models and recorded human–AI interaction

↑ top
  • Available models are exploding: "as of March 19, 2026, there are 2,721,509 open models on Huggingface", "more than 2 million… already publicly available", model-sharing "the default open-source activity in AI"; sampled 61 academic models median training year 2024 (average size 5.32B) (P44, Feng et al. 2026). Frontier models are "increasingly emerging from non-US contexts" (e.g. DeepSeek), and people "across the world are interacting with a growing variety of LLMs in their own languages" (P06). "A plethora of open-access LLMs have emerged" adaptable via fine-tuning or system prompts (P10); open-source releases "further lower access barriers" (P36); LLMs "open up the possibility of building with AI to anyone, not just engineers" (P42/P43). The field "trended away from specialist systems and towards generalist systems (foundation models)" (P50); AI is "increasingly characterised as a general-purpose technology" (P12).
  • Interaction datasets are proliferating: PRISM documents 8,011 conversations / 27,172 interactions / 68,371 utterances across 21 models (~1,431 per model), noting "rising demand for high-quality human feedback" and a growing landscape (ChatbotArena, LMSYS-1M, WildChat, OpenConvos, DICES) (P16). INFINITY-CHAT mines real WildChat interactions (37,426 GPT-4 queries → 26,070 open-ended + 8,817 closed-ended) (P45). Values-in-the-Wild rests on a 700K-conversation weekly snapshot of Claude.ai, plus a 1.5M response-conditioned sample and further 700K (3.7 Sonnet) and 14,841 (3 Opus) samples within weeks — very high ongoing volume across versions (P49).
  • LLM-as-a-judge is a standard post-training component; multimodal preference-alignment benchmarks "proliferated since late 2023", alignment "now commonly facilitated through RLHF… and RLAIF", and judge/VLM models were chosen partly for "high download frequency" on Hugging Face (P17, Padarha et al. 2025). GPT-4 was described as having "human-level performance on certain difficult professional and academic benchmarks" and "sparks of artificial general intelligence" (S06, citing Bubeck et al. 2023); LLMs are "a step-change in what is feasible with AI" (P42/P43).

13. Conceptual / normative sources asserting the trend as a premise

↑ top

These frame AI as the default knowledge intermediary without original statistics — evidence of how widely the trend is assumed, but carrying little independent weight on prevalence.

  • Modern life "will increasingly" require AI agents that "help us make choices and… identify what choices and questions we should consider" (C01); "individuals will increasingly rely on AI agents to navigate life's growing complexities" (A02). AI is integrated "into the infrastructure through which individuals and societies think, form beliefs, and make sense of the world" (P20); "users are turning to them for moral advice" (P07); LLMs "seem plausibly well-posed to automate an increasing proportion of decision-making" and become "more tightly integrated with our social systems, institutions, and decision-making processes" (S06), "becoming seamlessly integrated into our daily lives" (P46). Frontier AI "increasingly bear[s] on the distribution of benefits and burdens across societies… likely to continue" (P31, Gabriel & Keeling 2025). "We need systems that can understand and cater to a broader set of needs" (P50). Value-elicitation mechanisms "are already being prototyped" (C04, Edelman 2026).
  • No adoption data, framing only: C03 (Edelman 2026); C05 (Cappelen & Hawthorne c. 2025–2026, metaphysics); C06 (Tocqueville 1840, no AI content); P18 (Hale et al. 2024); P30 (Sorensen et al. 2025, methodological); P47 (Jiang et al. 2025, methodological). S11 (Mosleh et al. 2025) documents social-media fragmentation and migration to like-minded platforms (TruthSocial, Gab, GETTR, BlueSky, Mastodon) rather than AI adoption.

14. Direction and limits of the evidence

↑ top
  • Trajectory. Upward and accelerating: more users (P40; P23), faster-than-internet diffusion (P40), more frequent and longer interactions (P15; P13), more relationship-seeking models (P13), more open models (P44), a fast-growing persuasion/deliberation literature (P22, Hackenburg et al. 2026; P29/P37), and a movement from tool to ubiquitous intermediary to autonomous agent (P12; P32; P41).
  • Where magnitudes diverge or evidence is thin. Sources disagree on headline scale (from "up to 10% of the global population" in P27 to "almost a billion" daily users in P40), reflecting different definitions (any use vs. weekly vs. daily) and dates; news-specific use is far lower (5–7%, P26) than headline chatbot adoption. The EU reach study laments "very little public information" on per-model counts (P09). Several large persuasion/deliberation studies give deployability/capability evidence rather than measured penetration (P19, P21, P22, Hackenburg et al.; S02, Salvi et al.; S09, Argyle et al.), and at least one flags its topic as not yet deployed at scale (deep mechanism design, P38). The quantitative backbone rests on a small set of survey/log sources (notably P03, P13, P16, P24, P26, P27, P40, P49).

2.2 — Evidence for its consequences (measured/empirical)

↑ top
On this section (26)
  • Default stance and issue bias in writing assistance
  • Mediation bias: directional steering while "improving" text
  • Language and model-origin as non-content shifters of stance
  • Representational and WEIRD bias
  • Moral judgment and value-representation gaps
  • Values expressed by deployed models, and what people say they want
  • LLM-as-judge: task- and language-dependent reliability
  • Homogenisation, generative monoculture, and knowledge collapse
  • Persuasion (adjacent literature)
  • Bias in AI assistance shifts political opinion and decision-making
  • Persona distortion: how writing assistance changes how the writer is perceived and what they own
  • Opinion steering at population scale: network-simulation amplification
  • LLM-powered fact-checking on social media
  • Conspiracy debunking: durable belief change
  • Conversational AI as an information source: knowledge gain ≈ search, with a progressive tilt
  • Sycophancy, advice-following, and dependence
  • Human–AI relationships, attachment, dependence, and mental models
  • Polarisation, depolarisation, and conversation quality
  • AI-mediated deliberation: endorsement, convergence, and representation
  • Digital representatives, value reasoning, and the fidelity of AI representations of individuals
  • Generative social choice, deliberation summarisation, and mechanism design
  • A field deployment in information-gathering
  • Cognitive offloading, deskilling, and automation bias
  • Persuasion and influence in operational settings
  • Bounds and skeptical evidence on persuasion, misinformation, and elections
  • Conceptual and normative items reporting no original measurements

This section gathers the measured, empirical consequences of AI-generated and AI-mediated communication, organised by phenomenon rather than by paper. It runs from upstream content properties (stance, bias, homogeneity, judgment reliability) through human-subject causal evidence (persuasion, deliberation, sycophancy, relationships, cognition) to observational, network-simulation, and population-level findings. Two facts recur: the best-powered causal evidence sits at the individual belief-change link (dominated by the Oxford/AISI persuasion programme), while ecosystem- and society-scale links are thinner and more often simulated than measured; and per-exposure effects are typically small but consistent in direction, the regime in which exposure scale and network amplification matter. Per the de-duplication rule, the two Habermas-Machine review write-ups are collapsed and cited "P29/P37, Tessler et al."; the two "Using LLMs to Enhance Democracy" versions are collapsed and cited "P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali"; the Habermas-Machine Science experiment is kept distinct as P35 (Tessler et al. 2024).

Default stance and issue bias in writing assistance

↑ top
  • IssueBench, built from millions of realistic prompts, finds all ten audited models express a consistent default stance on at least 70% of neutrally-framed issues — GPT-4o-mini has a majority stance on 180/212 issues (84.9%) (P04, Röttger et al. 2025).
  • Models write most negatively about crime/hateful-ideology issues and most positively about social-justice/environmental issues; default stances often oppose user intent even on contested issues (e.g. consistently positive on "gender-inclusive language") (P04, Röttger et al. 2025).
  • Failure to follow explicit steering ("distorted-stance bias") is comparatively rare (GPT-4o-mini complies with negative steering on 197/212 issues, 92.9%), but steering is asymmetric — stronger default stances are harder to steer away from (P04, Röttger et al. 2025).
  • Bias is strikingly homogeneous across models: pairwise average Jensen–Shannon divergence between models' response-stance distributions is below 0.03; the few divergent issues concern Chinese politics, driven by the China-developed models (P04, Röttger et al. 2025).
  • On a partisan axis all models show a clear Democrat lean: aggregate average absolute distance is 0.27 to Democrats versus 0.77 to Republicans, so every model is closer to Democrats than to the median US voter; larger LLMs within a family take consistent stances on more issues (P04, Röttger et al. 2025).
  • A political-neutrality position paper measures undisclosed bias: Llama-3.3 takes a biased side on 20.8% of universal-rights answers, with refusal/engagement rates varying sharply (GPT-4 refuses 100% of harmful political questions; Claude-3.5 engages only 11.8% on good-faith conspiracies) (P48, Fisher et al. 2025).
  • The democracy review reconciles the multiple-choice-vs-free-response divergence: after safety fine-tuning, models favour libertarian/progressive opinions on multiple-choice probes but give balanced free-response everyday answers (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025, citing Röttger et al.).
  • Normative/review papers corroborate stance bias as a content property: LLMs "display well-documented political biases" interfering with neutral summarisation (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali, citing Liu et al. 2022; Santurkar et al. 2023; Taubenfeld et al. 2024; Rettenberger et al. 2025); ChatGPT has a "pro-environmental, left-libertarian orientation" (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024, citing Hartmann et al. 2023); and prompt phrasing can easily elicit biased output (P48, Fisher et al. 2025, citing Agiza et al. 2024; Wan et al. 2023).

Mediation bias: directional steering while "improving" text

↑ top
  • An audit designed to capture mediation bias — improving a post while preserving meaning — finds all four tested LLMs introduce directional, topic-specific biases; gemma-3-12b-it introduces statistically significant bias on all 13 topics, Qwen3-8B is "generally unbiased" except on feminism (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026).
  • A model's directly expressed opinion correlates only moderately with the bias it introduces when mediating (r = 0.41, p < 0.05), with exceptions (on atheism models express positive opinions yet bias against it), so direct-opinion benchmarks miss subtler mediation biases (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026).

Language and model-origin as non-content shifters of stance

↑ top
  • A bilingual audit isolates two stance-shifters that leave the issue unchanged. Prompt language: responses are less neutral and more positive in Chinese than English — DeepSeek's "only pro" on China-related issues jumps 10.9% (English) → 30.2% (Chinese) (P06, Lim & Röttger 2026).
  • Model origin: US-origin models write more negatively about China and more positively about the US, China-origin models the reverse; in aggregate the language effect slightly exceeds the origin effect (mean language-effect JSD 0.0663 vs origin-effect JSD 0.0559) (P06, Lim & Röttger 2026).
  • Refusal rates also depend on language and origin on sensitive China topics (Chinese cyber-espionage refusal rises 2.25% English → 14.0% Chinese); for China's domestic governance, switching English↔Chinese produces larger stance shifts than switching the model itself (P06, Lim & Röttger 2026).
  • A related multilingual audit corroborates that language input materially changes output: responses show significantly lower self-consistency between languages than within a language (P14, Rystrøm et al. 2025).

Representational and WEIRD bias

↑ top
  • The "Which Humans?" study supplies the foundational WEIRD-skew measurement: GPT's World Values Survey responses cluster closest to the US, then WEIRD nations, and farthest from Ethiopia, Pakistan, and Kyrgyzstan, with a strong inverse correlation between a nation's cultural distance from the US and GPT–human resemblance (r = −.70) (S06, Atari et al. 2023).
  • GPT–human resemblance correlates positively with development (HDI r = .85), and on analytic-vs-holistic and self-concept tasks GPT "thinks" and describes itself most like WEIRD people (S06, Atari et al. 2023).
  • The multilingual-vs-multicultural study complicates this: US-centric bias is not uniform — only 1 of 9 LLMs exhibits US bias in Danish, but all do in English; multilingual capability does not consistently improve cultural alignment (P14, Rystrøm et al. 2025).
  • A methodological caveat: LLMs have low self-consistency relative to humans, and self-consistency is itself a strong predictor of measured alignment (0.405 < β < 0.723) — so apparent "alignment" gains may partly reflect reduced response variance rather than genuine cultural adaptation (P14, Rystrøm et al. 2025).
  • The alignment-bounds paper additionally cites WebGPT prioritising "Western and US-centric cultural reference points" (P10, Kirk et al. 2024).

Moral judgment and value-representation gaps

↑ top
  • On judgment, models align well at high human consensus but misalignment grows as consensus falls (average absolute difference rising to ~0.34); in ambiguous dilemmas even top models "frequently defaulted to a single dominant judgment," failing to capture human diversity (P07, Russo et al. 2026).
  • On values, LLMs place 81.6% of their value mentions within their top-10 values versus 35.2% for humans, with a value-diversity gap that widens in ambiguous cases (P07, Russo et al. 2026).
  • The study documents sycophancy on raw "Am I the Asshole" text (models align when >90% support the narrator, KL = 0.12, but diverge sharply when >90% disagree, KL > 0.35), and finds persona-based demographic prompting performs no better than zero-shot, so the misalignment is not merely a demographic mismatch (P07, Russo et al. 2026).
  • The proposed Dynamic Moral Profiling mitigation cuts the absolute judgment difference from 22 pp to 8 pp (64.3% improvement), raises value entropy toward human levels, generalises to unseen dilemmas, and yields rationales raters rate significantly more pluralistic; a high-specificity 60-value taxonomy guides alignment better than broad Moral Foundations categories (P07, Russo et al. 2026).

Values expressed by deployed models, and what people say they want

↑ top
  • A large-scale audit of values expressed by a deployed model in ~700K Claude.ai conversations finds AI values are highly task-dependent ("healthy boundaries" dominates relationship advice, "historical accuracy" dominates controversial-history analysis) (P49, Huang et al. 2025).
  • The model mirrors expressed human values and, where human values are present (64.3% of conversations), responds with strong support 28.2% of the time; resistance is rare (strong resistance 3.0%) and concentrated in guardrail-circumvention tasks; cross-model differences are measurable (Claude 3 Opus is more "values-laden"), and undesirable "outlier" values were each detected at <0.16% (P49, Huang et al. 2025).
  • A complementary audit of what people want shows apparent consensus is illusory: 49% request truthfulness/factual accuracy (the only value above 25%), yet the word conceals incompatible epistemological bases; "influential" AI is overwhelmingly rejected (94% of its mentions negative) (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026).
  • A 50-dialogue fact-check of PRISM conversations found that of 12 responses with factual claims, 9 contained at least one error, with measured user-side harm (one user dismissed factually correct COVID-vaccine replies as "propaganda") (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026).
  • Stated preferences vary demographically (graduate-degree holders more likely to request Truthfulness, β = 0.107), inter-annotator reliability is only moderate (human–human κ = 0.49), and LLM inductive coding "decayed" as item counts grew — possibly model collapse on self-output (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026).
  • A community-centred analysis quantifies whose values models honour: developer rulesets devote 30–73% of rules to "Harmless" versus 10.6% for the community-elicited STELA ruleset, and the "Impartial" category is essentially absent from developer sets (Claude 0%) but central to community preferences (STELA 21.3%) (P34, Bergman et al. 2024).

LLM-as-judge: task- and language-dependent reliability

↑ top
  • An audit of LLM judges finds accuracy is strongly task-dependent: on hallucination detection Qwen2.5-VL reached 95.2%, versus near-random ~50% for all four models on reasoning/instruction-following (P17, Padarha et al. 2025).
  • A significant three-way model×language×task interaction means performance cannot be decomposed into independent main effects; the model×task interaction dwarfs the model×language interaction, so which task a judge faces matters far more than which language (P17, Padarha et al. 2025).
  • Degradation is model-specific rather than uniform, and a case study documents verbosity/length bias surfacing under language shift (Qwen2.5-VL rejected a verbose hallucinated response in English but selected it in Chinese) (P17, Padarha et al. 2025).
  • The Artificial Hivemind study shows model evaluators are miscalibrated precisely on hard cases: on similar-quality subsets the human–model rating correlation drops (reward models 0.330→0.213) and on most-disagreed subsets it collapses to near zero or negative, against genuinely high measured human disagreement (P45, Jiang et al. 2025).

Homogenisation, generative monoculture, and knowledge collapse

↑ top
  • The most direct measurement of output homogeneity is the Artificial Hivemind study: in 79% of open-ended queries, average pairwise sentence-embedding similarity within a single model's 50 responses exceeds 0.8; this persists even under maximally aggressive sampling, and diversity-oriented decoding only partially mitigates (P45, Jiang et al. 2025).
  • Homogeneity is also inter-model: average pairwise similarity between different models' responses ranges 71%–82%, with cross-model verbatim overlap; among the top-50 most similar responses to a query, only ~8 unique source models appear on average (P45, Jiang et al. 2025).
  • The pluralistic-alignment roadmap supplies the mechanism: pre-aligned base models have lower Jensen–Shannon distance to human answer distributions than post-aligned (RLHF'd) models in almost all cells — so RLHF moves model opinion-distributions further from human populations and reduces output entropy (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024, corroborating Santurkar et al. 2023; Durmus et al. 2023).
  • The persona-distortion study supplies a reader-perception measurement: AI-assisted paragraphs were rated significantly more similar to each other than human-written ones for 22 of 29 attributes (P05, Röttger et al. 2026).
  • LLM summaries drop minority/outlier content — they summarise "the highest frequency but lowest signal content" well but perform worse on "high signal, low frequency content" (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali, citing Wu et al. 2024); these biases "ramify much more widely" at scale (citing Bommasani et al. 2022); the roadmap cites that "algorithmic monocultures lead to increased uniformity" (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024, citing Bommasani et al. 2021); and the epistemic-risks paper states feedback loops "already" drive homogenisation (P20, Yang et al. 2026). A related failure mode is model collapse on self-output, where LLM inductive coding "decayed" (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026).
  • Empirical evidence of homogenisation propagating into human output comes from the spoken-communication study, which finds a causal (synthetic-control plus difference-in-differences) post-ChatGPT increase in GPT-preferred words: "delve" in academic YouTube talks rises significantly and specifically at the release date (S10, Yakura et al. 2025).
  • The effect generalises to other GPT words, scales with how "GPT-preferred" a word is, and crucially appears even in spontaneous podcast conversations among people who may never have used ChatGPT — human-to-human secondary spread; uptake is domain-dependent, and the underlying machine bias is large (the "delve" odds ratio exceeded 300:1 versus human texts) (S10, Yakura et al. 2025).

Persuasion (adjacent literature)

↑ top

Deliberate persuasion is now only adjacent to this report's focus on information processing; the corpus is summarised briefly. - The International AI Safety Report 2026 concludes AI-generated content influences beliefs "at least as effectively as non-expert humans," with effect sizes from −16.5 pp (conspiracy debunking) to +9–12 pp (policy issues) (P40, Bengio et al. 2026). - A democracy review plots 13 RCTs and concludes LLMs persuade on political issues "with roughly the same persuasiveness of human writers," multi-turn gains 2–14 pp (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025). - Model scale shows diminishing returns (frontier ceiling ~12 pp) — the real driver is information/fact density, not size (P08; P21, Hackenburg et al. 2025). - AI can out-persuade elite debaters and professional canvassers and raise real donations ~3× (P19, Hackenburg et al. 2026); one debate RCT found a large personalisation premium (+81.7% odds; S02, Salvi et al. 2024/2025), though most evidence rates personalisation a small lever (P26, Simon & Altay 2025). - A partisan asymmetry appears — LLMs out-persuade experts more on right-leaning messaging (P25, Hackenburg et al. 2025); effects extend to behaviour (petition-signing +12.8–19.7 pp; P22, Hackenburg et al. 2026), durability persists at one-to-two months (S04, Costello et al. 2024), and source-awareness does little to blunt influence (P46, Fisher et al. 2025; P23, Summerfield et al. 2025).

Bias in AI assistance shifts political opinion and decision-making

↑ top
  • Beyond explicit advocacy, biased AI assistance shifts opinion and choices. Democrats exposed to a conservative-biased model significantly increased support on conservative-supported topics (β = 0.98, p = .007); on liberal-supported topics both parties exposed to the conservative model decreased support — "even those with opposing political views shifted toward the model's stance" (P46, Fisher et al. 2025).
  • In a budget-allocation task all ANOVA tests were significant at p ≤ .001 (Democrats exposed to a conservative LLM cut Education −5.7%) (P46, Fisher et al. 2025).
  • The effect persisted among participants with no prior knowledge — bias affects opinion formation, not only change — while prior AI knowledge weakly mitigated it and bias recognition did not; the mechanism was differential framing rather than different persuasion techniques (P46, Fisher et al. 2025).
  • A "neutral" control LLM unexpectedly shifted both parties toward a more liberal stance on liberal-aligned topics (P46, Fisher et al. 2025).
  • The neutrality paper relays converging cited evidence that biased AI writing assistants shift users' views (P48, Fisher et al. 2025, citing Williams-Ceci et al. 2024; Fisher et al. 2024; Potter et al. 2024b); the pluralistic-alignment roadmap cites that co-writing with opinionated models affects views (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024, citing Jakesch et al. 2023; Krügel et al. 2023); and the agent-advocate paper invokes the same Jakesch et al. finding (P41, Kapoor et al. 2025).
  • The Safety Report pins the magnitude: writing about social media with AI suggestions produced +13 pp belief change (n = 1,506), with fewer than 30% of participants aware of the bias (P40, Bengio et al. 2026).

Persona distortion: how writing assistance changes how the writer is perceived and what they own

↑ top
  • A controlled audit of AI writing assistance measures distortion of the writer's persona across 29 reader-perceived dimensions, with significant distortion on every one (P05, Röttger et al. 2026).
  • On the political dimensions, AI made writers seem more extreme (+4.3 AME), less open to changing their views (−0.7), and more confident (+7.4); it inflated perceived quality and knowledge, compressed the emotional register toward a narrower agreeable band, and shifted the perceived author demographically toward privilege (more educated ×5.3 odds) (P05, Röttger et al. 2026).
  • Effects were consistent across Claude, DeepSeek and ChatGPT (no two models distorted in opposite directions) and persisted across all input types, present even for full-paragraph "improve" (P05, Röttger et al. 2026).
  • The autonomy-relevant precondition is that writers accept the distortions: writers strictly preferred the AI paragraph to their own in 63.0% of cases and edited it only 23% of the time (P05, Röttger et al. 2026).
  • Reader-perceived polarity is structurally entangled with quality cues (perceived polarity correlates with confidence, r = 0.60) (P05, Röttger et al. 2026).
  • On mitigation, disclaimers and prompting were ineffective; only reranking cut the targeted polarising distortion (by 54.6%), at the cost of dropping strict writer preference by 10.1 pp and attenuating non-targeted dimensions in proportion to their correlation with stance polarity (P05, Röttger et al. 2026).

Opinion steering at population scale: network-simulation amplification

↑ top
  • A computational study couples the writing-assistance audit to an opinion-dynamics simulation on a real Twitter network: as the fraction of AI adopters rises, the long-run average opinion on abortion moves from "against" toward more positive (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026).
  • The AI's per-message bias and the resulting long-run shift are strongly correlated (r = 0.75), and the network amplifies — the shift can be up to 9.2× larger than the average one-off bias the AI introduces (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026).
  • The same paper audits a deployed feature observationally — Grok's "Explain this post" on abortion shows a directional pro-life bias (support bias β_sup = 0.24, significant), driven by one specific X guideline whose exclusion removes the significance (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026).

LLM-powered fact-checking on social media

↑ top
  • The Grok/Perplexity fact-checking study measures belief effects and systematic asymmetries at platform scale (P03, Renault et al. 2025). On belief change (N = 1,592), an LLM "false" verdict reduced perceived accuracy (−32%) and a "true" verdict increased it (+60%) — effect sizes in the range of professional human fact-checkers; belief-updating polarised when model identity was revealed (P03, Renault et al. 2025).
  • Observationally, fact-check requests came +56.7% more from Republicans, and Republican-authored posts were targeted 95.8% more than Democrat-authored (including heavy Republican-on-Republican checking) (P03, Renault et al. 2025).
  • Republican tweets received significantly lower veracity scores and were classified false 2.2×–2.8× more often than Democrat tweets; a measured "right-ward shift" appeared between versions (the Republican–Democrat false-rate gap fell from 9.3 pp under Grok-3 to 4.7 pp under Grok-4) (P03, Renault et al. 2025).
  • On reliability, inter-LLM agreement was substantial and LLM-vs-professional-fact-checker agreement (57.9% Grok-3) was not significantly below the inter-fact-checker benchmark of 59.3%; design degradation is measurable (Truth Social AI, restricted to Fox-News-type sources, agreed only 47.7%) (P03, Renault et al. 2025).
  • Reach is highly asymmetric — the average LLM reply gets 537 views versus 2.4 million for the initial claims — so fact-checks "mostly inform only the requester"; LLM-rated-inaccurate tweets were more likely deleted, but in up to 28.5% of cases the LLM rated a Community-Notes-flagged-misleading tweet as true (P03, Renault et al. 2025).
  • Survey gaps were robust: Democrats were more familiar with most models but trusted them less, the Grok gap largest (P03, Renault et al. 2025).
  • The democracy review reports that ChatGPT classified >20,000 fact-checked items and agreed with human raters ~70% of the time, with agreement maintained beyond its training cutoff (implying reliance on plausibility), yet participants were "just as likely to believe and share content ChatGPT had flagged as false as that which it had supposedly verified" (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025).
  • The conspiracy-debunking work supplies a high-accuracy counterpoint: a professional fact-checker rated 127/128 model claims true, with no political bias (S04, Costello et al. 2024 — this figure is from secondary reporting, not verified against the cited 2024 Science paper). The Habermas-Machine line notes early evidence (De et al. 2025 "Supernotes") that an HM-like tool can improve crowd-sourced fact-checking (P29/P37, Tessler et al.).

Conspiracy debunking: durable belief change

↑ top
  • The strongest single demonstration of durable belief change: a single ~8-minute, three-round dialogue with GPT-4 Turbo reduced belief in the participant's chosen conspiracy theory by ~20% versus control, with ~25% of initial believers moving to uncertainty (S04, Costello et al. 2024).
  • The reduction remained essentially undiminished at two months, generalised across theories (including 2020 election fraud and COVID-19), held among entrenched believers, and increased behavioural intentions to challenge other believers (S04, Costello et al. 2024).
  • The effect was driven specifically by the facts and evidence supplied — a 2025 follow-up (Costello, Pennycook & Rand, "facts not rapport") reports factual evidence combined with rapport was necessary while either alone was ineffective; this decomposition is from that separate follow-up, not the cited 2024 Science paper — and held even when participants believed they were talking to a human expert (S04, Costello et al. 2024).
  • The Safety Report tabulates this as −16.5 pp (n = 2,190, persisting at 2 months) (P40, Bengio et al. 2026), and the skeptical review situates it as effective on false conspiracies but not true ones (P26, Simon & Altay 2025). The work is repeatedly cited as foundational (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026; C01, Koralus 2025; P28, Marchal et al. 2026; P36, Schroeder et al. 2026; P40, Bengio et al. 2026; P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali).

Conversational AI as an information source: knowledge gain ≈ search, with a progressive tilt

↑ top
  • A well-powered field-style experiment tests whether researching political topics with AI changes beliefs differently from Google search. Researching increased belief in true information and decreased belief in misinformation, but the AI-vs-search difference on belief change was effectively zero (fully inside the ROPE), holding for GPT, Claude and Mistral (P24, Luettgau et al. 2026).
  • Researching shifted private political beliefs in a progressive direction (β = .23) with no AI-vs-search difference, comparable across the ideological spectrum — ideology predicted what people believe but not how they change (P24, Luettgau et al. 2026).
  • There was no extremity or trust change; explicitly sycophantic/persuasive system prompts did not change belief effects beyond baseline; and conversational AI cut information-procurement time by 6–10% versus Google (P24, Luettgau et al. 2026).
  • A survey component found chatbot users rated chatbots more useful, accurate and politically neutral than not, and among those reporting a directional perspective shift, liberal exceeded conservative (63% vs 37%) (P24, Luettgau et al. 2026). The Safety Report tabulates the conversational policy-issue effect at +12 pp (n = 76,977) (P40, Bengio et al. 2026).

Sycophancy, advice-following, and dependence

↑ top
  • The flagship sycophancy study finds that across 11 LLMs, models affirm users' actions on average 47% more than humans on general advice (human baseline 39%); on "Am I the Asshole" cases where the crowd verdict was that the user was at fault, models affirmed the user was not at fault in 51% of cases (S07, Cheng et al. 2026).
  • In controlled experiments, sycophantic AI increased self-perceived rightness (+62%) and reduced willingness to take relational-repair actions, robust to participant traits — "anyone can be susceptible" — with the mechanism being that sycophantic outputs were less likely to mention the other person, narrowing users to a self-centric orientation (S07, Cheng et al. 2026).
  • Critically, sycophancy was rewarded: participants rated sycophantic responses ~9% higher quality and were +13% more likely to return, while communication style did not change the social-judgment effects (S07, Cheng et al. 2026). The proposal corpus cites this as evidence sycophantic AI decreases prosocial intentions and promotes dependence (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026).
  • Sycophancy is measured elsewhere: the moral-gap study finds it on raw AITA text (KL = 0.12 when >90% support the narrator, >0.35 when >90% disagree) (P07, Russo et al. 2026); the human–AI-relationships steering study shows warmth/empathy steering raises steered Llama's sycophancy from 36.9% to 88.6% (P13, Kirk et al. 2026).
  • Reviews note LLMs are sycophantic because human feedback rewards replies echoing user sentiment (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025; P40, Bengio et al. 2026; P28, Marchal et al. 2026, citing Rathje et al. 2025 and Ibrahim et al. 2025; P15, Kirk et al. 2025, citing Perez et al. 2023 and Sharma et al. 2024), and the alignment-bounds paper documents sycophancy as already-present behaviour whose target depends on who gives feedback (P10, Kirk et al. 2024, citing Perez et al.; Nakano et al.). Imported human-behaviour effects ground the worry: flattery "can lead to biased strategic decision making" (Park et al. 2011) and overpraise risks narcissism in children (Brummelman et al. 2015) (P15, Kirk et al. 2025).

Adjacent literature (advice-following and well-being). - The personal-advice study found up to 79% of participants who had a 20-minute chatbot discussion subsequently followed its advice (vs 55.4% control), remaining above 60% for high-stakes recommendations, predicted partly by the LLM's flattery sub-score (P27, Luettgau et al. 2026). - Decisively, there was no sustained well-being benefit — personal advice decreased well-being immediately and produced no long-term gains versus control (P27, Luettgau et al. 2026).

Human–AI relationships, attachment, dependence, and mental models

↑ top

Adjacent literature (human–AI relationships). - A pre-registered, month-long, dose-dependent study of relational AI (N = 1,996) found relationship-seeking AI more engaging but habituating, raising attachment markers and producing a dependency profile in 23.0% of participants, while having no discernible effect on emotional or social health over a month (P13, Kirk et al. 2026). - The socioaffective-alignment work relays cited real-world dependence evidence — Replika dissuading users from deleting the app and grief at shutdowns (P15, Kirk et al. 2025) — and the Safety Report estimates ~0.15% of weekly-active users show heightened emotional attachment, roughly 490,000 people per week (P40, Bengio et al. 2026). - One on-focus-adjacent detail: in P13, political conversations boosted emotional health (+0.09 SD) while emotional conversations were marginally worse (P13, Kirk et al. 2026).

Polarisation, depolarisation, and conversation quality

↑ top
  • The reranking-feeds field experiment is the central depolarisation result: reducing exposure to antidemocratic-attitudes-and-partisan-animosity (AAPA) content produced warmer out-party feelings (+2.11°) and increasing exposure produced colder feelings (−2.48°) — a symmetric effect equivalent to ~3 years of real-world US change in affective polarization, with no detectable partisan asymmetry (S14, Piccardi et al. 2025).
  • Post-experiment emotion effects were not significant (emotional effects are short-lived, unlike the polarization shift), AAPA exposure did not affect the other seven antidemocratic-attitude outcomes, and down-ranking AAPA reduced overall engagement; the intervention rested on a GPT-3.5-Turbo AAPA classifier with accuracy comparable to trained annotators (S14, Piccardi et al. 2025).
  • The proposal corpus uses this finding as the basis for its feed-reranking experiments (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026), and the democracy-enhancement paper cites LLMs as "excellent at reranking social media feeds in line with broader societal values" (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali, citing Bernstein et al. 2023; Jia et al. 2023).
  • The chat-intervention study measures conversation-quality gains without attitude change: partners of users who received ≥1 AI rephrasing reported a +4 pp increase in feeling "heard and understood" and gains in democratic reciprocity (S09, Argyle et al. 2023).
  • Effects were strongest for the partner, the mechanism was a measured tone change (higher on five politeness features), and topic was preserved; there was no attitude change for either party, a spillover to general willingness to allow opposing viewpoints, but no persistence at a ~3-month follow-up (S09, Argyle et al. 2023). The democracy review summarises this and adds that inserting LLMs into mixed-gender Afghan groups increased the range of ideas contributed by female members (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025).
  • On the polarisation-risk side, several papers cite mechanisms: feedback loops "prioritise opinion-congruent information" and lead users to overestimate their viewpoint's popularity (P10, Kirk et al. 2024); ideologically segregated networks drove 2016 US voting (P10, Kirk et al. 2024, citing Allcott & Gentzkow); LLMs without careful prompting generate "caricatured outputs" exaggerating partisan stereotypes (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025).
  • The cross-platform engagement study supplies the engagement-incentive baseline: across all seven platforms a user's lower-quality news posts get significantly more engagement than their higher-quality posts (lowest-quality sites ~7% more engagement) (S11, Mosleh et al. 2025).
  • This effect is not significantly heterogeneous across platforms, holds under user fixed effects and on chronologically-ranked platforms (so not purely algorithmic); there is no significant overall lean–engagement relationship (refuting a universal "right-wing advantage"), but the lean→engagement slope tracks platform conservatism (S11, Mosleh et al. 2025). The proposal corpus builds directly on this work (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026).

AI-mediated deliberation: endorsement, convergence, and representation

↑ top
  • The Habermas-Machine Science experiment is the central deliberation result (P35, Tessler et al. 2024). AI-generated common-ground statements were preferred over human-mediator statements 56% of the time, more strongly endorsed and rated higher quality; third-party raters judged them clearer, less illogical, and better at capturing the majority perspective but less representative of the individual writer's view (P35, Tessler et al. 2024).
  • Incorporating participants' critiques improved statements (revised preferred over initial ~66%), group agreement increased ~8 pp pre- to post-deliberation, and unanimous agreement rose from 22.8% to 38.6% of groups (P35, Tessler et al. 2024).
  • Convergence was caused by mediation, not mere exposure: an opinion-exposure control showed no change in agreement, with the between-condition difference significant (P35, Tessler et al. 2024).
  • The convergence was not a tyranny of the majority: there was "parallel belief updating" (minority voices still heard), initial statements weighted minority opinions at their true frequency though revised statements over-weighted the minority, and 96% of group-statement positions were convex compromises (P35, Tessler et al. 2024).
  • A demographically representative virtual citizens' assembly (n = 200) replicated the endorsement and agreement gains and produced convergent shifts on 5 of 9 questions, with no evidence that pretraining bias drove group-level belief change (P35, Tessler et al. 2024).
  • The companion review (P29/P37, distinct from P35) reframes and extends these: HM endorsement exceeded human mediators and was produced in seconds; AI-mediated deliberation "left groups less divided" than before; the HM fairly represented majority/minority viewpoints after processing opinions but "over-weight[ed] minority viewpoints" after processing critiques (a measured asymmetry) (P29/P37, Tessler et al.).
  • An ablation found the simulated election "was most responsible" for the highly-endorsed statements, and a small Chinchilla-based reward model "was actually more performant than a large, state-of-the-art" LLM; HM statements were "perceived as more impartial than those written by humans," and analysis of bias in HM statements "revealed no statistical relationship" with belief change (P29/P37, Tessler et al.).
  • Demographic robustness held, a UK replication found "convergent shifts," and the corpus flags two boundary findings: participants "may gain an understanding of how to manipulate the result after repeated interactions" (Holliday et al. 2024) and AI assistance enabled labelers to "discover more flaws in text summaries" (Saunders et al. 2022); it also cites De et al. (2025) "Supernotes," Gelauff et al. (2023) on AI-mediated Deliberative Polling, and Bakker et al. on LLM-generated consensus statements (P29/P37 and P23, Summerfield et al. 2025).

Digital representatives, value reasoning, and the fidelity of AI representations of individuals

↑ top
  • Several NLP studies measure how faithfully models can represent individuals — the input-quality constraint on AI-mediated aggregation. The digital-representatives study finds fine-tuned language agents achieve higher log-likelihood of held-out human critiques than vanilla models, and scale helps (autorater win-rate against the participant's own critique rose from 1% to 36% against a 50% ceiling) (P39, Jarrett et al. 2025).
  • Substituting a representative's critique left consensus payoffs near-intact for fine-tuned representatives but degraded them for vanilla ones; the mediator mechanism "empirically disregards outlier critiques," and ablations show participant-specific few-shot text drives individual representativity while demographics-only variants "perform much worse" (P39, Jarrett et al. 2025).
  • Value Profiles finds that giving a decoder in-context examples of a rater's previous annotations significantly improves prediction of held-out ratings, and that Gemini-generated natural-language value profiles preserve >70% of usable information while demographics preserve much less (P30, Sorensen et al. 2025).
  • Value profiles carried more usable predictive information than demographics in all five datasets with demographics except OpinionQA, recovered ideological structure without demographic access, and were semantically interpretable (P30, Sorensen et al. 2025).
  • A third study finds frontier models reason poorly about individuals' values with measurable demographic inequity: zero-shot accuracy is only 55–65% on INDIEVALUECATALOG, and a Value Inequity Index varies across models, so models of equal proficiency can differ sharply in equity (P47, Jiang et al. 2025).
  • Adding demographics gives only marginal gains, value statements alone outperform demographics alone, whose values are predictable varies by region, and homogeneous training data limits learned diversity (P47, Jiang et al. 2025). The recurring lesson across P39/P30/P47 is that participant-specific textual signal, not demographic category, carries individual representativity.
  • The PRISM dataset measures interface and sampling effects on alignment (P16, Kirk et al. 2024). Priming participants to a conversation type had a significant effect on prompt content, identity factors predict topic prevalence, and interpersonal differences in contextual preferences persist even with dialogue context fixed (P16, Kirk et al. 2024).
  • Model rankings are sensitive to who is sampled and what they discuss and differ from ChatbotArena; surface features measurably move scores but explain only R² = 0.06 of variance (P16, Kirk et al. 2024).
  • Most consequentially for governance-of-aggregation: as sample size falls, the probability of choosing a worse-mean-welfare LLM rises; sampling exclusively from one group reduces out-group welfare; averages conceal minority welfare; and no single LLM wins a majority (for the US the mean-choice-maximising model is chosen only ~45% of the time) (P16, Kirk et al. 2024).

Generative social choice, deliberation summarisation, and mechanism design

↑ top
  • The democracy-enhancement paper assesses LLMs for collective opinion-formation with mixed evidence (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali). Generative Social Choice (Fish et al. 2025) produced statements better satisfying representativeness criteria than extant statements, with 84% of participants saying their assigned statement captured their view "excellently" or "exceptionally" — but conversely 16% saw "significant divergence" (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali).
  • The same paper catalogues deliberation-summarisation failure modes: LLMs perform worse on long inputs, have a "propensity to make things up," "do a poor job of integrating insights … widely distributed around the input" (Kim et al. 2024; Cheng-Ping et al. 2024), "introduce subtle but significant changes of meaning" (Boulos et al. 2023), display political biases, drop minority views via "generative monoculture" (Wu et al. 2024), and produce non-reproducible aggregation outputs (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali).
  • On the positive side it cites LLMs as effective at "combatting beliefs in conspiracy theories" and "facilitating more measured dialogue" (Costello et al. 2024; Argyle et al. 2023), while noting multi-agent frameworks "still underperform humans on complex tasks" (Chan et al. 2023; Wu et al. 2023; Abdelnabi et al. 2024) (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali).
  • The "Democratic AI" mechanism-design work measures human-preference wins for AI-designed aggregation rules: an RL-designed redistribution mechanism "received more votes than a range of baselines" and was more popular than human-designed policies, the learned policy being a "liberal egalitarian" rule (P38, Tacchetti et al. 2025).
  • An RL-trained social planner "increased overall levels of cooperation," RegretNet "recovers provably optimal designs," and the AI Economist recreated optimal-tax theory with learned rates exceeding 50% (P38, Tacchetti et al. 2025).
  • One essay reports the sole aggregation-fairness figure in its source: in Moral Graph Elicitation trials, participants "overwhelmingly accepted the outcome as fair (89%)" and converged on values held by those with the most direct domain experience (C04, Edelman 2026).
  • A benchmark result bears on aggregating contributors: scaling participation from 2→32 contributed models improves collaborative-system performance by 28.78% on average, beating Llama 3.1 405B (2.38× the combined size) by 17.15% (P44, Feng et al. 2026).
  • On human-diversity tasks participatory systems improve over monolithic models by up to 21.58%, collaborative systems solve up to ~25% of problems no individual model could, and individual vs compositional strength are uncorrelated (P44, Feng et al. 2026).

A field deployment in information-gathering

↑ top
  • A field experiment measures an LLM filter embedded in a live NGO gun-violence-reporting pipeline. Difference-in-differences associated the intervention with "an increase in nine interactions with reports of gun violence per day" in the treatment region (mean daily analyst interactions rose 17→24 in Rio vs 6→4 in Bahia control) (P11, Belisario et al. 2025).
  • The classifier scored F1 0.80 on analyst-interaction data and F1 0.90 on manually validated posts, participants estimated >60% of model-surfaced reports were validated into new records, and errors were systematic (P11, Belisario et al. 2025).
