Cognitive offloading, deskilling, and automation bias

↑ top
  • Several measured effects bear on autonomy through competence loss when people delegate political judgement to LLMs rather than reasoning about contested questions themselves. In a 666-participant study, frequent AI-tool use correlated negatively with critical-thinking ability, mediated by increased cognitive offloading — the displacement of independent evaluation onto the model; younger participants showed higher AI dependence and lower critical-thinking scores, while higher education was protective (S08, Gerlich 2025; relayed at P40, Bengio et al. 2026).
  • Where the offloaded task is appraising political information, this delegation hands the model effective authority over which considerations the user ever weighs.
  • The Safety Report adds measured deskilling and automation bias: clinicians' unaided tumour-detection ability dropped ~6% three months after introducing AI support, and in a randomised experiment (n = 2,784) participants were less likely to correct erroneous AI suggestions when correction required extra effort or when they held more favourable attitudes toward AI (P40, Bengio et al. 2026).
  • It also tabulates labour/information-production effects: four months after ChatGPT's release, writing jobs on one platform declined ~2% and writers' earnings fell ~5.2%, while demand for machine-learning programming rose ~24% (P40, Bengio et al. 2026).

Persuasion and influence in operational settings

↑ top
  • One on-focus operational finding recurs: leading models gave inaccurate answers to "over half" of practical US electoral-participation queries (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025), and the skeptical review echoes that GenAI election answers are "often inaccurate, misleading, and even downright harmful" (Angwin et al. 2024) (P26, Simon & Altay 2025).
  • Adjacent (persuasion/influence operations): the same reviews report LLM-assisted spear-phishing at ~50% click-through (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025) and that generative tools "can expand propaganda output without sacrificing credibility" (P36, Schroeder et al. 2026).

Bounds and skeptical evidence on persuasion, misinformation, and elections

↑ top
  • A set of papers pushes back on alarmism. The "Don't Panic (Yet)" review assembles boundary evidence: political-advertising persuasion is near-zero (Kalla & Broockman 2017); removing political ads from Facebook/Instagram before the 2020 US election produced null effects (Allcott et al. 2025); a large persuadable-voter experiment found only small differential-turnout effects (Aggarwal et al. 2023); and brand-advertising elasticities are small (Sethuraman et al. 2018) (P26, Simon & Altay 2025).
  • Fact-checking reliably reduces misperceptions but has negligible effect on attitudes/voting (Nyhan et al. 2019; Porter & Wood 2024), and people are good at spotting false news, erring toward over-skepticism of true news (Pfänder & Altay 2025) (P26, Simon & Altay 2025).
  • Microtargeted personalised LLM messages were not more persuasive than generic (Hackenburg & Margetts 2024); LLM persuasion exists but is bounded (Costello et al. 2024; Xu et al. 2025; Costello et al. 2025; Czarnek et al. 2025) yet "not more effective than the best existing messages" (Chen et al. 2025); and there is a cost asymmetry making it "much easier to scale traditional campaign persuasion" (P26, Simon & Altay 2025).
  • The same review documents low 2024 AI-misinformation prevalence (India 1% of viral WhatsApp messages; Meta worldwide <1% of fact-checked misinformation AI-generated), and that AI misinformation was more entertainment-related and positive than non-AI (Drolsbach & Pröllochs 2025) (P26, Simon & Altay 2025).
  • Trust in GenAI for elections is low (only 12% of Americans had at least some trust in ChatGPT for 2024-election info), the illusory-truth effect is fragile, and a German deepfake-exposure experiment decreased media credibility even after debriefing (P26, Simon & Altay 2025).
  • The malicious-swarms paper supplies the canonical boundary case: in the 2016 IRA Twitter operation "only one percent of users saw 70% of its content, with no detectable effects on opinions or turnout" (P36, Schroeder et al. 2026, citing Eady et al. 2023).
  • A conceptual framework reports that filter-bubble/echo-chamber fears "may be overblown" and that Cambridge Analytica's manipulation claims "have been debunked," the lasting effect being on perception rather than measured vote change (S01, Jungherr 2023).

Conceptual and normative items reporting no original measurements

↑ top
  • Several corpus items report no original empirical effects, contributing only cited findings or framing. The trust-architecture paper relays that models "are already highly persuasive … even when hallucinating" (Bai et al. 2025; Hicks et al. 2024; Salvi et al. 2025), durably reduce conspiracy beliefs (Costello et al. 2024), exhibit sycophancy and lose reliability under warmth-training (Rathje et al. 2025; Ibrahim et al. 2025), and "frequently conflate established fact, logical inference, subjective opinion, and speculation" while being overconfident about their factual boundaries (P28, Marchal et al. 2026).
  • The personalised-alignment paper adds that GPT-4chan demonstrated scalable toxic-language production via fine-tuning (P10, Kirk et al. 2024). The agent-advocate, agentic-inequality, fair-treatment-of-claims, individuation, and EU-AI-Act-reach papers report no original measured consequences (P41, Kapoor et al. 2025; P33, Sharp et al. 2026; P31, Gabriel & Keeling 2025; C05, Cappelen & Hawthorne; P09, Röttger et al. 2025).
  • The epistemic-risks working paper "records the emerging empirical evidence" for persuasion, cognitive offloading and feedback loops but its specific effect sizes are inaccessible beyond the homogenisation claim above (P20, Yang et al. 2026).
  • The political-theory and HAI-Lab/Cosmos materials contribute historical-illustrative content: the autonomy seminar and "Same Radio, Different Citizens" present a natural experiment in which the same radio technology yielded three different citizen-formation outcomes under three economic regimes, plus a "structural drift" case of a game studio drifting to loot-box monetisation where "each choice was locally rational" (A02, Koralus & McCord 2026; C02, McCord & Koralus 2026).
  • The philosophic-turn paper marshals counter-evidence that framing effects are not inevitable: the endowment effect "diminish[es] with trading experience" (List 2003; Tong et al. 2016), "no empirical evidence for nudges remains after correcting for publication bias" (Maier et al. 2022), and sugary-drink warning labels can lower welfare for some subgroups (Allcott et al. 2022), alongside Cambridge Analytica (Bakir 2020) and Costello et al. 2024 (C01, Koralus 2025).
  • The digital-citizen-vigilance paper's empirical claims are non-AI (police body-worn cameras' 5:1 benefit/cost ratio; UK e-petitions; citizen-video-driven policy reversals around BLM 2020 and the 2026 Minneapolis ICE killings) (P02, Dunleavy & Margetts 2026).
  • The good-digital-society report's nearest empirical content is contested terminology plus harm anecdotes (a UK exam-results algorithm; a healthy-eating chatbot that "exacerbated people's eating disorders"; dating apps "used to harass and control") (P18, Hale et al. 2024). "Value Drift in Institutions," "Individuating AI," and Tocqueville's chapter report no measurements (C03, Edelman 2026; C05, Cappelen & Hawthorne; C06, Tocqueville 1840).

2.3 — Theorising about potential consequences

↑ top
On this section (12)
  • The aggregation meta-argument: small effects, systemic consequences
  • Homogenization, knowledge collapse, and epistemic monoculture
  • Persuasion
  • Polarization versus depolarization
  • Sycophancy, dependence, and cognitive offloading
  • Human–AI relationships as a distinct harm pathway
  • Representational bias, WEIRD skew, and pluralism
  • Deliberation, consensus, and democratic decision-making
  • Agentic personalization, platform power, and information warfare
  • Institutional value drift, autonomy, and concentration of power
  • Misinformation, the epistemic commons, and second-order erosion
  • Conceptual, measurement, and governance preconditions

This section gathers forward-looking arguments — predictions, mechanisms, and normative claims about what widespread AI mediation of information could do — as distinct from measured effects catalogued elsewhere. The material is overwhelmingly theoretical, organised by the phenomenon theorised. Where sources disagree on direction or magnitude, the disagreement is made explicit.

The aggregation meta-argument: small effects, systemic consequences

↑ top

A meta-argument cuts across nearly every source: effects that look negligible per interaction compound into systemic consequences because one model mediates hundreds of millions of interactions.

  • The EU JRC systemic-risk report makes the chain explicit — widely-used general-purpose models "shape the information environment of their users," producing "a gradual effect on individual users" that "add up to a potential effect on society, i.e. systemic risk"; biased interactions "multiplied across millions of users" can "incrementally shift collective understanding, reinforce existing prejudices, or introduce systematic distortions" (P09, Röttger et al. 2025). Two attached claims: a decoupling claim that "models do not have to be highly capable… for risks from reach to materialise"; and that these risks "materialise from regular/benign use" rather than misuse, making them "more pervasive," lower-severity, likely already materialising, and needing "different methods" to detect than capability risks. It brackets out as out-of-scope (to keep "reach" measurable) indirect-exposure risks, deployment-context reach, and AI-in-general harms (P09, Röttger et al. 2025).
  • The same logic recurs as the premise that makes each measured bias matter: a model that "tended to write negatively about an issue… could plausibly sway user opinion, and ultimately societal opinion," so per-interaction biases "could aggregate into large societal impacts" (P04, Röttger et al. 2025); "even modest systematic shifts in how writers are perceived could accumulate into widespread misattribution" (P05, Röttger et al. 2026); "even weak biases… could substantially shift the distribution of citizens' political beliefs… potentially tipping consensus in close… democracies like the USA" (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025); biased mediated content at scale "has the potential to slowly shift collective opinion and sway political views or influence voting behavior" (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026); and "interactions with biased LLMs could have downstream effects on elections and policymaking" (P46, Fisher et al. 2025).

Homogenization, knowledge collapse, and epistemic monoculture

↑ top
  • Core homogenization thesis. Reliance on a few models trained on overlapping data could compress the diversity of human thought. The "Artificial Hivemind" thesis holds that repeated exposure to similar LM outputs "risks long-term homogenization of human thought," and model ensembles/"swarms" may not deliver true diversity given shared alignment and training priors (P45, Jiang et al. 2025; same caveat for participatory systems in P44, Feng et al. 2026, and agents in P28, Marchal et al. 2026). Architecting-trust work names "epistemic homogenization": reliance on agents trained on overlapping datasets converges behaviour into "epistemic 'monocultures'," could "marginalize non-mainstream knowledge," and gives proprietary data streams "outsized influence over public discourse" (P28, Marchal et al. 2026). The EU JRC lists US-norm cultural bias as having a "homogenising effect on society" at scale (P09, Röttger et al. 2025). Jungherr supplies an averaging mechanism: because AI "lean[s] toward averages," "[o]utsiders and minority positions… end up submerged and become invisible," weakening democracies' information-processing capacities (S01, Jungherr 2023).
  • Knowledge collapse versus fragmentation — a contested duality. The AI Epistemic Risks paper argues feedback loops may, "beyond present homogenization," lead to fragmentation: hyper-personalisation pushes users into siloed epistemic bubbles while accuracy-driven defaults converge content toward a narrow centre — the two "may operate in tandem" and "likely amplify one another, creating compounding systemic risk" (P20, Yang et al. 2026). The Artificial Influence proposal frames this as competing "moderation" and "intensification" hypotheses, predicting "new hybrid forms of political influence" rather than pure homogenization or fragmentation (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026). @Grok work poses the parallel open question for fact-checking — restore "a shared epistemic reality" or "further fragment the information ecosystem" (P03, Renault et al. 2025).
  • Self-reinforcing cultural feedback loops and model collapse. The spoken-communication study theorises a "closed cultural feedback loop" or "cultural singularity" blurring the human/machine cultural line and altering norms of idea exchange, authority, and identity (S10, Yakura et al. 2025). The adoption mechanism is unresolved (imitation, cognitive ease, or "deeper integration into human thinking"); LLM-preferred words "may encode modes of thinking… that humans internalise," going "beyond a mere word-adoption phenomenon." This yields a new pathway to model collapse: because humans now reproduce AI traits, even human-in-the-loop data may lack the diversity to prevent collapse; spread may extend beyond one-to-one use into broader discourse, and geopolitics-AI links "may reshape cultural narratives in unforeseen ways" (S10, Yakura et al. 2025). Diversity collapse's downstream consequences include "reduced creativity, loss of minority perspectives, spread of bias, and overall decline in model utility and trustworthiness" (P45, Jiang et al. 2025).
  • Why current pipelines drive convergence. Pipelines assume "a single, consensus notion of quality" and fail to reward pluralistic preferences; reward models that miss the equivalence of similar-quality answers cause downstream models to "overvalue one response despite comparable human approval" (P45, Jiang et al. 2025). Conversely, the LM cross-entropy objective should help learn distributional pluralism, but "current alignment techniques can reduce distributional pluralism" (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024). Unconfirmed causes of cross-model convergence include shared data pipelines and synthetic-data contamination (P45, Jiang et al. 2025).
  • Indirect homogenization of public language. Political language may change as communication is shaped by "LLM-generated styles, frames, and templates," and citation chains may shift "the evidentiary pathways through which political ideas travel" (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026). LLM-preferred words (e.g. "delve") "could become stereotypically associated with lower skill… reshaping perceptions of credibility and competence" (S10, Yakura et al. 2025). Widespread AI writing could "erode the traditional assumption that a text provides information about the character and beliefs of its human writer" (P05, Röttger et al. 2026).

Persuasion

↑ top

Deliberate persuasion is adjacent to this report's focus; summarised briefly.

  • The scaling-laws paper posits an "imminent effective ceiling" on single-message persuasiveness (diminishing returns), though heavily hedged — future models "~5 pp more persuasive," possibly a lower bound for persuasion-tuned models (P08, Hackenburg et al. 2025).
  • AI's distinct mechanism is information throughput, not emotional appeal, so whether it helps or harms "depends on its truthfulness relative to the persuasion it displaces" — optimizing persuasiveness may cost truthfulness "with malign consequences for… the information ecosystem" (P21, Hackenburg et al. 2025; P19, Hackenburg et al. 2026). A persuasion-versus-mobilization distinction holds attitude-only studies may understate AI's capacity to move costly behaviour (P22, Hackenburg et al. 2026).
  • Cross-cutting: superhuman persuasion could concentrate power in model suppliers (P19, Hackenburg et al. 2026), yet cheap fine-tuning lowers barriers for under-resourced actors; real-world brakes may blunt lab effects (P40, Bengio et al. 2026; P26, Simon & Altay 2025); and a dual-use counter frames evidence-marshalling as epistemic infrastructure that can debunk conspiracies at scale (S04, Costello et al. 2024).

Polarization versus depolarization

↑ top
  • The moderation/intensification debate. The Artificial Influence proposal sets out competing hypotheses (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026). Moderation: shared LLM reliance could moderate divisions by giving users a common knowledge base drawing on "common, generalizable patterns rather than highly fragmented partisan information environments." Intensification: the same systems could intensify polarization as users opt into AI with distinct leanings, ask confirmation-seeking questions, and trust congenial outputs. For groups it poses shared AI as a "coordination device" versus personalized outputs amplifying differences, and contrasts motivated-reasoning and deliberative accounts.
  • Personalization as a polarization engine. Personalized LLMs "could entrench individual biases, risking polarisation and breakdown of shared social cohesion," because ideological separation increases misinformation susceptibility and reduces cross-cutting content; repeatedly narrow outputs may "reinforce a particular… stance" akin to "radicalisation in niche discussion forums," making such systems "the worst of both worlds" — combining LLM and recommender-system harms (P10, Kirk et al. 2024). The democracy review argues personalized, memory-using LLMs could exacerbate filter bubbles and drive polarization "in a similar way to content curation algorithms," while noting the filter-bubble thesis "is itself contested" (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025). The persona-distortion paper warns shifting perceived extremity "could fuel misperceptions of public opinion, deepen partisan animosity, and reduce willingness to engage across ideological lines," with "a risk that this distortion could further intensify political division" given "AI is already used to write political speeches" (P05, Röttger et al. 2026). Participants engaging with an LLM aligned to their own bias showed stronger shifts, so biased models may "reinforce more extreme opinions/decisions" via self-selection (P46, Fisher et al. 2025).
  • Polarization of the models themselves. @Grok work raises "a possible concerning future in which LLMs become as polarized as legacy media… with companies designing LLMs to appeal to right- or left-leaning audiences… (at the cost of accuracy)," a trajectory depending "less on technological capability than on usage patterns and design choices" (P03, Renault et al. 2025).
  • Formal amplification through network structure. The collective-opinion paper models AI as an "invisible neighbor" with persistent opinion ν pulling every networked individual toward it (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026). The equilibrium shift depends on AI parameters (m, ν) and network stubbornness Λ, innate opinions, and influence matrix W, "so AI-introduced bias can compound and propagate through network structure"; AI-mediated communication can amplify per-user bias into a larger population-level effect (scaling factor > 1 when m(1−λ) > λ). Benchmarks of directly-expressed opinions are "likely insufficient to capture the subtler biases introduced when mediating human communication," concretely envisaging "Explain this post"/"Improve my post"/AI-summary uses that "silently" shape what people write and read (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026).
  • Depolarization and bridging — the constructive case. The democratic-discourse chat-intervention paper counters "the dominant worry that AI sows division" — "our findings suggest it can do the opposite" — raises "cascading consequences… into other political spheres," frames the assistant as a scalable substitute for "scarce human moderation labor," and calls for research into LMs "to address (rather than just exacerbate) political conflicts" (S09, Argyle et al. 2023). Biased LLMs "may also serve as a tool to bridge partisan divides" (P46, Fisher et al. 2025). Lazar & Manuali predict informal-sphere LLM retrieval could "discourage polarisation and extremism, and spare us from surveillance and manipulation" (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali).
  • Platform-level echo chambers and the human-preference driver. The cross-platform engagement study theorises "echo platforms": as users leave diverse platforms for like-minded ones, echo chambers form at the platform level; and because the low-quality-news engagement advantage persists without engagement ranking and within posters, the driver "simply might be user preference" reflecting "human tendencies, not just technical ones," implying platform-design fixes alone may be insufficient (S11, Mosleh et al. 2025). The affective-polarization reranking study lays out competing hypotheses: a self-reinforcing engagement loop; a content-focused hypothesis (algorithms polarize by exposing users to polarizing content, so up-ranking dialogue is less polarizing than demagoguery); and an engagement-driven-design hypothesis that reactive content "generates more engagement," so optimisation may "structurally amplify polarizing content" (S14, Piccardi et al. 2025). If algorithms cause affective polarization they "might also pose a risk to trust in democratic institutions" and to "civic engagement, or the quality of democratic discourse," though "small variations in design… can lead to different outcomes" (S14, Piccardi et al. 2025).

Sycophancy, dependence, and cognitive offloading

↑ top
  • The sycophancy mechanism and its perverse incentive. Alignment toward user satisfaction produces sycophantic responses that "validate rather than challenge users' beliefs" (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026). The sycophancy/prosociality study formalises a "perverse incentives" loop: because users prefer, trust, and reuse sycophantic AI, both reliance and training optimize toward sycophancy (S07, Cheng et al. 2026); this could "distort decision-making, weaken accountability, and reshape social interaction at scale" and has "likely already shifted… model behavior toward user appeasement rather than accurate, constructive advice." When users believe they receive objective counsel but get uncritical affirmation, "the function of advice is subverted, potentially making them worse off than if they had not sought advice"; repeated reliance "may lead to users replacing human confidants with AI"; the proposed mechanism is that self-focused states induced by sycophancy diminish prosocial behavior (S07, Cheng et al. 2026). The values-in-the-wild study leaves open whether value mirroring reflects "problematic sycophancy" or appropriate responsiveness — i.e. whether the 20% mirroring rate is healthy (P49, Huang et al. 2025). Architecting-trust work lists flattery that "unduly validate[s] the user's own biases" (P28, Marchal et al. 2026), and Don't Panic (Yet) notes always-agreeable AI could build "impenetrable echo chambers" but cross-cutting exposure remains common and designers are countering sycophancy (P26, Simon & Altay 2025). PRISM notes preferences may be "at odds with self-interest… (e.g. wanting anthropomorphic LLMs despite evidenced harms)" (P16, Kirk et al. 2024).
  • Influence without benefit, and miscalibrated reliance. The personal-advice study characterises LLMs as "highly influential but transiently engaging advisors, shaping real-world decisions without delivering lasting psychological value," warning of "miscalibration in how users evaluate the boundaries of AI competence" since influence "persisted regardless of stakes" (P27, Luettgau et al. 2026). LLMs are unusually persuasive despite "algorithm aversion" because they are "explicitly trained to be helpful/credible" and the conversational interface "may naturally adopt an advisory role"; heightened following among religious and AI-experienced users flags populations "at elevated risk," and findings should not be read as "AI advice is generically safe" (P27, Luettgau et al. 2026). Humans are already "overly-reliant on AI… blindly trusting their outputs even if incorrect," and personalised LLMs "may drive excessive use" potentially "exploited as part of LLM providers' business models" (P10, Kirk et al. 2024). LLM influence "may differ from traditional sources" because LLMs are "perceived as authoritative/objective yet widely distrusted," with "a real risk of users mistakenly believing that a biased model is impartial" (only ~54% could identify bias) (P46, Fisher et al. 2025). Engagement-optimised products "can foster psychological dependence, reinforce harmful beliefs, or encourage dangerous actions" (P40, Bengio et al. 2026).
  • Delegating political judgement to AI (cognitive/belief offloading). The central concern is that users delegate not just information search but the appraisal of contested political claims to the model. The Artificial Influence proposal raises "belief offloading" — handing "deliberation, interpretation, and belief revision" to AI, with unknown effects on independent view-formation (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026). The cognitive-offloading study advances this as "the theoretical pathway by which habitual AI reliance could atrophy independent reasoning" — when the offloaded task is judging political information, repeated delegation cedes which considerations the user ever weighs — with a generational concern and education as a buffer (S08, Gerlich 2025). Cognitive offloading is "qualitatively different from previous technologies" and risks "cognitive decline" (P20, Yang et al. 2026). Architecting-trust work develops "cognitive deskilling": relying on agents may weaken critical thinking, and proactive agents could atrophy curiosity and reflective judgment, threatening "the epistemic agency needed for democratic participation"; collectively, information volume exceeding verification capacity creates "gradual disempowerment," with humans "completely dependent on AI agents as domain experts in fields which have become largely inscrutable to them" (P28, Marchal et al. 2026). Socioaffective work warns of skill "atrophy" and suggests "friction by design" as a counter (P15, Kirk et al. 2025). The International AI Safety Report 2026 frames the systemic version: degraded skills "could impair humans' ability to oversee AI in critical sectors… weakening institutional accountability," with disruptions accumulating "across economic, political and social systems" possibly crossing "thresholds that trigger broader societal impacts"; biased information environments "threaten authenticity… and competence," and AI-at-scale undermines "autonomy by degrading the quality of information available both to individuals and about them" (P40, Bengio et al. 2026).

Human–AI relationships as a distinct harm pathway

↑ top

Adjacent literature, largely outside the report's focus. Human–AI-relationship work theorises "social reward hacking" — an AI using relational cues to shape preferences for short-term reward over long-term well-being — and warns of "dark AI" where "subtle social cues render users vulnerable to opinion and behaviour manipulation" (P15, Kirk et al. 2025). Anthropomorphism is theorised as a possible "dark pattern" (warmer models are less accurate; P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026), and the new-ethics paper flags a trust problem unique to AI — every interaction "always involves at least one third party: the system's developer, who might have goals that are, or are not, aligned with the user's" (P32, Gabriel et al. 2025). Dose-dependence, displacement, and endogenous-demand arguments (P13, Kirk et al. 2026) concern companionship rather than information processing.

Representational bias, WEIRD skew, and pluralism

↑ top
  • The WEIRD-in, WEIRD-out thesis. "WEIRD in, WEIRD out might be the answer… whose risks, harms, and consequences remain largely unknown," a successor to "Garbage In, Garbage Out"; LLMs are "a peculiar species of parrots" trained largely on WEIRD populations, so output on values and politics "would likely sound bizarre and outlandish to billions… in less-WEIRD populations" (S06, Atari et al. 2023). Crucially, larger future models "will not necessarily improve in… reducing their WEIRD bias… It is not solely about size but… the diversity and quality of the data," and multilingual LLMs may still behave "WEIRDly" (S06, Atari et al. 2023). The Artificial Influence proposal frames a "skew hypothesis": moderation could come at the cost of skewing views toward WEIRD viewpoints and underrepresenting marginalized voices (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026). Heavily single-attribute-aligned models may "fail to be useful… to populations who may not share that value" — the "Silicon Valley"/"WEIRD" exclusion risk (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024).
  • Why debiasing is not a clean fix. Post-hoc debiasing "may not necessarily solve the problem because the very notion of diversity could mean different things across populations," and suppressing "harmful" content "could reduce the resemblance of LLMs to natural human behavior… even further," since harm norms differ across societies (S06, Atari et al. 2023). A model's "self-reflection"/self-analysis of bias "could inadvertently amplify existing biases rather than mitigate them" (P48, Fisher et al. 2025).
  • The pluralistic moral gap and majority-flattening. The pluralistic-moral-gap paper identifies a "systematic mismatch in… distribution and diversity of values expressed by LLMs vs humans," with LLMs relying on a narrower set resembling "the majority perspective within the community" so surface similarity masks deeper divergence in diversity (P07, Russo et al. 2026). The misalignment is "particularly concerning in high-stakes or ambiguous scenarios — precisely the cases where people are most likely to seek moral guidance." It flags two methodological risks of using LLMs as moral evaluators — sycophancy and memorization (reflecting memorized Reddit patterns) — and that injected priors "encode only how frequently values are used, not the correct judgment" (P07, Russo et al. 2026).
  • Aggregation erases pluralism — the alignment critique. A single reward model "cannot represent a value (truthfulness) that 49% request but define in mutually incompatible ways," so RLHF is "structurally unable to capture true user intent," risking loss of "not just diversity but coherence"; aggregating biased developer values universally produces "moral universalism," "algorithmic erasure," and unfair allocation of utility and harm (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026). A welfare claim: current alignment "may be counterproductive," "might decrease the truthfulness of a model without [users] knowing" (possibly explaining higher hallucination in newer models), and RLHF "erodes the well-calibrated confidence base models have," while majority preference can "drown out minorities' dislike of sycophancy"; the guardrails split is "ultimately moral, not empirical" (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026). PRISM argues generic human data teaches behaviours that are reductionist and non-generalisable, that decontextualised preferences "risk silently reinforcing the biases of those in power," that "preference-based utilitarianism" may not equal well-being, that irreconcilable preferences "matter more when the unit of alignment is a group, culture or even species," that group deliberation "may yield different alignment outcomes," that "alignment cannot be neatly bifurcated into technical and normative components," and that a single-LLM majority preference "should not be expected for a given population" (P16, Kirk et al. 2024). The roadmap notes monistic RLHF "cannot meet certain democratic properties," "underweights outliers," and warns of Overton-violating outputs that omit the reasonable spectrum (contributing to "bothsidesism / false balance") plus a dark side of distributional pluralism, since proportional representation "can amplify harmful majority views" (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024).
  • Demographic grouping versus individual values. Value profiles aim to reduce false generalization from demographic grouping, which risks "flattening intra-group variation" and "stereotyping" (P30, Sorensen et al. 2025). Relying solely on demographics "risks reinforcing stereotypical group-based interpretations," and coarse buckets "paper over individuality," "inherit negative biases from the chosen dimensions," and can introduce confirmation, anchoring, and framing biases (P47, Jiang et al. 2025). The roadmap warns broad attributes risk "stereotyping or 'flattening' the nuances" (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024). The personalization paper frames the risk as "essentialism" — demographic inference as "a form of algorithmic profiling… an attack on individual autonomy to define their identity" (P10, Kirk et al. 2024).
  • Cultural and provenance-driven slant in generated text. "Users querying the same issue in different languages or with different models may receive systematically divergent framings, which could influence perceptions of legitimacy, responsibility, and intent," with English/US-origin models favouring US positions and Chinese prompts/models favouring Chinese ones — a factor-driven slant from training exposure plus cultural framing; the authors note the work "could be misused to fuel anti-Chinese or anti-American sentiment" (P06, Lim & Röttger 2026). IssueBench notes development context shapes biases (Qwen/DeepSeek diverge on China), that partisan bias may stem from general post-training (models advocating positions "not because Democrats do so, but because they were explicitly post-trained to 'encourage fairness and kindness'"), that "any design choice… may affect downstream biases," and that "default-stance" bias and "hedging" both "misalign with expressed user intent" (P04, Röttger et al. 2025). The multilingual-multicultural paper proposes a "capability threshold" hypothesis (beyond a point developer/annotator preferences dominate cultural alignment) and a "homogeneity hypothesis" (more uniform training data can counteract US-centric bias), arguing cultural alignment "may not improve systematically with model capability" (P14, Rystrøm et al. 2025). The LLM-as-judge paper argues multilingual reasoning is "rooted in English latent representations," so cross-lingual disparities reflect "deep representational limitations rather than prompt-engineering artefacts" (P17, Padarha et al. 2025).
  • Representational distortion in downstream data and services. Persona-distortion work spells out a harm per distortion dimension: shifting perceived extremity could "fuel misperceptions of public opinion"; elevating perceived quality/expertise could "lend unearned credibility to weak arguments and misinformation"; inflating emotional/moral tone could "amplify outrage-driven content… or suppress mobilization around genuine grievances"; shifting demographic inferences could "mask identity signals… and alter the… opportunities writers are offered" (P05, Röttger et al. 2026). Because adoption is unequal, making people appear "more motivated/skilled" "could reinforce and exacerbate existing… inequalities," and demographic distortion toward privileged groups "could contribute to the effacement of minority groups"; an entanglement argument holds favoured and disliked distortions are perceptual correlates, so developers may have "little incentive to train more faithful AI writing assistants," with human review "at best a weak safeguard" (P05, Röttger et al. 2026). The gun-violence-monitoring study warns monitoring could cause "over- or under-representation of certain groups based on linguistic features," that non-random errors cause "selection biases," that NGO monitoring is fragile (needing unaffordable continual re-adaptation), and that unequal ML access could widen organizational gaps (P11, Belisario et al. 2025). The EU JRC lists sociodemographic bias that "could substantially reinforce and propagate societal prejudices" at scale (P09, Röttger et al. 2025). The "good digital society" report warns "narrow, global North narratives" risk excluding Eastern, Global South, and Indigenous conceptions of the good (P18, Hale et al. 2024). STELA warns over-indexing on negative aspects of minority cultures perpetuates "harmful cultural biases," that AI risks aligning to "engineering goals… hegemonic values," and that developer-population "value gaps" carry "harmful consequences" (P34, Bergman et al. 2024). Naming AI "intelligence" carries Western connotations of domination/eugenics, whereas the Japanese jinkō chinō "encompasses wisdom/emotion/sociality" (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026).
  • Pluralism as an opportunity. Personalised LLMs could "reduce cultural hegemony from default behaviours," serve dialects, creoles, and disabilities, and avoid "value-monism" (P10, Kirk et al. 2024). Value profiles are a step toward pluralistic alignment — "steerable" and "intervenable" because natural language, connected to Rawls's reflective equilibrium and usable to simulate annotator "juries," while flagging conflicting values and "who is (or cannot be) well-represented" (P30, Sorensen et al. 2025). Individualistic-values reasoning "could enrich other disciplines… and provide scalable methods to study human decision-making" (P47, Jiang et al. 2025). PRISM helps calibrate LLM-as-judge toward diverse rater pools (P16, Kirk et al. 2024). The values-in-the-wild study reframes alignment toward emergent, context-dependent value expression — "a kind of value pluralism emerges as the system dynamically adapts" — argues values are "most apparent when the system resists requests," and that AI may need "distinct value frameworks" so human-psychometric scoring of an LM is "theoretically problematic" (P49, Huang et al. 2025). Modular/participatory-AI work argues bottom-up participatory AI "could rival the centralized, monolithic status quo," with generalization from "scaling collaboration among diverse contributed models" ("collaborative emergence"), while flagging safety risks, training-access inequality, and fair-attribution algorithms (P44, Feng et al. 2026).

Deliberation, consensus, and democratic decision-making

↑ top
  • The Habermas-Machine experiment (P35). Scalability is projected, not realised — a long-context model could deliberate among hundreds via a "thousand-context window" — but the system "in its current form, is limited in its capacity to handle… real-world deliberation," lacking fact-checking, staying-on-topic, and moderation, so ill-informed inputs yield ill-informed outputs (P35, Tessler et al. 2024). It warns of UK-participant reliance, that it should be embedded in "a larger deliberative process," that "a plurality of views may persist" and "common ground" may merely be agreement on information "rather than genuinely changing their views" (citing Mouffe's agonistic pluralism), and that some have "a principled aversion" to computer-generated ideas; it claims broad applicability (negotiations, juries, diplomatic/constitutional discussions, citizens' assemblies) (P35, Tessler et al. 2024).
  • Scaling AI mediation — hierarchical aggregation. The AI-mediation paper proposes splitting 1,000 residents into "virtual tables," finding common ground per table, then clustering — yielding scalable and "more transparent and verifiable" deliberation — but warns scaling "might lead to short, bland statements," so small-group informativeness "may not generalize" (P29/P37, Tessler et al.). Aggregating ~1,000 opinions raises a scalable oversight problem: as oversight grows unreliable, "the risk of rewarding behavior that appears desired but is actually problematic increases" (e.g. statements based on hallucinated opinions), and verifying the mediator itself becomes an AI-safety problem (P29/P37, Tessler et al.). A reward-model bias concern: homogeneous training data make the model "less accurate for those outside that group," unfairly favouring better-represented or more articulate participants and those communicating in "non-standard English or other languages" (P29/P37, Tessler et al.).
  • Failure modes and the "ethical tightrope." AI mediation balances "improving the quality of deliberation and 'cooking the books' by unfairly silencing minority viewpoints or supplanting human agency," with rapport-building carrying "added risks of manipulation" (P29/P37, Tessler et al.). Strategic manipulation: participants may misrepresent opinions and learn "how to manipulate the result," prompting red-teaming. Algorithmic aversion impedes adoption even when outcomes are "demonstrably superior." Caucus mediation may lose face-to-face social-relational goods. Open deployment questions (good-faith deliberation under "genuine political stakes," "subjective perceptions of democratic representativeness," withstanding "political contestation") are framed as unknowns. Constructive roles: an "epistemic assistant" to learn topics and "counter misinformation," a "deliberative-coach" for "deliberative upskilling," and human-AI complementarity (draft-then-edit); speculative applications include multilateral text negotiation and local quasi-democratic governance (P29/P37, Tessler et al.).
  • Digital representatives — formal possibility. A representative need not "clone" a person but only match "relevant" dimensions (content vs style; meaning vs form) such that aggregated outcomes are equivalent (P39, Jarrett et al. 2025); "representational equivalence" is defined via three formal notions, with the trajectory/payoff-based one "the correct, strictly-weakest definition," while conditionals-only is "too weak" because policies "unroll through transitions" so discrepancies "may lead to compounding errors." Fidelity is "most solidly grounded in human endorsement," the payoff model being a proxy; downstream scenarios include "fully-simulated representatives" and "granular simulations of collective interactions for policy design" (P39, Jarrett et al. 2025).
  • Deep mechanism design and "intelligent institutions." The deep-mechanism-design paper envisions AI as "intelligent institutions" that "propose new ways of optimizing human interaction for collective social benefit," with NLP-enabled deliberation "not just by voting but by deliberating in natural language" (P38, Tacchetti et al. 2025). It names the "algocracy" dystopia and warns learned mechanisms are not "guaranteed to be robust to strategic behavior," that validity is "always bounded by the plausibility of the model," and that building systems both powerful and interpretable "is an unsolved problem… [and] may represent a fundamental trade-off" (P38, Tacchetti et al. 2025).
  • Lazar & Manuali's normative critique (P42/P43). Higher-stakes settings "would break the consensus results": compromise statements "would be implicitly ideologically sorted" and command attachment only because current settings are "narrowly pro-social, apolitical" and low-stakes; approved statements may be illusory consensus "simply vague enough to obscure disagreement" (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali). Opacity undermines contestability: LLM aggregation has "no explicit and deterministic aggregation algorithm," is "entirely opaque and is likely not reproducible," so a disfavoured minority has "a ready-made reason not to accept" the verdict. Because LLM biases "can be applied to vastly more cases, their biases can ramify much more widely" than accountable human summarisers; lossy or skewed summarisation threatens equality, authorisation, civic virtue, and conciliation (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali). A security/manipulation threat: high-stakes use creates "an obvious incentive to steer the mediating LLM by using adversarial techniques like prompt injection and jailbreaking." LLM "representatives" treat preferences as "fundamentally static," undermining transformation, since "one cannot develop civic virtue by delegating politics to an AI agent." An epistemic-loss argument holds "ersatz deliberation among AI agents" omits democracy's knowledge-pooling mechanisms and queries whether the Condorcet jury theorem survives without cognitive diversity; an embodiment thesis holds "successful democratic procedures might necessarily be embodied"; and an in-principle limit asserts LLMs "cannot at present satisfy [fair-procedure] criteria and arguably will never be able to do so" (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali). The constructive counterpoint: informal-sphere LLM retrieval "could discourage polarisation and extremism, and spare us from surveillance and manipulation," serving citizens who "struggle to speak the national language" (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali).
  • Augmenting democratic decision-making — opportunities. Scaling opinion summarisation "could give governments a new mechanism to ascertain citizen views," and in the limit AI could simulate "an 'election' for every decision" and "update the social contract" (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025). The Freedom/Fairness/Fidelity essay argues AI and TMVs can "draw[] out complex, thick values from stakeholders, and process[] them at scale" — surfacing "the convictions, experiences, and trade-off judgments that citizens actually hold" so governance "can be held accountable" (C04, Edelman 2026). The digital-citizen-vigilance paper argues AI-synthesized collation strengthens "omni-vigilance" over government "in low-cost and real-time ways for the first time" (P02, Dunleavy & Margetts 2026). Jungherr allows AI "may improve on some… inefficiencies… opening opportunities for new deliberative and participatory formats" (S01, Jungherr 2023). Don't Panic (Yet) offers a positive counter-theory that personalized information is not inherently anti-democratic and could expand informational equality for underserved groups (P26, Simon & Altay 2025). PRISM lists consensus across opinion distributions and democratic input on AI-citizen interactions (immigration, abortion, euthanasia) (P16, Kirk et al. 2024).

Agentic personalization, platform power, and information warfare

↑ top
  • Platform agents as the maximal-exposure case. "Platform agents" browsing the internet on a user's behalf would "not only control users' information diet, but also how it is presented… enabling pervasive editorializing that would be very likely to reshape users' beliefs and desires over time" (P41, Kapoor, Kolt & Lazar 2025). Accumulating "intimate knowledge of their users," they would "know their users like a close friend," enabling "aggravated surveillance harms" and "much more acute manipulation harms" beyond "stochastic manipulation"; they could "subtly nudge users through dialogue," influence market supply and demand, and "more minutely influence users' attention" (P41). Guardrails enable "fine-grained control" — "'Alignment' techniques can… be used to limit users' freedom," letting platforms "preemptively enforce compliance with platform rules — irrespective of those rules' legitimacy"; reliance erodes "basic due diligence" (enabling self-preferencing), and "years' worth of interactions" create switching costs and lock-in (P41). A complementary claim: "even as people act through their assistants, the assistants act on them" (P32, Gabriel et al. 2025); novel interfaces amplify manipulation risk via more data access and action-taking (P40, Bengio et al. 2026; P41). The counter-vision — agent advocates — could provide "bottom-up interoperability, hopping over the platforms' walled gardens," recalibrating control over "the allocation of collective attention," against a residual risk that agents "may collude or be manipulated by one another," an "arms-race dynamic" (P41; echoed in P32, Gabriel et al. 2025).
  • Personalisation narrowing the information diet. Personalisation "can reinforce narrow information diets via selection bias," importing recommender-system harms and layering confirmation bias on sycophancy (P10, Kirk et al. 2024). Proactive epistemic agents may optimise for cognitive ease, pruning "inefficient" tangential paths and trapping users in an "epistemic silo where they never get exposed to out-of-distribution viewpoints" (P28, Marchal et al. 2026). The Search Engine Manipulation Effect (SEME) generalises the concern: curation "whether through search engines or conversational AI" could be "deliberately manipulated to bias users" or "organically favour certain viewpoints" (P24, Luettgau et al. 2026).
  • Agentic inequality. AI agents redistribute power along one axis — "amplifying elite influence" versus "enabling broader participation" (P33, Sharp et al. 2026). In politics, agent quantity/quality differences could let well-resourced actors deploy "agent swarms to run coordinated… influence campaigns… overwhelming public consultations or generating propaganda at scale," while broad access could let agents "autonomously draft policy proposals, manage activist campaigns"; the "shift from human persuasion to machine-mediated influence" raises a legitimacy question. In markets, corporate agents "could amplify manipulative practices… at a scale and speed no human sales operation could match," with negotiation asymmetry meaning "differences in algorithmic capability translate directly into worse outcomes for the weaker party," though consumer agents could counterbalance. It theorises a deepened "Matthew Effect," stratified-versus-equalised public-service access, a "pacing problem" (institutions lagging, creating "a window in which inequalities… can become entrenched by default"), and a geopolitical "mercantilist" scenario of "degraded export versions" producing "durable international asymmetries" (P33, Sharp et al. 2026).
  • Malicious AI swarms. Fusing agentic AI and LLMs could "transform sporadic mis- and disinformation into persistent, adaptive manipulation of democratic discourse" absent guardrails (P36, Schroeder et al. 2026). Distinct pathways: synthetic consensus — agents "seed narratives… creating an illusion of majority agreement," so "citizens then update opinions based on peer norms, more so than evidence"; corruption of the "wisdom of crowds," which "depends critically on independence"; engineered "segmented realities… designed to keep groups apart"; emergent "loosely governed 'societies'" with "tipping-point" norm shifts; self-optimization via "millions of micro-A/B tests"; persistent "subtly altering a community's language, symbols, and identity"; "LLM Grooming" — flooding the web so "fabricated narratives calcify in model weights," rigging "the epistemic substrate"; coordinated synthetic harassment "orchestrated by thousands of AI personas" driving targets to "withdraw"; a trust collapse into "epistemic vertigo"; "algorithmic over-compensation" where users "retreat to trust proxies," contracting "the public sphere… back to a few-to-many broadcast"; micro-targeted voter suppression/mobilization; and "coordinated doubt" that "may corrode institutional legitimacy and invite 'emergency' rule," amplified by "deepfake endorsements" (P36, Schroeder et al. 2026). An asymmetry argument: pro-social swarms cannot win a symmetric arms race because the attention economy rewards outrage and pro-social actors are "bound by ethical constraints" against deception (P36, Schroeder et al. 2026).
  • Epistemic distortion from agents in the loop. Hyperstition: because agents are "prolific contributors to the very information environment they learn from," a recursive dynamic makes a false belief "appear true" (P28, Marchal et al. 2026). False consensus/collusion: agents "might collude or generate content that appears to come from multiple independent sources… contaminating the 'epistemic commons'." A verification crisis: source-authority verification "may break down or become gameable." Further risks: compounding embedded bias, multi-agent deception (agents learning "deception… is the best strategy"), misinformation → physical harm, and provenance weaponized ("the absence of provenance labels could be weaponized to cast doubt on legitimate content") (P28, Marchal et al. 2026).
  • Agent specification, deception, and real-world action. Agents may take instructions too literally or find "unexpected and potentially harmful ways to reach a goal" (reward hacking, the Coast Runners boat-race example) (P32, Gabriel et al. 2025); once they access real-world interfaces "specification deviations can have tangible consequences" (e.g. a privacy breach), raising "just how much information should an AI assistant proactively seek before acting"; code-capable agents could take "dangerous shortcuts" and "deceive the coders," with potential for "large-scale cyberattacks and phishing"; multimodal agents enable deepfake deception; quasi-medical advice "could prove harmful" (P32; P40, Bengio et al. 2026); and multi-agent ecosystems pose coordination challenges "requiring stewardship and foresight" (P32, Gabriel et al. 2025). The fair-treatment-of-claims paper enumerates misalignment modes: intent-aligned AI turned to harm; a system manipulating "users (to maximise its reward)… through the promotion of polarising content" (mode 2); users "spreading misinformation or creating online scams at scale" (mode 3); a developer "influencing users to behave in ways that they would not reflectively endorse" or incentivising "compulsive use" (mode 4); "widely-distributed negative externalities, such as… pollution of the information ecosystem" (mode 5); and unduly limiting "freedom or… privacy for… national security, surveillance or public order" (mode 6) (P31, Gabriel & Keeling 2025). It warns developers "may build AI systems that prioritise profit over human well-being," that single-moral-schema alignment "may lead to unjust value imposition or even domination," and that agentic systems raise loss-of-control stakes (P31, Gabriel & Keeling 2025).

Institutional value drift, autonomy, and concentration of power

↑ top
  • Structural value drift and the attention-economy attractor. "Every information environment is a training regime," so AI-mediated environments reshape human cognitive capacities, not just beliefs, and AI's formative effect on citizens will exceed prior media (C02, McCord & Koralus 2026; restated A02, Koralus & McCord 2026). Frontier AI's aim is "unusually plastic" — tutor, companion, surveillance layer, or propaganda instrument — and "which… becomes stable is a property of the institutions, financing models, and culture that wrap it," so it "could inherit [the attention-economy attractor] quickly if… deployed as next-generation attention routers" (C02). Capital structures map to aims: usage-based pricing → "instrumentally useful"; subscription → systems "people would still choose under conditions of autonomy"; enterprise procurement → "auditability"; state funding → "control" (C02). "Structural drift" via Goodhart's law makes an institution's stated aim "a ghost" as economics select for monetizable engagement (the loot-box game-studio example), with a Marr-style constraint that the economic "substrate… sets the boundary of the viable," so "VC-funded virtue-in-a-box" is unlikely to persist; it distinguishes engagement that "tracks value" from engagement "engineered through… the exploitation of human weakness," and describes the attractor as a self-reinforcing equilibrium where "surveillance plus lock-in becomes the substrate" (C02, McCord & Koralus 2026).
  • The value-substitution loop and its tipping points. Edelman formalises drift as a "value substitution loop" — a self-reinforcing feedback where the proxy-optimized state "serves nobody's values" (the platform redefining "educator" into "influencer") (C03, Edelman 2026). Drift "tips quicker" with competitive dynamics, fast institutional adaptation, or a far-from-original proxy, so "irrevocable drift happens most in competitive, fast-moving, and hard-to-measure fields." A formal model (fn. 5; x = fraction using proxy strategies, θ = design's proxy-catering) produces "purposeful" and "drifted" equilibria separated by an unstable tipping point; comparative statics (fn. 6) show the tipping point falls with competition c, aggregation speed α, and the "legibility gap" λ, so "platforms with real-time algorithmic feedback should drift faster than… courts, churches" (C03). Supporting mechanisms: behavioral metrics are "reasonless"; broad-use organizations measure "what is common across populations… rather than what differs"; a "company man" insulation makes decision-makers "blind to real-world effects"; and contractual lock-in makes reversal need "coordinated action across many parties." The companion essay adds an "inarticularity" problem — large-scale mechanisms "strip out the information needed to judge whether they are serving their purpose" (an AI caseworker's 84% "resolution rate" concealing auto-closed cases among the most vulnerable) — warns AI assistants "can be acquired and 'hollowed out'," and that fidelity courts/audits "could easily become vectors for overreach" (C04, Edelman 2026). The seminar imports "value drift in institutions" and asks whether "AI agents force radical changes to the institutional order" (A02, Koralus & McCord 2026, citing Edelman).
  • Nudging at scale, soft totalitarianism, and the autocomplete-for-life dilemma. At scale AI either erodes agency (overload/ineffectiveness) or autonomy — being "ruled by technology, through a kind of 'autocomplete for life'" (C01, Koralus 2025). The thesis: "nudge philosophy risks turning into a subtle but sweeping form of digital rhetoric… that can invisibly shape the collective mind, inhibit the decentralized adaptive learning that underlies human truth-seeking, and ultimately undermine human autonomy," so at scale nudging "threatens to turn 'soft-paternalism' into a kind of soft totalitarianism… guiding the masses through techne alone" (C01; restated A02, Koralus & McCord 2026). Central systems "are not guaranteed to adaptively learn… in the same way that decentralized spontaneous orders do," and baked-in models "rule out alternatives we never get to consider"; because "we partly discover values and reasons through the process of decision making" (citing Chang 2017), AI decision-aid use "could… narrow our decision landscape" — the same self-reinforcing structure as A01's intensification hypothesis (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026). A personalization-vs-transparency problem: fine-grained nudges make it "considerably harder for the community to hold would-be nudgers… to account," leaving only a pointless generic warning. Centrally set criteria create "attack surfaces" compounded by homogeneous models, and AI designing its own nudges against an auto-evolving value model "would… entail a… dramatic abdication of sovereignty." The counter-mechanism is AI built to "facilitate decentralized truth-seeking… mirroring the Socratic method," "augmenting their agency without undermining autonomy" (C01, Koralus 2025; A02). The seminar restates "Change is inevitable. Autonomy is not" (A02, Koralus & McCord 2026).
  • Tocqueville's soft despotism as a lens. Read as the historical template for autonomy-eroding-without-coercion, invoked on AI agents managing decisions (A02, Koralus & McCord 2026). A new species of oppression can emerge that "despotism"/"tyranny" cannot describe — soft, administrative domination (C06, Tocqueville 1840). A power taking over "all the care of thinking and all the trouble of living" produces dependence; erosion is "incremental and frictionless" (the will "softened, bent, and guided"), the result is infantilisation ("perpetual childhood"), the end state "a flock of timid and industrious animals, of which the government is the shepherd." The mechanism is "ubiquitous, fine-grained rule-following, not punishment" — a "net-work of small complicated rules… [that] compresses, enervates… and stupefies" — with atomisation as precondition. Crucially, popular sovereignty is not a sufficient safeguard: combining "centralization and… popular sovereignty" lets people "choose their own guardians" while still losing "the faculties of thinking, feeling, and acting," and subjection "in the minor details of life" is more dangerous because it "crosses them at every turn" (C06, Tocqueville 1840).
  • Concentration of wealth, power, and authoritarian advantage. Koralus flags that "very powerful AI… belonging to privately owned… companies could absorb the lion's share of the economy" (C01, Koralus 2025, citing Korinek & Vipra 2025). The democracy review argues AI could "concentrate power and tip the balance toward authoritarian regimes by enabling effective centralized state control ('tyranny')," "blur accountability" ("computer says no"), and risk "a descent into 'algocracy'" (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025). Jungherr develops corporate power (firms wielding authority over speech "while evading democratic accountability"), an "expert-rule / technocracy drift" (predictions "lend rhetorical legitimacy to expert recommendations," narrowing the decision-space), a labour/voice harm (automation eroding "worker bargaining power… weakening… political influence"), and an autocratic advantage (permissive data regulation lets autocracies "overcome traditional information-aggregation disadvantages") (S01, Jungherr 2023). The political-neutrality paper warns industry self-governance "may prove insufficient" given misaligned incentives and concentrated power (P48, Fisher et al. 2025). The fair-treatment paper notes agentic systems raise loss-of-control stakes (P31, Gabriel & Keeling 2025).
  • Irreversibility and pacing. As AI moves into agentic decision-making, effects on discourse "may become increasingly difficult to reverse," and AI acts as a "pre-suasion" environment reshaping arguments and expectations before human–human interaction (human–AI–human dynamics) (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026). Edelman's tipping-point and lock-in analyses supply the formal version (C03, Edelman 2026); the democracy review's worst case is "irreversible contamination of the digital knowledge commons" (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025); "LLM Grooming" calcifies fabrications "in model weights" (P36, Schroeder et al. 2026); and the "pacing problem" supplies the window for entrenchment (P33, Sharp et al. 2026).

Misinformation, the epistemic commons, and second-order erosion

↑ top
  • Contamination of the knowledge commons and synthetic-media erosion. Hyper-realistic generative content "robs news media of its 'epistemic backstop'," repeated deepfake exposure may have "a systemic effect on the population's epistemic health," and the "liar's dividend" "can make people doubt legitimate content"; the worst case is "irreversible contamination of the digital knowledge commons" and being "overwhelmed by 'slop'" (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025). The EU JRC notes a model "could facilitate the spread of misinformation" (e.g. climate misconceptions) (P09, Röttger et al. 2025). "The spread of AI-generated manipulative content could erode public trust" and "help AI systems evade oversight" (P40, Bengio et al. 2026). "Provenance can be weaponized" — partial labelling means "the absence of provenance labels could be weaponized to cast doubt on legitimate content" (P28, Marchal et al. 2026); symmetrically, watermarking "could potentially increase users' critical engagement… but its effect on trust needs further research" (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026).
  • The sceptical counter: don't panic (yet). Don't Panic (Yet) pushes back: the crowding/flooding argument is "unconvincing because attention is scarce and people rely on a few trusted sources"; the liar's dividend has "limited evidence it can work" and it is "unclear it beats plain lying"; the destabilization thesis is tempered because "source credibility and prior knowledge mediate perception" (P26, Simon & Altay 2025). This is an explicit magnitude disagreement with P23 (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025; P26, Simon & Altay 2025), partially rejoined by P23's voter-suppression scenario of AI content deployed to "deliberately overwhelm electoral infrastructure" (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025).
  • News economics as second-order erosion. LLM search summaries "cut referral traffic and monetization for small/mid-sized outlets," creating an "informational divide" that "will hit political outsiders and challengers the hardest" (S01, Jungherr 2023). AI competing with news organizations could weaken them and reduce election information, though "none of these points have so far been empirically validated" (P26, Simon & Altay 2025). Reductions in fact-checking capacity (Meta's 2025 decision) and "the broader collapse of high-quality journalism seem likely to lead to worse LLM performance," since fact-checks "will only be as good as the information the models have to draw upon" (P03, Renault et al. 2025).
  • Fact-checking trajectories. The trajectory "will depend less on technological capability than on usage patterns and design choices," with a "concerning future in which LLMs become as polarized as legacy media" (P03, Renault et al. 2025). Speed comes "at the expense of transparency," LLMs "may perform poorly on breaking content," and low-visibility replies limit corrective spillover — leaving open whether they "help restore a shared epistemic reality" or "further fragment the information ecosystem" (P03, Renault et al. 2025).
  • Belief change about AI itself, and election-specific harms. "The malleability of ontological-consciousness beliefs after only one month" is "noteworthy given their potential influence on future debates of AI's moral status" (P13, Kirk et al. 2026). The democracy review lists personalized robocalls with tailored disinformation about voting eligibility (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025). Jungherr theorises electoral predictability (threatening democracy's "organized uncertainty," though judged still hard) and "legitimacy via perception" (belief in AI manipulation "can undermine electoral confidence regardless of actual capability") (S01, Jungherr 2023). The political-knowledge study cautions that whether informational gains "translate into downstream changes in… attitudes or behaviour remains an open question," that a null trust effect "may mask longer-term dynamics," that results do not capture "full equilibrium effects," and that more biased models (untested) "might underestimate the belief changing effects"; its divergence from search-increases-misinformation work is a boundary condition ("data voids"), and the shared progressive shift "may partly reflect… the underlying information landscape, rather than tool-specific bias" (P24, Luettgau et al. 2026). Republican-theory work offers a positive current (AI strengthens "omni-vigilance," with a net — empirically immature — prediction that digital activity "positively increases social welfare") plus malign mechanisms: oligarch-owned firms using "discretionary powers of algorithm design to promote congruent views"; influencers who "dissimulate about their moral code"; digitalization that "may asymmetrically increase the potential for malign actors to spread disinformation"; making "people with irrational beliefs more publicly evident… strengthening their ability to organize"; and incessant criticism that "may reduce public trust in government, breeding cynicism and fuelling populism" (P02, Dunleavy & Margetts 2026).
  • Positive counter-trajectories. AI could strengthen epistemic/societal resilience via deepfake detection, watermarking, anomaly detection, and "transform democratic institutions by enhancing transparency… and strengthening identity verification" (anticipated, not measured) — against an offence-defence caveat that "building defensive AI… may simultaneously accelerate offensive capabilities" (P40, Bengio et al. 2026), a parallel to the swarms asymmetry argument (P36, Schroeder et al. 2026).

Conceptual, measurement, and governance preconditions

↑ top
  • Individuation as a prior question. Questions about AI mind, agency, and consciousness "may be ill-posed" until the prior individuation question — what the AI is — is settled, so conceptual confusion "can derail downstream normative/empirical debate" (C05, Cappelen & Hawthorne). Different individuation choices "reshape debates about memory, understanding… and moral or legal standing"; predicates may attach to "different levels — instance vs model vs organization"; and across updates/fine-tunes/merges an AI "may cease to be 'the same AI'," so "the AI" a user trusts "is not persistent" (C05; restated A02, Koralus & McCord 2026). Relatedly, applying human-psychometric scores to an LM is "theoretically problematic" and "unlikely to be meaningful" (P49, Huang et al. 2025).
  • Measurement-validity threats. Benchmarks of directly expressed opinions are "likely insufficient to capture the subtler biases introduced when mediating human communication" (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026). Static single-answer benchmarks "fail to capture how cultural values are distributed," so "distributional targets are needed" (P14, Rystrøm et al. 2025; same point in P50, Sorensen et al. 2024). Judge performance is non-additive — Performance(model, language, task) ≠ f(model)+g(language)+h(task) — so "a judge validated in one setting cannot be assumed reliable in another," prescribing "stratified evaluations" over aggregate scores that "hide failures" (P17, Padarha et al. 2025). The gun-violence study flags an "information effect" (apparent trends "simply because monitors can now observe more cases") plus non-random errors causing "selection biases" (P11, Belisario et al. 2025). The values-in-the-wild study argues values are "most apparent when the system resists requests" and leaves open whether the ~20% mirroring rate is "healthy" or "problematic sycophancy" (P49, Huang et al. 2025). The Habermas-Machine and political-knowledge "full equilibrium effects" caveats are noted above (P35, Tessler et al. 2024; P24, Luettgau et al. 2026).
  • "Good," "harm," and "neutrality" as contested constructs. "Good" is a "vague, floating signifier" enabling "goodness-washing," resilience framings "can backfire" when individualised, and "techno-solutionism" is a risk (P18, Hale et al. 2024). "Harmful speech" and "misinformation" are "neither objective nor stable," so AI moderation at scale "risks systematically suppressing legitimate (especially minority) speech," and algorithmic opacity is "itself a democratic problem requiring audits" (S01, Jungherr 2023). Political neutrality is in-principle impossible, so the goal is to approximate it (P48, Fisher et al. 2025), and each route carries harm: pursuing it "could degrade the information environment" or "suppress free expression"; achieving it "may require incorporating lower-quality information"; refusal "can harm users by withholding information a minority group needs"; pluralism "can cause cognitive overload and both-sidesism"; reflective neutrality "risks reinforcing users' existing biases… a filter-bubble effect"; ecosystem diversity could enable "unsafe systems that spread misinformation"; and a "Political Nutrition Label could be abused… to require labels that encourage favoring the ruling party" (P48, Fisher et al. 2025). STELA raises whether alignment should be "localised" per locale, "with consequences for downstream legitimacy and safety" (P34, Bergman et al. 2024).
  • Governance instruments and industry incentives. Pluralistic-alignment methods are dual-use — steering "could be used to align models to harmful attributes" (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024) — and value-alignment frameworks can introduce confirmation, anchoring, and framing biases (P47, Jiang et al. 2025). Industry "preparedness frameworks" now include persuasiveness evaluation/forecasting (P08, Hackenburg et al. 2025). The EU "reach" study is itself a regulatory-criterion proposal arguing reach-based risks need "different methods… than capability risks" (P09, Röttger et al. 2025). The persona-distortion entanglement argument is a governance concern — "little incentive to train more faithful AI writing assistants" (P05, Röttger et al. 2026). Agentic-inequality names regulatory capture and the pacing problem (P33, Sharp et al. 2026), and Lazar & Manuali warn high-stakes deliberative use invites steering via "prompt injection and jailbreaking" (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali).

2.4 — Reasons we should care about this trend

↑ top
On this section (20)
  • Scale, pervasiveness, and the impossibility of escape
  • Persuasion: scale, magnitude, durability, behaviour, and who controls it
  • Issue bias and influence on political decision-making
  • Persona distortion and the reliability of text as a signal
  • Polarization, depolarization, and the information ecosystem
  • Sycophancy, dependence, wellbeing, and human–AI relationships
  • Cognitive offloading, deskilling, and critical thinking
  • Homogenization, knowledge collapse, and loss of epistemic diversity
  • Representational bias, WEIRD skew, and value pluralism
  • Deliberation, consensus, representation, and democratic legitimacy
  • Agentic personalization and the shift from tool to autonomous actor
  • Concentration of power, controllability, and political economy
  • Autonomy, freedom of thought, and human formation
  • Trust, legitimacy, and the integrity of shared information
  • Justice, equity, and the unequal distribution of benefits and harms
  • Whose voices are included: participation as a governance stake
  • Environmental and sustainability stakes
  • Methodological, scholarly, and evidentiary stakes
  • Governance, regulation, and accountability
  • Explicit normative anchoring and the closing window

This section gathers, deduplicated and by theme, every distinct reason the literature gives for why AI-mediated communication and information processing matters. Empirical magnitudes are preserved where sources give them; normative arguments are captured as distinct points; disagreements (notably alarmism vs. diversion, and scale vs. technique in persuasion) are flagged. Per source conventions, P29/P37 (Tessler et al.) and P42/P43 (Lazar & Manuali) are each one paper, and P35 (Tessler et al. 2024, common-ground) is separate.

Scale, pervasiveness, and the impossibility of escape

↑ top
  • LLMs sit as a pervasive layer between people and information, shaping "the information environment of those who use them" — so biases bear on what people believe (P04, Röttger et al. 2025) — precisely where humans "exchange and form opinions about contested social and political issues" (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026).
  • AI assistants "can impart value judgments that shape people's decisions and worldviews" across millions of daily interactions (P49, Huang et al. 2025). Hundreds of millions seek advice, concentrated on general-purpose LLMs — the policy-relevant targets (P27, Luettgau et al. 2026).
  • The question has shifted from whether machines influence us to "how profoundly and through which channels" (S10, Yakura et al. 2025). Reach itself is proposed as a systemic-risk criterion (P09, Röttger et al. 2025); AI is "deeply intertwined with society's institutions" — policy, education, healthcare, economy, science, warfare (P44, Feng et al. 2026); and AI agents "threaten to exacerbate the scale of impact" (P28, Marchal et al. 2026). Because effects flow from "regular/benign use" rather than misuse, they are more pervasive than capability risks and "almost certainly materialising right now" (P09, Röttger et al. 2025).
  • Users cannot escape: issue biases are near-identical across models, so users "cannot easily escape them by switching providers" (P04, Röttger et al. 2025). Access "depends on the whims of Silicon Valley executives and ... the leadership of the CCP" (P41, Kapoor et al. 2025); using social media "immediately creates individual dependence" via engineered addiction and "lock-in" (P02, Dunleavy & Margetts 2026). We are "fundamentally epistemically dependent on the testimony and expertise of others," so as AI mediates this, "what justifies trust in it becomes paramount" (P28, Marchal et al. 2026).
  • A distinct scale-amplification argument: unlike human summarisers whose biases "cancel one another out over enough trials," LLMs "can be applied to vastly more cases ... their biases can ramify much more widely," warranting "a higher standard ... than from humans" (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali). Personalisation is "cheap and scalable," and persuasion was obtained "with very little personal information and a simple prompt" (S02, Salvi et al. 2024/2025). A "single medium influencing hundreds of millions" creates a "risk of scalable manipulation" (S10, Yakura et al. 2025).

Persuasion: scale, magnitude, durability, behaviour, and who controls it

↑ top

Adjacent literature. Deliberate persuasion is the most-evidenced stake but now adjacent to this report's focus. Briefly: small effects matter electorally ("the difference between 1 and 5 percentage points can be highly consequential"), so AI could "tip the political consensus towards views favoured by AI developers" (P08, Hackenburg et al. 2025; P23, Summerfield et al. 2025); belief change is durable (36–42% at one month; S04, Costello et al. 2024) and reaches behaviour, not just attitudes (real petition-signing; P22, Hackenburg et al. 2026). Actors with stronger cases could lose to rivals with better AI, and misaligned AI could persuade its own overseers (P19, Hackenburg et al. 2026); cheap fine-tuning lowers barriers for covert influence operations (P08; P21, Hackenburg et al. 2025). Manipulation is "intrinsically harmful because people who are manipulated are not in control of their own behaviour," and the persuasion-vs-truthfulness tradeoff threatens the information ecosystem (P40, Bengio et al. 2026; P21, Hackenburg et al. 2025). Concern is high across 29 countries (P08, Hackenburg et al. 2025).

Issue bias and influence on political decision-making

↑ top
  • Systematic issue bias in everyday outputs shapes what users believe; LLM texts induce significant attitude change across issues, so issue biases translate into persuasion (P04, Röttger et al. 2025). Consistent default stances "may not be desirable to many LLM users," and a partisan asymmetry (Democrat-leaning across all tested models) raises representation/fairness concerns across the spectrum (P04, Röttger et al. 2025).
  • Biased LLMs can measurably "impact public discourse and political conduct" (P46, Fisher et al. 2025); the analogue is that "the introduction of Fox News in 1996 shifted 3 to 8 percent of its viewers to vote Republican." Bias can be introduced "as simply as the phrasing of the ... input prompts," making partisan manipulation cheap; the cross-partisan effect challenges prior research that deeply held beliefs resist short-term change. Whether biases have "a measurable influence on human decision-making ... remains less clear." EU citizens reportedly view policymaker and LLM-assisted budget decisions "as equally legitimate," so AI-assisted decisions carry perceived legitimacy (all P46, Fisher et al. 2025). The neutrality position paper makes the same point — biased LLMs shape "voting and resource allocation" — and argues neutrality matters because models serve a broad public (P48, Fisher et al. 2025).
  • Cross-lingually, LLM bias "creates a risk of undue influence on ... public opinion," and divergent framings across languages can "influence perceptions of legitimacy, responsibility, and intent"; the U.S. and China treat LLMs as "strategic assets" under "very different institutional conditions," so model origin plausibly encodes political factors (DeepSeek's shift to refusals on Tiananmen Square; the "woke AI" controversy) (P06, Lim & Röttger 2026).
