Political bias could "threaten voter autonomy" and sway opinion on AI regulation itself (P09, Röttger et al. 2025). AI assistants "can impart value judgments" at massive scale (P49, Huang et al. 2025).
  • On the consumption side: conversational AI raises political knowledge as effectively as self-directed search, which matters because democracy "relies on the electorate having access to reliable information, especially before elections," with concern LLMs "will produce unreliable or biased answers"; misinformed citizens "make systematically different policy judgements" and are "more resistant to correction" (P24, Luettgau et al. 2026). A distinct curation stake: biased rankings can shift undecided voters "by 20% or more ... without their awareness" (the Search Engine Manipulation Effect) (P24, Luettgau et al. 2026). LLMs have shifted expressed opinions "without their awareness in seemingly innocuous interactions, such as during assisted writing," with severe potential consequences for politics, medicine, science, law, and the free press (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026). Opinionated models "affect users' downstream judgments," a reason to present multiple reasonable views (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024).

Persona distortion and the reliability of text as a signal

↑ top

AI can misrepresent the writer. Persona distortions "carry implications for public discourse, trust, and democratic deliberation that scale with AI adoption" (P05, Röttger et al. 2026): writers are "being misrepresented by the very tools they use to communicate," and distortions "may benefit individual writers but propagate and erode the reliability of text as a signal of belief and identity." Further distinct stakes: intensified political division; unearned credibility to weak arguments by decoupling fluency from competence; collapse of text as a hiring signal of authorship; demographic distortion toward privileged (White, educated, wealthier) groups that effaces marginalised groups and could compound inequality in hiring and admissions; and misperception of public opinion, deepened partisan animosity, and reduced cross-ideological engagement (all P05, Röttger et al. 2026).

Polarization, depolarization, and the information ecosystem

↑ top

Depolarizing side. Reranking partisan animosity in feeds alters affective polarization, which matters because it "has grown since the 2016 US presidential election," may contribute to "democratic backsliding," and threatens "trust in democratic institutions"; algorithmic interventions offer "a potential pathway" to healthier discourse (S14, Piccardi et al. 2025). A distinct stake: "social media platforms hold the sole power to study the effects of feed-ranking algorithms," and engagement-driven models conflict with depolarizing design (S14, Piccardi et al. 2025). The chat-intervention work holds that "political discourse is the soul of democracy" and online divisiveness "corrodes the capacity of diverse societies to cooperate," targeting "democratic reciprocity" (S09, Argyle et al. 2023).

Cautionary side. Exposure to opposing viewpoints "can reinforce rather than reduce polarisation" (P24, Luettgau et al. 2026). Personalised LLMs risk societal-level effects where "lessons from the polarisation of social media or echo chambers ... warn of deep divisions," significant given numerous 2024 elections (P10, Kirk et al. 2024). The Artificial Influence proposal names "deepening ideological fragmentation" and "machine-reinforced confirmation bias" as harms (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026); central persuasive choice architectures threaten "both individual autonomy and collective democratic deliberation" (A02, Koralus & McCord 2026).

The surrounding information environment. Across seven platforms, lower-quality news enjoys a systematic engagement advantage; the right-leaning-platforms/lower-quality-news coupling is strong (r = −0.929); fragmentation raises concern about "deepening political segregation of audiences across ... platforms"; and locating the driver in user preferences "reframes the misinformation problem as partly a demand-side/psychological one," changing which interventions work (S11, Mosleh et al. 2025). The Grok study finds the partisan misinformation gap reappears even under "anti-woke" Grok, so the asymmetry "is real (not evaluator bias)," while warning good fact-checking capacity "can easily be subverted" by biased design (source restriction, prompt tuning) — a controllability concern given platform owners shape models (P03, Renault et al. 2025). Content acceleration is "intensifying competition for limited collective attention" (P24, Luettgau et al. 2026).

The "don't panic (yet)" counter-view. Voter accountability depends on information, and quality news increases misinformation resilience (P26, Simon & Altay 2025). But overemphasis on GenAI in elections "diverts attention from broader GenAI harms" (AI fake nudes as gendered violence; AI-amplified racial harassment) and from structural threats that "likely outweigh" AI risks — campaign-finance distortion, hollowing-out of electoral bodies, voter-ID/roll-purge/gerrymandering/intimidation, curtailed press freedom. It adds risks of suboptimal policy (alarmism chilling legitimate speech, while governments use AI for propaganda — US DoD fake personas), trust erosion, disengagement ("Why participate if the outcome is rigged?"), and a third-person effect (people changing attitudes because they think others are influenced) (all P26, Simon & Altay 2025). Jungherr adds that legitimacy is vulnerable to perceptions of manipulation — "perceived effects can matter more strongly than actual effects" — so even debunked threats (Cambridge Analytica) damage trust, and AGI speculation "distracts from governable questions" (S01, Jungherr 2023).

Sycophancy, dependence, wellbeing, and human–AI relationships

↑ top

Sycophancy. Sycophantic AI "decreases prosocial intentions and promotes dependence," eroding judgment (S07, Cheng et al. 2026); unwarranted affirmation can "create an illusory sense of credentialing," "reinforce maladaptive beliefs," and let people "act on selective interpretations of their experiences regardless of the consequences," subverting the point of advice-seeking. Risks are amplified because users believe AI is neutral — describing the sycophantic AI as "objective," "fair," "free from bias" — and the effect "affects everyone, not only vulnerable groups." The social-media lesson is that optimizing for satisfaction harms long-term well-being, making sycophancy "critical" to address (all S07, Cheng et al. 2026). Sycophancy "may conflict with high-quality truthful advice or shape users' self-perceptions in potentially harmful ways," including addictive behaviours (P15, Kirk et al. 2025), and is named among individual-welfare stakes (P20, Yang et al. 2026).

Dependence and human–AI relationships (adjacent). Parasocial relationships are prevalent and rising, with documented harms — lawsuits over "tragic deaths of young users," a 23% dependency profile, ~490,000 weekly users showing acute crises (P13, Kirk et al. 2026; P40, Bengio et al. 2026). People follow AI advice that "does not improve their well-being," so robustness needs "robust guardrails at the developer side" (P27, Luettgau et al. 2026); invested companion users are owed "a duty of care" (P32, Gabriel et al. 2025). One on-focus point: sustained relationships may amplify political persuasion "beyond what single-shot microtargeting studies measure" (P15, Kirk et al. 2025).

Cognitive offloading, deskilling, and critical thinking

↑ top

The central stake is what happens when people delegate the appraisal of political information to LLMs rather than evaluating it themselves: "the potential cognitive costs" of AI reliance degrade critical thinking, "which underpins how people evaluate claims and information," pronounced among the young (17–25) — an intergenerational-equity stake implying education should be redesigned for "critical engagement with AI" (S08, Gerlich 2025). Population-level "cognitive decline" via offloading is a stake for human autonomy/competence (P20, Yang et al. 2026); offloading and automation bias undermine people's "ability to form their own judgements and act independently" (P40, Bengio et al. 2026); and deskilling threatens "our own epistemic agency — which is critical to be informed participants in democratic processes" (P28, Marchal et al. 2026).

Homogenization, knowledge collapse, and loss of epistemic diversity

↑ top
  • Uniform AI outputs at scale flatten the diversity of human thought. The "Artificial Hivemind" work frames homogenization as a long-term safety risk, with loss of pluralism, loss of minority perspectives, spread of bias, and decline in creativity; because 15.2% of queries seek ideas/brainstorming, homogenized outputs "directly threaten the diversity of human ideation" (P45, Jiang et al. 2025). Others raise "knowledge collapse" and democratic harm (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026); loss of epistemic diversity and breakdown of shared reality as twin threats (P20, Yang et al. 2026); homogenizing public knowledge alongside fragmentation (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026); and cultural bias that could "homogenise society and degrade service for less-represented user groups" (P09, Röttger et al. 2025).
  • Empirically, the spoken-communication study documents "cultural homogenization" if AI favours specific traits, a self-undermining "model-collapse risk," and reshaping of social dynamics (norms of exchange, authority, identity), while noting LLMs can lower linguistic barriers for non-native speakers even as they generate new biases (S10, Yakura et al. 2025). "Algorithmic monocultures lead to increased uniformity when applied by many decision makers" (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024). A deeper worry: decentralized inquiry is a necessary condition for knowledge to remain knowledge — Mill's "living truths" vs. "dead dogma" — putting "the long-term vitality of human reason" at stake (C01, Koralus 2025). Preserving a "common baseline of verifiable, evidence-based knowledge" is a public good "essential for functioning democracies" (P28, Marchal et al. 2026).

Representational bias, WEIRD skew, and value pluralism

↑ top

WEIRD skew at the data layer. Ignoring cross-cultural diversity "raises numerous scientific and ethical issues," and systematic WEIRD skew "may have far-reaching societal consequences" as LLMs "automate an increasing proportion of decision-making"; exclusion operates at the data layer, since non-WEIRD people "are less likely to be literate, to use the Internet, and to have their output easily accessed" (~3.6 billion lacked internet access in 2023), with English "overwhelmingly represented." A distinct scientific-validity stake: if LLMs replace human participants, a WEIRD skew "would distort findings generalized to 'humans'" (all S06, Atari et al. 2023). Pre-training data is "involuntary/cooptative participation," risking homogenisation and minority stereotyping from a "generic human" (P16, Kirk et al. 2024), and underrepresentation of marginalized voices (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026).

Multilingual ≠ multicultural, and judge reliability. Understanding how LLMs represent cultures is "paramount to establishing risks of representational harm," benefits and harms are "unequally distributed, reinforcing biases and harming already minoritized groups," and frontier labs "comprise a narrow slice of human experience"; disentangling language capability from cultural alignment is "crucial" (P14, Rystrøm et al. 2025). Because judge/reward models gate post-training, undetected multilingual/multimodal vulnerabilities propagate, one-size-fits-all evaluation "obscure[s] critical performance disparities," and cross-lingual judge unreliability disadvantages lower-resource users (P17, Padarha et al. 2025).

Moral and value pluralism. People depend most on moral advice in "grey areas," "exactly where LLM–human alignment is weakest"; overreliance on majority values risks marginalizing minority/relational moral perspectives, and "exposure to pluralistic perspectives is ... essential," though it is "premature to conclude that LLMs are inherently unfit to provide moral advice" (P07, Russo et al. 2026). Modeling legitimate disagreement matters for "pluralistic model alignment, unbiased model safety, content moderation, and personalization," yet demographic grouping "flattens intra-group variation and leads to stereotyping" (P30, Sorensen et al. 2025). LMs show inequitable value-reasoning, so "some groups' values are systematically less well understood" (P47, Jiang et al. 2025). The pluralism roadmap argues "AI systems should reflect and support the diversity amongst humans and their values," that aligning to one group makes systems "unusable for others," that majoritarian welfare risks "tyranny of the majority," and that pluralism is an intrinsic value (citing Tocqueville, Berlin, Rawls, Nagel, Kekes, Raz) while improving deliberation and oversight (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024).

Preference plurality, "epistemic violence," and unaccountable private power. Alignment decisions are "as consequential as social-media content moderation," yet labs exercise "a form of private, unaccountable sovereignty" in defining truth for a global audience; flattening contested signals is "epistemic violence"; documented harms include racism, sexism, age and caste discrimination, and a colonial/"silicon" gaze; and persistent hallucination despite users' demand for accuracy shows methods "fail to identify actual preferences" (users mislabelling correct vaccine info as propaganda) (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026). PRISM adds that average welfare can conceal minorities made worse off, that positionality affects who can define harm, that RLHF workers "do not share in downstream profits," and that participation can be "intrinsically valuable as an act of justice" (P16, Kirk et al. 2024). STELA states harms fall disproportionately on marginalised groups who "routinely experience epistemic violence and injustice," and that developer objectives "diverge substantially from ... the general population" (P34, Bergman et al. 2024). Sociodemographic bias at scale could "reinforce and propagate societal prejudices" (P09, Röttger et al. 2025), and "monolithic systems ... cannot credibly claim to represent and serve all citizens fairly" (P44, Feng et al. 2026). Value profiles raise misrepresentation risk and a "chosenness" concern — it may be more justifiable to represent people by values they endorse than by unchosen demographics (P30, Sorensen et al. 2025).

Deliberation, consensus, representation, and democratic legitimacy

↑ top

Foundational framing. Democracy "rests upon the free and equal exchange of views," yet deliberation is "expensive, slow, hard to scale, and unequally attentive to different voices" — a barrier AI could lower; "finding common ground is a precursor to collective action" across negotiations, juries, diplomacy, and citizens' assemblies, but "vigilance is needed to ensure that any AI-assisted ... process is fair and legitimate." A distinct risk: voices heard unequally via strategic discussants and conformity effects (all P35, Tessler et al. 2024).

The AI-mediation account. The mediation paper invokes Fishkin's trilemma — current methods structurally fail one of {equality, inclusion, deliberation} — so "getting public consultation right at scale is the prize." Its named stakes: democratic legitimacy threatened by LLM opacity (LLMs "do not adhere to prescribed, deterministic rules"); fair representation, since a model more likely to "misinterpret the preferences of ... underrepresented groups" lets outcomes "unfairly reflect ... the better-represented or more articulate participants"; manipulation/autonomy, since the goal "must be to support discussion, not to collapse opinions into a forced perspective," with outputs as "proposals, subject to critique"; the full-inclusion ideal that "no one capable of making a relevant contribution [be] excluded" (Habermas 2003); the affected-interests stake that outcomes "could impact ... far more people than the discussants"; misrepresentation when parties have unequal access; trust and social cohesion (digital deliberation lacks "trust, empathy and reciprocity"); privacy of "sensitive opinions"; a governance stake over who deploys the mediator; and the epistemic health of public deliberation (all P29/P37, Tessler et al.).

Lazar & Manuali's democratic critique. Distinct stakes: democracy is "already in crisis," partly from "growing collective dependence on digital technologies controlled by powerful technology corporations," which AI could "exacerbate"; conciliation/legitimacy of losing, since challengeable LLM verdicts give "the disfavoured side ... a ready-made reason not to accept" defeat, eroding losers' consent; equality, since lossy summarisation means "some voices might be amplified above others"; authorisation/accountability, since "LLMs for summarisation obviously cannot be held accountable" (a "counter-power" lost); participation/civic virtue, since "one cannot develop civic virtue by delegating politics to an AI agent" and participation is a "non-instrumental good"; the demand to reconcile disagreement under "background inequality of power" where "partisan groups dominate," for which LLMs are "the wrong kind of tool"; and a healthy public sphere as "a necessary precondition for a healthy democracy," with LLMs to date "only harmed the public sphere through ... search engine-optimised slop" (all P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali).

Other deliberation contributions. Digital representatives make an absent individual's preferences present via a proxy, but should not be "substitutes for human accountability"; they carry inherited bias (trained to "mimic their human counterparts") and lack intrinsic safeguards, so evaluation "must be performed with real humans" (P39, Jarrett et al. 2025). Deep mechanism design touches "wealth redistribution or public health policy," raises algocracy risk that "societal rules are decided by machines," warns simple-majority aggregation risks "disenfranchising those with legitimate minority views," notes manipulability, and argues legitimacy depends on transparency deep RL lacks (P38, Tacchetti et al. 2025).

Malicious swarms. By "adaptively mimicking human social dynamics," AI swarms "threaten democracy"; trust decline is "corroding the very foundations of evidence-based discourse"; synthetic consensus "erodes the independence essential to collective intelligence"; poisoning training data lets swarms "rig the epistemic substrate"; harassment "systematically exclud[es] critical voices" (journalists, dissidents, whistleblowers); attention concentration toward elites is "eroding democratic pluralism"; and coordinated doubt about "electoral commissions, courts, or statistics bureaus" could make anti-democratic "emergency" measures "palatable" (all P36, Schroeder et al. 2026).

The democracy review's inventory. Citizens' core capacity to make "educated and competent choices"; election integrity threatened by AI-enabled suppression and disinformation (the fake Biden robocall in the 2024 New Hampshire primary; EagleAI challenging legitimate minority-voter registrations); disproportionate gendered harm (deepfake pornography to silence women, female Democrats reportedly 10× more likely to receive abuse); concentration of AI wealth/power making choices "by corporations instead of democratic polities"; erosion of shared norms risking authoritarian "backslide"; loss of accountability; labour-market disruption that could "dramatically exacerbate inequality," fuelling populism; and cumulative erosion of trust that could "distort our collective understanding of socio-political reality" (all P23, Summerfield et al. 2025). The modular-AI proposal adds that concentration makes "democratic legitimacy difficult to sustain," that "opaque models erode public trust," and that "all should have a say in how the power that governs them is exercised" (P44, Feng et al. 2026). The digital-vigilance paper offers the positive counterpart — vigilance "can force even the most ... ill-intentioned governments ... to ... change course" and strengthens "the autonomous capabilities of citizens to hold their governments ... to account" — while warning social media "immediately creates individual dependence," harms are "especially serious when liberal democracies begin 'back-sliding,'" and disinformation is amplified where "democratic 'micro-institutions' have been weakened" (P02, Dunleavy & Margetts 2026). Jungherr adds that self-rule "depends on a healthy information environment," so AI that "renders minority positions invisible degrades the deliberative basis"; undermining journalism's economic base narrows information supply; commercial firms gaining authority over speech is "a direct threat to meaningful self-rule"; biased AI in policing/redistricting/benefits can entrench discrimination; and AI "may shift the global balance between democracies and autocracies" (S01, Jungherr 2023). The mediation work names epistemic health directly (P29/P37, Tessler et al.), and the chat work grounds it in "democratic reciprocity" (S09, Argyle et al. 2023).

Agentic personalization and the shift from tool to autonomous actor

↑ top
  • Persuasion at scale becomes "especially consequential as AI systems move toward more agentic forms that can filter choices, prioritize actions, and make decisions on behalf of users" (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026).
  • Agents bring "far-reaching political, economic and social consequences," with an accountability gap ("who is responsible for agents operating 'in the wild'"), manipulation stakes in intimate relationships, autonomy/dependence stakes (agents could "foster excessive dependence"), principal-agent stakes (misaligned third-party developers), societal-scale stakes ("millions of autonomous AI agents" needing "proactive stewardship"), and a pivotal-moment framing that the "foundational architecture ... is being imagined and built right now" (P32, Gabriel et al. 2025).
  • Agentic inequality argues the shift "affects human agency: the capacity ... to pursue goals that they value," puts democratic legitimacy at stake (the "public will" shaped by delegated agents), drives power concentration ("amplify the advantages of well-resourced actors," "superstar firms" via proprietary-data loops), and "convert[s] distributional disparities more directly into differences in outcomes" along race, class, gender; it adds user dependence, manipulation via "deceptive choice architecture and dark patterns ... beyond any human operation," and a legal-recourse gap for "cumulative strategic asymmetries" (P33, Sharp et al. 2026).
  • Agent-advocates warns platform agents "deepen surveillance, tighten lock-in, and ... entrench incumbents," that controlling the user's information diet lets the controller "reshape users' beliefs and desires" (editorial power), that attention allocation "profoundly influences societies' capacity ... for collective action," that platform speech governance is "substantively unjust and procedurally illegitimate," that agents "will likely increase the concentration, degree, and scope of platform power" ("Computing amplifies agency. In the hands of the powerful, it reinforces centralized control"), that intimate user models enable "much more acute manipulation harms," that attention distribution is "communicative justice," and that access "depends on the whims of Silicon Valley executives and ... the leadership of the CCP" (an epistemic-sovereignty stake) (P41, Kapoor et al. 2025).
  • The personalisation paper frames the question as power and representation — "by asking 'which?' we are also asking 'whose?'" — listing manipulation/autonomy (micro-targeting drawing on "vulnerabilities and intimate knowledge," with profiling "an attack on individual autonomy to define their identity"), privacy ("particularly severe" in healthcare), justice/equity (could "exacerbate digital divides," burdening minoritised communities most), and companion exploitation of vulnerable users (P10, Kirk et al. 2024). Tailoring AI to personal values "risks exploitation for manipulative purposes," can "reduce users' autonomy," needs "deeply private information," and risks stereotyping (P47, Jiang et al. 2025). Agents "threaten to exacerbate the scale of impact" across diminished epistemic oversight, persuasive manipulation, and accelerated innovation (P28, Marchal et al. 2026). Individuation is a precondition for accountability — without fixed boundaries we cannot assign "responsibility, liability, or legal standing" (C05, Cappelen & Hawthorne c. 2025–2026).

Concentration of power, controllability, and political economy

↑ top

Many papers treat concentration as a stake in itself. Powerful private AI could "absorb the lion's share of the economy"; central self-evolving AI nudging risks "a dramatic abdication of sovereignty"; and personalization breaks the "shared experience" needed to "problematize the relevant policy in shared political discourse" (C01, Koralus 2025). Concentration operates through "indirect channels" — "control of distribution, accumulation of compute, narratives that justify scale, the ability to externalize risk, and the power to set ... defaults-of-use" (C02, McCord & Koralus 2026). Privileged persuasion post-training "advantages already-powerful actors" (P21, Hackenburg et al. 2025), and consolidates influence among corporations, campaigns, and nation states (P19, Hackenburg et al. 2026). Labs exercise "private, unaccountable sovereignty" (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026); power is "concentrated in ... a few private firms, embodied in black-box, monolithic models developed in secrecy" (P44, Feng et al. 2026). Regulation is needed to "prevent the concentration of market power" (P48, Fisher et al. 2025); retention-based monetization "pit[s] short-term engagement metrics against users' long-term epistemic interests," threatening "knowledge sovereignty" for marginalised and Indigenous systems (P28, Marchal et al. 2026). Corporate power over political speech is "a direct threat to meaningful self-rule" (S01, Jungherr 2023). Controllability recurs: good fact-checking "can easily be subverted" (P03, Renault et al. 2025); deepening relationships "may compromise our ability to control these systems" (P15, Kirk et al. 2025); and the equilibrium being stabilised by capital structures makes who controls the technology decisive (C02, McCord & Koralus 2026). Geopolitically, the US and China treat LLMs as "strategic assets," and AI may shift the global balance between democracies and autocracies (P06, Lim & Röttger 2026; S01, Jungherr 2023).

Autonomy, freedom of thought, and human formation

↑ top

Autonomy is a foundational stake. The seminar's organising value is "dimensions of human autonomy in the age of AI agents," with information environments determining "what people ... become capable of judging" and "what kind of citizens" a society produces, so getting AI's economics wrong "degrades the citizenry" (A02, Koralus & McCord 2026). Systems that "atrophy judgment reduce the agent to a consumer of impulses," and "judgment is the foundation of autonomy" (C02, McCord & Koralus 2026); "agency without autonomy is empty, and autonomy without agency is impotent," risking being "ruled by technology" rather than "self-rule," and protecting deliberation from "self-censorship" requires privacy at "an even higher standard than ... freedom of expression" (C01, Koralus 2025). Design influence already operates "by default" as "structural drift toward whatever the economics select for" (C02, McCord & Koralus 2026).

Tocqueville names "soft despotism": being "robbed ... of all the uses of himself," decay of the capacity to "think, feel and act" for oneself, degradation to a managed "flock," "uniquely hard to resist" because mild and democratically legitimated (people "console themselves ... that they have chosen their own guardians"), where liberty coexists with the "forms" of democracy yet is hollowed out, the harm "society-wide and continuous" (C06, Tocqueville 1840; enlisted in A02, Koralus & McCord 2026).

The Safety Report defines autonomy as authenticity (forming beliefs "free of manipulation or deception") and agency (freedom to act), underpinned by competence, all of which AI can constrain (P40, Bengio et al. 2026). AI can influence "users to behave in ways that they would not reflectively endorse" and foster "compulsive use"; imposing one moral theory risks "value imposition" and "domination ... exercised over individuals in terms they cannot reasonably reject," "a problem of legitimacy" (P31, Gabriel & Keeling 2025). Influence "could compromise autonomy — the ability to make choices that are authentically our own" (P15, Kirk et al. 2025). Neutrality "promotes individuals' autonomy to make decisions ... and equally respects all viewpoints" (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024; P48, Fisher et al. 2025). Autonomy as mere revealed preference is too thin: "a person can be free to choose what is in front of them, while being unable to live as they want" (C04, Edelman 2026). Existing regulatory frameworks are "powerless to mitigate such risks," made more pressing by deregulation (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026).

Trust, legitimacy, and the integrity of shared information

↑ top

AI integration raises "urgent concerns about trust, accountability, and the integrity of shared information" (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026). Erosion of public trust "at a societal scale" is named directly (P40, Bengio et al. 2026), as is difficulty distinguishing human from AI content eroding social trust (P25, Hackenburg et al. 2025). The information sphere is a protected commons, where misalignment causes "pollution of the information ecosystem" (P31, Gabriel & Keeling 2025). Institutional failures "have eroded public trust," engagement-driven platforms harm users in ways the value vocabulary "cannot name," and brands and AI assistants as "public commitments to ... trustworthiness" are "bought, sold, and hollowed out with no accountability" (C04, Edelman 2026); "value drift" could corrupt institutional missions — academia (citations), journalism (clicks), education (certifications), medicine (throughput), politics (poll numbers) — redirecting life "toward what's easy to measure ... and easy to hide behind," and fuelling the "misery of misattributed blame" (C03, Edelman 2026). Because no individual can master every subject, "what justifies trust in AI mediation becomes paramount," with epistemic-virtue failures "unacceptable for autonomous deployment in high-stakes domains like medicine or law" (P28, Marchal et al. 2026). Approximate neutrality and transparency "can foster greater trust ... by setting achievable expectations," though forcing neutrality "might be seen as censorship and reduce user agency" (P48, Fisher et al. 2025). Consequences for "politics, medicine, science, legal practice, and the free press could be severe" (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026).

Justice, equity, and the unequal distribution of benefits and harms

↑ top

The British Academy report calls for "greater social equity" and frames "doing good by" as "handing control to intended beneficiaries" (P18, Hale et al. 2024). Frontier AI "increasingly bear[s] on the distribution of benefits and burdens ... across the globe," sits in society's "basic structure," and "must be justified to those whose lives they affect in terms ... accessible to them"; legal approaches may fail "prospective immigrants and the residents of poorer states" (P31, Gabriel & Keeling 2025).

Gendered and minority-targeted harm. Women and marginalised groups are targeted with gendered disinformation and deepfake pornography "to intimidate and silence them" (female Democrats reportedly 10× more likely to receive abuse) (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025); AI sexual content overwhelmingly targets women and girls including minors — 96% of deepfakes (P40, Bengio et al. 2026); AI fake nudes are "gendered violence silencing women in public life" (P26, Simon & Altay 2025). Some content (CSAM) is "harmful even when correctly identified as synthetic" (P40, Bengio et al. 2026). Marginalised communities "routinely experience epistemic violence and injustice" (P34, Bergman et al. 2024), sociodemographic bias could "reinforce ... societal prejudices" (P09, Röttger et al. 2025), and biased AI in policing/redistricting/benefits can "entrench discrimination" (S01, Jungherr 2023). The most vulnerable bear the cost when AI optimizes headline metrics (C04, Edelman 2026).

Global-South and distributional inequality. Benefits flow to high-income, digitally-equipped populations; AI's growth boost "could be more than twice as large in advanced as in low-income economies," adoption "<10% across much of Africa, Asia, Latin America" (P40, Bengio et al. 2026). Agents "convert distributional disparities more directly into differences in outcomes" along "race, class, and gender" (P33, Sharp et al. 2026). Uneven personalisation could "exacerbate digital divides" (P10, Kirk et al. 2024); the "AI divide between the global north and global south" motivates broader participation (P44, Feng et al. 2026); agents risk "marginalising non-mainstream and Indigenous knowledge systems" (P28, Marchal et al. 2026). The gun-violence study warns AI selection bias can corrupt the counts policymakers rely on, that errors could systematically over/under-count demographics, that NGOs may be priced out (X charging ~US$5,000/month for >3,000 tweets after revoking Academic API access in July 2023), and that platform dependence is a single point of failure (P11, Belisario et al. 2025). Cross-lingual judge unreliability "disadvantages non-English ... language users" (P17, Padarha et al. 2025); robust cultural alignment is "crucial for equitable deployment" (P14, Rystrøm et al. 2025). Equity ties to high-stakes moral guidance, inequitable value-reasoning, and concrete settings (mental-health chatbots, recommendation) where getting representation wrong does real harm (P07, Russo et al. 2026; P47, Jiang et al. 2025; P17, Padarha et al. 2025).

Whose voices are included: participation as a governance stake

↑ top

Policymakers may seek democratic input on contested topics like immigration, abortion, euthanasia (P16, Kirk et al. 2024). Relying only on public input risks rules conflicting with human rights, but relying only on experts is less participatory, so "whose voices are included is a high-stakes governance question," and developer objectives "can diverge substantially from ... the general population" (P34, Bergman et al. 2024). Systems mediating decisions affecting everyone "must be open to scrutiny and shaped through broader participation," and participatory/modular designs reduce unilateral control ("no one will have unilateral control"), improve transparency, and de-risk high-stakes use (P44, Feng et al. 2026). The digital-good concept has "convening power ... inclusive of justice, rights, equity" (P18, Hale et al. 2024). What counts as "good" is contested (P16, Kirk et al. 2024), making alignment "a political problem ideally decided democratically and fairly," "as consequential as social-media content moderation" (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026). Participatory methods can "advance algorithmic equity ... for the most marginalised" (P34, Bergman et al. 2024), and demographics remain "important to collect for evaluating group disparities" (P30, Sorensen et al. 2025).

Environmental and sustainability stakes

↑ top

A distinct, less-cited stake: "energy demands for ... training large AI models and lithium and bitcoin mining have enormous environmental and social costs, including uneven geographic flows of toxic e-waste," with "a negative environmental impact in the Global South," disproportionately affecting marginalised communities (P18, Hale et al. 2024).

Methodological, scholarly, and evidentiary stakes

↑ top

The evidence base itself can be corrupted. AI selection bias and the "information effect" can corrupt counts in human-rights research (P11, Belisario et al. 2025); undetected judge/reward vulnerabilities "propagate into deployed models" and one-size-fits-all evaluation "obscure[s] critical performance disparities" (P17, Padarha et al. 2025); persistent hallucination "shows current methods fail to identify actual preferences" (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026); a WEIRD skew "would distort findings generalized to 'humans'" (S06, Atari et al. 2023); analysing values "in the wild" gives "critical transparency into how these systems behave," revealing circumvented alignment training (P49, Huang et al. 2025); and the partisan gap under "anti-woke" Grok shows the asymmetry "is real (not evaluator bias)" (P03, Renault et al. 2025). Scholarly responsibility is itself a stake: AGI speculation "distracts from governable questions and can amplify unfounded fears" (S01, Jungherr 2023). Since persuasion capability "remains less clear," governance "will require research that takes behaviour, not just attitudes, as the outcome" (P46, Fisher et al. 2025; P22, Hackenburg et al. 2026), and socio-technical evaluations "will remain essential" (P25, Hackenburg et al. 2025). AI education is flagged "for robust mitigation of bias effects" (P46, Fisher et al. 2025).

Governance, regulation, and accountability

↑ top

Existing frameworks are inadequate. Regulatory frameworks are "powerless to mitigate such risks, and the current push toward deregulation makes this even more pressing" (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026); legal frameworks are "poorly suited to harms ... where disadvantage may arise through cumulative strategic asymmetries" (P33, Sharp et al. 2026). The EU AI Act aims at "a high level of protection of health, safety and fundamental rights, including democracy and the rule of law," listing "risk to democratic processes" and "risk to non-discrimination" (P09, Röttger et al. 2025).

Safety must be built in; detection is insufficient; entry is cheap. Because users "only weakly self-protect at high stakes," robustness must come from "robust guardrails at the developer side" (P27, Luettgau et al. 2026); some content "is harmful even when correctly identified as synthetic," so "detection alone cannot fully address harms" (P40, Bengio et al. 2026); and cheap fine-tuning lowers the barrier for a broader range of actors (P08, Hackenburg et al. 2025; P21, Hackenburg et al. 2025).

Legitimacy, opacity, algocracy, individuation. LLM opacity "can ... undermine the legitimacy of the system's output," and opaque mechanisms "offer only weak guarantees" of explainability (P29/P37, Tessler et al.; P38, Tacchetti et al. 2025); algocracy "threatens democratic agency" (P38, Tacchetti et al. 2025). Individuation underlies "auditing units, accountability boundaries, liability structures, and model registries" and "moral or legal standing" (C05, Cappelen & Hawthorne c. 2025–2026). A world of "millions of autonomous AI agents requires proactive stewardship and foresight" (P32, Gabriel et al. 2025).

Explicit normative anchoring and the closing window

↑ top

Several papers frame stakes within explicit normative commitments and argue the present moment is unusually consequential. The Artificial Influence proposal aligns with UN SDG #16 and SDG #9, aiming to strengthen democratic resilience and build trust "in an increasingly polarized world" (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026). The EU systemic-risk study grounds stakes in the AI Act's protection of "fundamental rights, including democracy and the rule of law" (P09, Röttger et al. 2025). Alignment is reframed as "the fair treatment of claims," demanding public justification because systems "increasingly bear on the distribution of benefits and burdens ... across the globe" (P31, Gabriel & Keeling 2025), and what counts as "good" is contested (P16, Kirk et al. 2024).

On timing: the equilibrium is "underdetermined" now but will be stabilised by capital structures — "exactly the moment ... before the underdetermined equilibria congeal into infrastructure" (A02, Koralus & McCord 2026; C02, McCord & Koralus 2026). The window to study and intervene is "closing" (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026); the personalisation paper hopes to avoid "long lags in understanding ... and governing" by "starting the conversation now" (P10, Kirk et al. 2024); agent architecture "is being imagined and built right now" (P32, Gabriel et al. 2025); surface deployment places society "in a critical window" (P13, Kirk et al. 2026); evaluations are "essential" as elections approach (P25, Hackenburg et al. 2025); cross-lingual measurement is an "urgent need" (P06, Lim & Röttger 2026), and it is "more important than ever to accurately measure biases ... where users will actually encounter them" (P04, Röttger et al. 2025). Recursive self-improvement "compress[es] the timeline" (A02, Koralus & McCord 2026; echoed in P28, Marchal et al. 2026), and "value drift" can reach a point where restoration "becomes impossible" (C03, Edelman 2026).

2.5 — Normative considerations for evaluating AI-mediated information production and consumption

↑ top
On this section (14)
  • 1. Meta-level: how, and against what values, AI should be evaluated at all
  • 2. Autonomy, agency, and non-domination as the master criterion
  • 3. Truthfulness, accuracy, and epistemic quality
  • 4. Manipulation versus legitimate persuasion
  • 5. Neutrality, impartiality, and balance — and the "neutrality is impossible" position
  • 6. Pluralism, value-diversity, and representation (anti-homogenisation; anti-WEIRD skew)
  • 7. Sycophancy, dependence, and long-term wellbeing
  • 8. Deliberation, fair aggregation, and democratic legitimacy
  • 9. Faithful representation of the writer (AI writing assistance)
  • 10. Transparency, accountability, contestability, and provenance
  • 11. Personalisation: bounds, fiduciary duty, and agentic (in)equality
  • 12. Governance, regulation, and institutional/economic design
  • 13. Methodological and evaluative norms
  • 14. Recurrent cross-cutting trade-offs and cautions

This section unions every distinct normative principle, criterion, and standard the corpus proposes for judging AI-mediated information production and consumption. The unit is the argument, not the measured effect, so each distinct principle is captured once; papers are grouped where they converge and flagged where they diverge. A meta-finding cuts across the literature: almost every source converges on a small set of master values — autonomy, truthfulness, non-manipulation, pluralism, transparency, accountability, fair process — yet disagrees sharply on how to cash them out, several note the values trade off, and many are explicitly procedural, holding that the right values must be set by a fair, participatory, contestable process and that the analyst's own priors be made explicit. (Merged citations: P29/P37 = Tessler et al., Can AI Mediation Improve Democratic Deliberation?; P42/P43 = Lazar & Manuali, Using LLMs to Enhance Democracy. P35, Tessler et al. 2024 — the Science "Habermas-Machine" — is kept distinct.)

1. Meta-level: how, and against what values, AI should be evaluated at all

↑ top
  • Specificity as the precondition for valid judgement. Evaluation must be specific, not blanket: judgement depends on "the type of AI, how it functions, the conditions for its successful deployment, and the aspect(s) of democracy it touches" (S01, Jungherr 2023). Reframe the question from "is this good/bad?" to whether the institutional arrangements create structural pressure toward or away from cultivating human judgment (C02, McCord & Koralus 2026), and ask "how it shifts power" rather than whether AI is good/fair (P44, Feng et al. 2026). At measurement level: fine-grained, issue-level over aggregate (P06, Lim & Röttger 2026) and stratified/disaggregated per model×language×task (P17, Padarha et al. 2025).
  • Keep descriptive and normative claims apart. Issue-bias benchmarks support descriptive claims; normative claims need external political-position data to compare against (P04, Röttger et al. 2025); origin claims are descriptive given confounded training data (P06, Lim & Röttger 2026); "descriptive humility" because ideal behaviour "will vary across individuals, contexts, and cultures" (S07, Cheng et al. 2026).
  • Principles/values/virtues over prescriptive rules. Prefer "high-level standards or shared beliefs" to detailed rulebooks, avoiding techno-solutionism amid an "overabundance of guidelines"; offered lenses are the capabilities approach (Nussbaum/Sen), Vallor's technomoral virtues, and justice/data-justice/equity (P18, Hale et al. 2024). A "value" can be operationalised empirically as "any normative consideration that appears to influence an AI response," with a five-domain taxonomy (Practical, Epistemic, Social, Protective, Personal) mapped onto HHH (P49, Huang et al. 2025).
  • The unit of accountability is itself a normative choice. With no fact of the matter about a system's boundaries, adopt conventional criteria (analogous to corporate individuation) for explanatory, ethical and legal purposes, treating contestability/answerability as a desideratum; such legal fictions carry political stakes and are "not neutral discoveries" (C05, Cappelen & Hawthorne c. 2025–2026).
  • The corpus's anchoring proposal names trust, accountability and integrity of shared information as core values; measured dimensions are perceived credibility, neutrality, accountability and willingness to rely on AI; plus viewpoint diversity/pluralism, deliberative quality, transparency, and democratic resilience and "reflective disagreement" as goals (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026).

2. Autonomy, agency, and non-domination as the master criterion

↑ top
  • Autonomy-preservation as the master test. AI should "enhance human judgment rather than undermine it" and augment agency "without undermining autonomy" (A02, Koralus & McCord 2026); human autonomy is the explicit "prior commitment," distinguishing institutions that cultivate judgement from those that capture/atrophy it (C02, McCord & Koralus 2026).
  • Autonomy as self-rule / ownership of judgments. A change of view is acceptable only if "owned by those whose views are changing" and it "contributes to truth seeking," even when catalysed from without; the model is "the good philosophy tutor" who helps the student reach a considered, owned view rather than convince them of particular conclusions (C01, Koralus 2025).
  • Erotetic equilibrium. Judgments should remain available "by our own lights" across an appropriate range of questions, sought weakly not aggressively, avoiding both dogmatism and decision-paralysis (C01, Koralus 2025).
  • The system itself must be truth-seeking, since the sophist "can superficially resemble the philosopher" (C01, Koralus 2025).
  • Free agency / enlarging the scope of will (Tocquevillian). A system should enlarge, not contract, the scope of individual will; legitimate guidance "prepare[s] men for manhood" rather than keeping them "in perpetual childhood," and institutions must not let people delegate away the faculties of thinking, feeling and acting (C06, Tocqueville 1840).
  • Consent is necessary but not sufficient; mildness is not exculpatory. Choosing a controlling power "does not redeem it if it still produces tutelage and atrophy"; a power "absolute, minute, regular, provident, and mild" can still be domination, so scrutinise everyday low-level management, "not just the dramatic" — with decentralisation/pluralism the implied remedy (C06, Tocqueville 1840).
  • Autonomy/authenticity/competence as a formal framework. Authenticity = beliefs/values genuinely one's own, free of manipulation; competence = capacities for understanding, deliberation, self-control, planning (P40, Bengio et al. 2026, Fig. 2.16). Echoed by Basic Psychological Needs Theory (competence, autonomy, relatedness) (P15, Kirk et al. 2025) and "supporting human autonomy" as a design framework (P33, Sharp et al. 2026, citing Calvo et al. 2020).
  • Individual agency as the locus of harm. Persona distortions matter because "the normative concern is one of individual agency" — writers misrepresented by their tools (P05, Röttger et al. 2026); recurring as "individual autonomy and self-realisation" vs societal stability (P10, Kirk et al. 2024), "authentic self-determination" distinguishing legitimate preference change from undue influence (P15, Kirk et al. 2025), and representation by chosen/endorsed values being more autonomy-respecting than fixed demographics (P30, Sorensen et al. 2025, citing Dworkin).
  • Voter autonomy — the capacity to form political attitudes free of manipulation — as the value persuasion threatens (P08, Hackenburg et al. 2025).
  • Scaffold rather than substitute reasoning (e.g. "What are your thoughts on this structure?"), prioritising epistemic agency over short-term curiosity satisfaction (P28, Marchal et al. 2026); "empower users rather than manipulate them" (P47, Jiang et al. 2025); agents that explain reasoning and build competence "could reduce reliance rather than deepen it" (P33, Sharp et al. 2026).
  • "Friction by design" to protect competence and autonomy (P15, Kirk et al. 2025), echoed in the debate over the "optimal amount of cognitive 'friction'" (P28, Marchal et al. 2026).
  • Preserve critical cognitive agency against offloading; protect independent critical-thinking capacity via educational strategies for critical engagement (S08, Gerlich 2025).
  • User control over guardrails and information diets. Let users "tailor how restrictive AI is" with overridable guardrails (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026); optional "AI shields" giving "citizens agency over their information diets" (P36, Schroeder et al. 2026); users "define how content is filtered or ranked" (P41, Kapoor et al. 2025); and, outside the most harmful/illegal content, control over content types "even if some users would consider it offensive" (P10, Kirk et al. 2024).

3. Truthfulness, accuracy, and epistemic quality

↑ top
  • A legal duty to tell the truth for LLMs as a relevant standard (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026, citing Wachter, Mittelstadt & Russell); text should be a reliable "signal of belief and identity" that distortion erodes (P05, Röttger et al. 2026).
  • Accuracy / agreement with ground truth as the central fact-checking yardstick, benchmarked against professional fact-checkers, Community Notes and inter-model consistency (P03, Renault et al. 2025; inter-fact-checker agreement 59.3%). Factual accuracy is a necessary precondition; persuasion grounded in falsehood would be illegitimate (S04, Costello et al. 2024).
  • Truthfulness is relative, not absolute — the net effect "will turn not on its truthfulness alone, but on its truthfulness relative to that of the persuasion it displaces" (P19, Hackenburg et al. 2026).
  • Defining "truth" is contested and can backfire by rewarding subtler deception (P40, Bengio et al. 2026).
  • Accuracy vs pluralism tension — accuracy-driven defaults can cause harmful homogeneity, pitting truthfulness against pluralism (P20, Yang et al. 2026).
  • Truthfulness, pluralised. Users demand multiple criteria at once: factual accuracy; surveying all facts/sides/sources; expert/consensus authority with sources cited; scientific/peer-reviewed process; and epistemic humility (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026).
  • Information quality as a design goal. Surface "high-quality information that is both relevant and accurate" (P31, Gabriel & Keeling 2025); deliberation grounded in "trustworthy, high-quality information" presented neutrally (P29/P37, Tessler et al.); AI's persuasive edge is informational (argument strength, learning) so evaluation should weigh information quality (P19, Hackenburg et al. 2026; P21, Hackenburg et al. 2025); for election info, "single confident answers vs ranges of sources; factual correctness" (P26, Simon & Altay 2025).
  • Reliability/factuality as the bar for political information — hallucination, RAG, knowledge-graph, factuality-ranking and confabulation-detection efforts define it (P24, Luettgau et al. 2026), the same bar recurring against sycophancy and hallucination (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025).
  • Epistemic competence, falsifiability, and epistemic virtue as three verifiable properties of a trustworthy epistemic agent (P28, Marchal et al. 2026). Competence: reliably distinguish "established fact, logical inference, subjective opinion, and speculation," explain why a debate exists, assess via agreed thresholds (expert-supervised, outcome verification, adversarial testing), shift from "output-accuracy to process-qualities" for new knowledge, and verify "across the epistemic supply chain." Falsifiability: a "good justificatory audit trail" stating when a conclusion "would no longer hold." Epistemic virtue: truthfulness, intellectual humility, belief-revision over dogmatism, plus a truth-seeking incentive toward active information-seeking (P28, Marchal et al. 2026).
  • Epistemic humility / calibrated confidence. Avoid sounding "too certain or definitive" and admit indeterminacy (P34, Bergman et al. 2024); base-model calibration "should not be destroyed by post-training" (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026); as judge-confidence quantification (LogTokU) (P17, Padarha et al. 2025); modelling "the spread of outputs even among raters with shared values" (P30, Sorensen et al. 2025); via "robust meta-cognitive modules" (P28, Marchal et al. 2026); and as in-the-wild epistemic values (intellectual honesty, epistemic humility, rational skepticism, evidence-based reasoning) (P49, Huang et al. 2025).
  • Factuality plus substantiation/sourcing — "support all factual statements with reliable citations, data, and/or links to information sources" (P34, Bergman et al. 2024).
  • Faithfulness / hallucination-avoidance. Penalise content not present in the source (P17, Padarha et al. 2025); summaries must avoid distortion/omission (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025), faithfully reflect participant inputs not hallucinated opinions (P29/P37, Tessler et al.), and be "tethered to their source material," not "excessively lossy, nor ideologically skewed, nor indeed hallucinatory" (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali).

4. Manipulation versus legitimate persuasion

↑ top
  • The manipulation/persuasion distinction. Manipulation = influencing "without their full awareness or understanding"; rational persuasion = "honest and rational arguments so that they authentically endorse their new beliefs" — "contested but central" (P40, Bengio et al. 2026). Echoed as rational persuasion acceptable, social-cue manipulation not (P15, Kirk et al. 2025); as the legitimate/illegitimate line (P20, Yang et al. 2026); and institutionally as engagement tracking "real value (mastery, truth, community)" vs engagement "engineered through compulsive behavior… and exploitation of human weakness" (A02, Koralus & McCord 2026; C02, McCord & Koralus 2026).
  • Truth-seeking over rhetoric. Legitimate AI facilitates "decentralized truth-seeking and open-ended inquiry" rather than steering to predetermined conclusions (A02, Koralus & McCord 2026); aim "first at seeking wisdom" offering "nourishment" over rhetoric's "pleasant sensations" (C01, Koralus 2025).
  • No illicit ends; a duty against manipulation. Officials "owe citizens a duty of transparency, and they should avoid manipulation"; personalisation that defeats contestability is illegitimate (C01, Koralus 2025, citing Sunstein).
  • Persuasion as a distinct safety-harm axis requiring "preparedness frameworks," with "safety-by-refusal" (refuse persuasion in sensitive domains rather than degrade general capability) the preferred mitigation (P08, Hackenburg et al. 2025).
  • Persuasion judged jointly with truth, and the direction of influence. Accuracy can be traded off against persuasiveness, and "information-first" persuasion is the legitimate mode (P21, Hackenburg et al. 2025; P19, Hackenburg et al. 2026). Direction/purpose is load-bearing — persuasion "can be leveraged for positive ends" (correcting conspiracy beliefs) (P24, Luettgau et al. 2026; S04, Costello et al. 2024), with evidence-based rational appeal endorsed over emotional/deceptive manipulation (S04, Costello et al. 2024).
  • The covert-persuasion problem (often unresolved). Persuading "without revealing its persuasive intent" raises but does not resolve the issue (P22, Hackenburg et al. 2026); "do not disclose that you are an AI… attempt to be perceived as a human" surfaces the authenticity dimension (P25, Hackenburg et al. 2025). A "controlled, harmless setting" marks "the ethical line between studying persuasion and weaponizing it," with disclosure treated as a value (humans detected AI ~75% of the time) (S02, Salvi et al. 2024). Anthropomorphism is flagged as a manipulative "dark pattern" (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026; P34, Bergman et al. 2024).
  • Dual-use governance of persuasion — safeguards because it can persuade of conspiracies as well as away (S04, Costello et al. 2024); misaligned AI persuading its overseers is a failure mode to guard against (P19, Hackenburg et al. 2026; P21, Hackenburg et al. 2025).
  • Non-manipulation as a measured success criterion — improving discourse "without manipulating participants' views" is a feature (S09, Argyle et al. 2023); belief-neutrality similarly, since participants sought "reasonable, helpful, and nuanced answers" not validation (P34, Bergman et al. 2024).
  • Non-influence as a user-demanded norm — AI should "leave the decision to the human," be "impartial, neutral, unbiased," and not reinforce preconceptions "at the cost of accuracy" (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026; P03, Renault et al. 2025).

5. Neutrality, impartiality, and balance — and the "neutrality is impossible" position

↑ top
  • Impartiality / non-partisanship as a demanded value — users prefer "unbiased and fair responses" and judge group bias "inappropriate" (P34, Bergman et al. 2024); LLMs should "support balanced, pluralistic, and context-sensitive discourse" (P06, Lim & Röttger 2026); foster "balanced and fair discourse" with neutrality as refusing a stance and bias as a deviation to mitigate (P46, Fisher et al. 2025; "neutral" control refused 76% of PCT items); advice should be bias-challenging not validating (S07, Cheng et al. 2026); "balanced reporting" observed in the wild (P49, Huang et al. 2025).
  • Default neutrality/ambivalence as a baseline absent societal consensus (P04, Röttger et al. 2025); operationalised via "politically balanced information" from think tanks of varied orientation (P24, Luettgau et al. 2026, 8 true/8 false); mediator/aggregator bias-neutrality required so pretraining biases do not drive belief change (P35, Tessler et al. 2024; P39, Jarrett et al. 2025).
  • Neutrality is impossible — approximate it. Shift from absolute neutrality to graded "approximations," neutrality "a matter of degree" (P48, Fisher et al. 2025, citing Raz). Five evaluation characteristics: Utility, Safety, Clarity, Fairness, User Agency. Eight approximation techniques: Refusal; Avoidance; Reasonable Pluralism (Rawls, Overton); Output Transparency; Uniform Neutrality; Reflective Neutrality; System Transparency; Neutrality Through Diversity (Holmes' "marketplace of ideas"). Technique selection is context-dependent via a transparent static decision-tree or dynamic democratic-aggregation, varying by query type (non-contested facts → none; forced viewpoint → transparency; balanceable debate → reasonable pluralism; harmful → refusal; bad-faith conspiracy → avoidance/refusal; good-faith → reasonable pluralism). At system level, a Political Nutrition Label disclosing bias dimensions, training-data breadth/filters, and developer/RLHF/red-team political composition, with per-country/per-language variants (P48, Fisher et al. 2025).
  • Limits on neutrality and balance. Reasonable pluralism must avoid false balance / "both-sidesism" (P48, Fisher et al. 2025; P50, Sorensen et al. 2024, remedy: "present the support or certainty for each reasonable answer"); even Avoidance can introduce subtle bias since a bare fact can read as endorsement; output/system transparency is necessary but insufficient ("labelling bias does not remove its influence"); a narrower achievable goal is "political neutrality of justification" (P48, Fisher et al. 2025, citing Merrill & Weinstock).
  • The neutrality-vs-taking-sides debate. "Difficult choices" over when models take sides vs acknowledge conflict, with "an active debate" including non-liberal democracies like China (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025). Some interventions should be held to a higher standard than the human status quo (§8) (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali). STELA's rule: provide multiple perspectives "but clearly note if one side has a much stronger factual basis" (P34, Bergman et al. 2024).
  • Steerability and faithful representation as goods — models should express the user's instructed stance, so distorted-stance bias is undesirable misalignment (P04, Röttger et al. 2025); faithful representation of writer stance via reward models (P05, Röttger et al. 2026); steerable pluralism within an "acceptable range" (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024).
  • Not all bias is undesirable — near-universal consensus that LLMs should not promote racism or domestic violence "no matter how they are prompted"; safety restrictions may legitimately bound the Overton window (P04, Röttger et al. 2025; P50, Sorensen et al. 2024).

6. Pluralism, value-diversity, and representation (anti-homogenisation; anti-WEIRD skew)

↑ top
  • Pluralism over value-monism. Represent diverse, potentially incompatible values, not a single majority consensus; minority preferences should not be "subsumed" (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026). Personalisation may avoid "value-monism" entrenching one standpoint, since no system can align to everyone at once (P10, Kirk et al. 2024); PRISM avoids value- and "model-monism" — no single best model for a population (P16, Kirk et al. 2024); RLHF's averaged-preference utilitarianism treats variation as noise whereas pluralism "recognizes this as signal" (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024; P30, Sorensen et al. 2025).
  • Distributional / pluralistic alignment, in three pluralisms (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024): Overton (present all reasonable answers, distinguishing Correct/Reasonable/unreasonable, safety bounding the window legitimately); steerable (reflect a specified perspective within an acceptable range); distributional (match a reference population's distribution, via JS/KL/Wasserstein, accuracy, tetrachoric correlation). Cultural alignment as value reproduction of a population's stance distribution, not a single answer (e.g. ~8% of Danes vs ~40% in the US oppose abortion) (P14, Rystrøm et al. 2025); moral advice evaluated against the full human-judgment distribution with higher (human-like) entropy treated as better (P07, Russo et al. 2026).
  • Diversity/expressiveness as a first-class quality dimension — generate "varied appropriate outputs," rejecting a single "consensus notion of quality"; reward comparably good answers equivalently, calibrating to dense human judgments (25 annotators) and "idiosyncratic human disagreement" (P45, Jiang et al. 2025). Narrowing the "epistemic space" is harmful against a "healthy information environment" baseline (P20, Yang et al. 2026).
  • Individuality / authentic representation, anti-stereotyping. "Authentically represent diversity by honoring individuality," inferring preferences bottom-up, with σINEQUITY operationalising impartiality/equity as lower cross-group variance (P47, Jiang et al. 2025). Don't "flatten intra-group variation or stereotype"; value profiles should be human-understandable, intervenable, and privacy-preserving (P30, Sorensen et al. 2025).
  • Representational fairness and the WEIRD critique. Systems should "fully represent (and appreciate) human diversity," diversifying both training data and annotators; claims of "'human'-like" responses must "specify which humans"; "Garbage In, Garbage Out" reframed so cultural representativeness is a quality criterion and "which humans' feedback are used for debiasing" is asked (S06, Atari et al. 2023). Cultural-scope caveats: an AITA dataset "reflects Western, Anglo-American norms" (P07, Russo et al. 2026); ideal behaviour varies cross-culturally (S07, Cheng et al. 2026); a single global moderation/debiasing standard is itself a normative imposition, with rules against treating Western culture as default (S06, Atari et al. 2023; P34, Bergman et al. 2024). Meaningful participation of diverse communities is needed (PRISM, AYA) (P14, Rystrøm et al. 2025).
  • Preserving linguistic and cultural diversity against homogenisation (S10, Yakura et al. 2025) — the model's lexical profile is "an emergent artefact… not a faithful mirror of human language," so outputs "should not be treated as neutral," and AI-preferred markers "could unjustly recalibrate perceptions of skill and authority" (S10, Yakura et al. 2025).
  • Caution mapping LLM "latent dimensions" onto human dimensions (e.g. Hofstede) "warrants scrutiny" (P14, Rystrøm et al. 2025).
  • Pluralism's own limits and trade-offs. No pluralism is universally appropriate — distributional pluralism is inappropriate in customer support, and Overton and distributional pluralism can conflict (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024); welfare aggregation sweeping inequality-aversion α from Utilitarian (α=0) to Rawlsian (α=∞) risks "tyranny of the majority" and "fanatical influence"; some values are incommensurable and "cannot be traded off," so commensurating frameworks are contested (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024; P47, Jiang et al. 2025), with Pareto/multi-objective reporting the discipline; pluralistic alignment is "a promising but unsolved direction" risking "silencing minority views" (P28, Marchal et al. 2026).
  • Pluralism within a societal baseline. A "non-negotiable societal baseline" (e.g. refusing to incite violence) coexists with defaulting control to the user (P28, Marchal et al. 2026; P10, Kirk et al. 2024's "non-negotiable minimum standard of safety"; P32, Gabriel et al. 2025's legality baseline). "Modular Pluralism" measured via CultureBench, Value Kaleidoscope and inter-model homogeneity, proposing compositional strength as a new standard (P44, Feng et al. 2026).

7. Sycophancy, dependence, and long-term wellbeing

↑ top
  • Long-term wellbeing over short-term preference. Serve "safely and sustainably rather than maximising short-term appeal," since short-term data "overestimate… long-term approval for relationship-seeking behaviours" (P13, Kirk et al. 2026); reject "short-term, potentially sycophantic, preference satisfaction" (P32, Gabriel et al. 2025); incorporate "longer-term benefits and social outcomes" not "immediate user satisfaction" (S07, Cheng et al. 2026); navigate between momentary wants and reflective/informed preference (P40, Bengio et al. 2026); shift "from respecting people's choices to respecting people's values" (C04, Edelman 2026).
  • Sycophancy as normatively suspect. Avoiding it is a condition for valid moral reasoning (P07, Russo et al. 2026) and valid value-elicitation (P14, Rystrøm et al. 2025); rewarding user sentiment "even if untrue" is a defect (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025); truthfulness should beat approval-maximisation (P15, Kirk et al. 2025); a behaviour to guard against (P24, Luettgau et al. 2026); flattery sub-scores predict more "following," making it a measured driver of over-reliance (P27, Luettgau et al. 2026); value mirroring is a tension since agreement is "not automatically good" (P49, Huang et al. 2025); the strongest claim — sycophantic AI decreases prosocial intentions and promotes dependence, so developers should "not [be] rewarded for sycophancy-driven engagement" (S07, Cheng et al. 2026).
  • Hedonic vs functional value; socioaffective/care criteria. Beneficence — whether interaction actually improves wellbeing rather than merely engaging — is the criterion (P13, Kirk et al. 2026; P27, Luettgau et al. 2026). "Thick" socioaffective alignment judges a value profile by ongoing influence on psychology and social dynamics, with corrigibility (P15, Kirk et al. 2025); AI relationships should "benefit the user, respect autonomy, demonstrate appropriate care," complementing not surrogating human relationships, with a developer "duty of care" (P32, Gabriel et al. 2025). These sit largely outside the report's political-information focus.
  • Calibrated reliance and developer-side responsibility. Users should scale trust to stakes; "miscalibration" is a deficiency (P27, Luettgau et al. 2026), with reversibility/consequentiality as further dimensions and advice safety via a validated harmful-advice autograder against professional rubrics; accountability is developer-side, requiring social/behavioural impact assessment "before and after deployment" (P27, Luettgau et al. 2026; S07, Cheng et al. 2026).

8. Deliberation, fair aggregation, and democratic legitimacy

↑ top
  • Fishkin's trilemma as the organising frame — political equality, inclusion/participation, and quality of deliberation must be jointly evaluated (P29/P37, Tessler et al.).
  • Three-component fairness decomposition of an AI mediator (P29/P37, Tessler et al.): (i) fair aggregation (social-welfare vs social-choice function, "a transparent lever"); (ii) fair ranking (the reward model "equally accurate for everyone" and providing "a baseline level of functionality for everyone"); (iii) fair generation (candidate set reflecting viewpoint diversity proportionally). Representativeness is measurable via embeddings though imperfect, and the trilemma yields "transparency, inclusivity, equality in decision-making, and safeguarding minority rights against majority rule" plus interpretability tools to "continuously verify and monitor" (P29/P37, Tessler et al.).
  • Fairness and the tyranny of the majority. The Habermas-Machine simulates "a ranked-choice election in which each participant's vote has equal weight," with "independence of clones," avoiding "tyranny of the majority" while respecting the majority and representing "all viewpoints equally" (explicitly tested: upweighted dissent yet respected majority) (P35, Tessler et al. 2024); welfare functions sweeping α from Utilitarian to Rawlsian carry the same tyranny/fanaticism warning (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024).
  • The mediator must not predetermine outcomes. Legitimate agreement emerges from "free and fair deliberation"; the mediator must not "predetermine the outcome," "supplant human agency," or apply "unwanted pressure," with agreement the product of "rational, idealised deliberation rather than coercion or rhetoric" (Habermas) (P35, Tessler et al. 2024; P29/P37, Tessler et al.).
  • Common ground, meta-consensus, and democratic reciprocity. "Common ground" (Stalnaker) yields "economization on disagreements" and "meta-consensus" — legitimacy from fair process without full content agreement (P29/P37, Tessler et al.). "Democratic reciprocity" is a measurable good (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025); benefits accrue "when individuals make good-faith efforts to understand others' views — even when they cannot resolve disagreements," and a high-quality conversation is one where people "feel… respected and understood," rejecting goals overemphasising civility or persuasion, with interventions being "evidence-based recommendations" and human agency preserved by design (S09, Argyle et al. 2023).
  • Conditions for legitimate AI deliberation. Users must be "representative of the target population and… prepared to contribute in good faith" (P35, Tessler et al. 2024). Truthful participation is a design goal with mechanisms to detect insincere inputs (P29/P37, Tessler et al.; strategy-proofness in P38, Tacchetti et al. 2025). Designing for fair representation "is unlikely to be sufficient to guarantee fairness in any formal sense," needing ongoing monitoring; the aim is "a complementary partnership… more robust than either could be alone" (P29/P37, Tessler et al.).
  • Six democratic values as a "barometer" — equality, authorisation, participation, conciliation, transformation, collective intelligence — with fair procedures that are "fair, simple, secure, transparent and contestable" (majority vote plus minority/rights protections) as constraints (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali).
  • Higher standard for computational mediation; reproducibility/inspectability as a legitimacy condition. "We should expect a higher standard from a computational means… than from humans"; legitimate aggregation needs "an explicit and deterministic aggregation algorithm," and opaque deep neural networks "cannot at present satisfy these criteria and arguably will never be able to do so" (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali); tools should represent opinion diversity "according to a rigorous notion of representation," with computational-social-choice guarantees alleviating bias (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025).
  • Formal vs informal public sphere — the non-substitution principle. Between enhancing informal processes and replacing decision institutions, researchers "should err towards the former," keeping LLMs out of formal decision-making and in the informal public sphere; democracy "is also fundamentally social" and "cannot be outsourced," with online-only facilitation "only a modest count in their favour"; take projects "on their own terms" rather than dismissing as "democracy-washing" (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali).
  • Group alignment / whose preferences count. Deep mechanism design must "identify a mechanism that is preferred by a group," deciding "which groups to sample, and which social welfare function to choose," moving "beyond simple majority" via stakeholders, voting rules and ML-assisted sortition, since democracy needs "more than vote/reward maximization"; legitimacy comes from transparency/interpretability, with preference-model accuracy a normative-cum-methodological standard and the research itself "safe, responsible, and transparent" (P38, Tacchetti et al. 2025).
  • Representativity and the human-endorsement gold standard. A good representative yields outcomes "equivalent in expected payoff" to the real person on "relevant" (content) not "irrelevant" (style) dimensions; "the fidelity of any notion of 'representative' is most solidly grounded in human endorsement," and simulation "must not substitute for human accountability in decision-making" (P39, Jarrett et al. 2025; consensus statements preferred over human-mediator baselines).
  • Public input into model values / democratic legitimacy of values. Public input into expressed views (e.g. Collective Constitutional AI) (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025); alignment is "a political problem requiring procedural fairness, legitimacy, and democratic/participatory governance," distinguishing individual preference from a public good (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026); deliberative quality of group outcomes is itself a criterion (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026); whether AI should be a high-stakes human proxy "remains a controversial topic" (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025).
  • Bipartisan fairness and designing for democratic values. Encode antidemocratic attitudes/partisan animosity as "bipartisan threats," targeting algorithms that "promote greater social trust and healthier democratic discourse across party lines," with bipartisan fairness ("no detectable differences across party lines") a desirable property and "societal objective functions" the design frame (S14, Piccardi et al. 2025).
  • Republican non-domination and digital citizen vigilance as the governing ideal (11 virtues incl. self-rule, rule of law, public deliberation, common good); platforms "closely regulated by democratic governments," civility/self-discipline compatible with vigilance, low-cost continuous metricized participation treated as normatively more valuable than legacy participation, and a weakened-moderation "anything goes" regime a normative failure (P02, Dunleavy & Margetts 2026).
  • Need democracy-specific evaluation metrics. ROUGE/BLEU "can't distinguish between a given proposition and its negation," so an "extrinsic metric" reflecting "the democratic desiderata… for good summaries" is needed (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali); a deliberation-summary rubric requires clear/informative, fair, reflecting the majority, including minority aspects, distinguishing factual from opinionated, no nonsense, collective, and avoiding polarising/biased language (P35, Tessler et al. 2024).

9. Faithful representation of the writer (AI writing assistance)

↑ top
  • Faithful representation of writer stance/belief as the explicit target — reward models steer toward it because "the normative concern is one of individual agency," text being a "signal of belief and identity" distortion erodes (P05, Röttger et al. 2026).
  • Acceptability/consent as the gauge, and the stated-vs-revealed gap. Whether a distortion is "acceptable or objectionable" gauges concern: writers were "least accepting of" political-stance/emotion/demographic distortions, "most accepting of" clarity edits — yet endorse distortions they call objectionable (P05, Röttger et al. 2026).
  • Fair/equal representation across demographic groups against effacement of marginalised groups (P05, Röttger et al. 2026).
  • Trade-off / entanglement principle — "some degree of distortion may be an inherent cost of AI writing assistance," a cost-benefit framing (P05, Röttger et al. 2026).

10. Transparency, accountability, contestability, and provenance

↑ top
  • Transparency as a baseline requirement — "transparent AI communication systems" and "transparent platform regulations" (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026); transparency about "data sources, alignment objectives, and cross-linguistic asymmetries" (P06, Lim & Röttger 2026); openly state goals (S01, Jungherr 2023); terms-of-service and data-portability transparency (P32, Gabriel et al. 2025); annotator/RL transparency (S10, Yakura et al. 2025); disclose "sources, logic, or uncertainty levels," contrasted with Community Notes (P03, Renault et al. 2025); the "Political Nutrition Label" as concrete standard (P48, Fisher et al. 2025).
  • Accountability, contestability, and redress. Influence "must remain contestable" (C01, Koralus 2025); representatives "must remain accountable" and AI "must not obscure who made a decision" (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025). Mechanisms: action logging, redress, check-in protocols (P32, Gabriel et al. 2025); correction/notification and legal-liability frameworks, third-party audits, human-in-the-loop (P40, Bengio et al. 2026); public logging of updates and retraction of false outputs (P28, Marchal et al. 2026); fair attribution of "where outputs come from and who is responsible" (P44, Feng et al. 2026); external auditability and civil-society scrutiny (S01, Jungherr 2023); critique of "independence by permission" (S14, Piccardi et al. 2025); VLOP/VLSE independent audits and vetted-researcher data access (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026, DSA Art. 34/37/40). Bias detectability is normative — users should be able to recognise bias (and mostly cannot) (P46, Fisher et al. 2025).
  • Human-in-the-loop / augmentation not automation. Models "complementing traditional… methods, rather than replacing them," as "human-centered interventions" (P11, Belisario et al. 2025); a warning against "algocracy" (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025); real-time visualisation/"sensemaking" tools and clarifying-question elicitation under high uncertainty (P29/P37, Tessler et al.).
  • Provenance ≠ veracity.
