Watermarking/provenance (SynthID, C2PA) indicate who generated content but not trustworthiness, and untagged AI content can still mislead (P28, Marchal et al. 2026); embedded citation and watermarking are proposed but watermarking is "not a silver bullet" and "provably impossible" to make strong (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025); disclosure/non-deception norms plus liability for "undisclosed synthetic content" (P40, Bengio et al. 2026); verifiable agent credentials and "least privilege" sandboxes, with metadata "exploitable for surveillance" caveats (P28, Marchal et al. 2026; P41, Kapoor et al. 2025); label AI content and keep editorial oversight (P26, Simon & Altay 2025).
  • Transparency's limits and the over-reliance paradox. Detailed explanations create overhead and "can paradoxically cause over-reliance," raising the "optimal amount of cognitive 'friction'" question (P28, Marchal et al. 2026); avoid anthropomorphism and make trade-offs transparent (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026).
  • Procedural legitimacy over semantic truth (the inauthentic-behaviour line). Target "inauthentic behavior (i.e., provenance and coordination), rather than the semantic content of speech," to avoid "a central arbiter of truth"; disclose flagged "coordinated inauthentic activity," deploy defensive AI "only if governed by strict, transparent, and democratically accountable frameworks," and "prioritize precision over volume" intervening "only where manipulation is detected and verified" (P36, Schroeder et al. 2026).
  • Trustworthiness is socio-technically conferred — "not just an intrinsic property" but conferred by socio-technical systems (peer review, incentives) (P28, Marchal et al. 2026); "trust in AI is no longer a unitary construct," so model-specific trustworthiness must be evaluated individually (P03, Renault et al. 2025).
  • Visibility/reach equity for fact-checks — value depends on reaching viewers of the original claim (Community-Notes model superior); source quality/breadth is a criterion (narrow ideologically-skewed sources degrade accuracy) as is robustness to biased prompting/source filtering (P03, Renault et al. 2025).

11. Personalisation: bounds, fiduciary duty, and agentic (in)equality

↑ top
  • Bounding personalisation is inherently normative — "what are appropriate bounds… and who decides?", trading "individual autonomy and self-realisation… with societal stability," via Kantian, Rawlsian, Mill's harm-principle and Sandel/Habermas lenses, with proportionality grounding the mechanism (P10, Kirk et al. 2024).
  • A hierarchical "stacked moderation" governance model — three tiers where higher cannot be violated by lower: (1) "non-negotiable minimum standard of safety"; (2) formal regulatory bounds; (3) discretionary organisational bounds, with a residual "Zone of Personalised Alignment." An acceptability gradient (an "anti-woke LLM" may be acceptable, a "racist or extremist model" not), "West Coast Code"/Lessig framing leaving in-scope only low-risk attributes, and free-speech balancing (P10, Kirk et al. 2024). "Personalisation within bounds" recurs — fine-tune only without contravening community values, with "greater diversity in who governs the process" (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026).
  • Agent advocates, not platform agents (fiduciary/loyalty standard). Advocates are "faithful representatives of their principal," acting "exclusively in their interests," with credentials certifying "a fiduciary responsibility" (P41, Kapoor et al. 2025; adopted in P33, Sharp et al. 2026). Distinguish "neutral intermediaries" from "constitutive intermediaries"; judge "de-boosting" and alignment-based control against platform-rule "legitimacy." Supporting criteria: user visibility/control, data-minimisation, interoperability/open APIs ("net neutrality"), competition/contestability ("a real threat of exit"), safety constraints, and accountability/attribution — moving "as far… towards advocates as is feasible" (P41, Kapoor et al. 2025).
  • Agentic inequality and distributive justice. "What distribution of agentic capability should count as fair?" and whether agentic inequalities need "stricter standards of justice" (P33, Sharp et al. 2026). "Universal basic agency" treats capable-agent access as a Rawlsian primary good, requiring sufficient quality not just formal availability, with public-interest infrastructure and participatory (citizen-assembly) elicitation of acceptable gaps (P33, Sharp et al. 2026). Access equality recurs for persuasion — concentration vs cheap-and-widely-available determines whether justice gaps narrow or widen (P19, Hackenburg et al. 2026), with warnings against "concentrating these actors' power" (P21, Hackenburg et al. 2025) and over "who decides which positions a model will… argue" (P19, Hackenburg et al. 2026).
  • Value alignment expanded, with a legality baseline — agents "aligned with user well-being and societal norms… intentions of users and developers," and "not permitted to perform any action that would be illegal for their human user"; interpretability must "keep pace," with interoperability standards, "regulatory agents that monitor other agents," incident reporting, and "certifying agent safety before deployment" (P32, Gabriel et al. 2025).

12. Governance, regulation, and institutional/economic design

↑ top
  • Evaluate the substrate, not just stated aims — ask whether institutional arrangements pressure toward or away from cultivating human judgment (C02, McCord & Koralus 2026), via Marr-style aim/mechanism/substrate analysis because "the lower levels shape what's possible at the higher ones"; "every term sheet is a design document for what will be optimized" (A02, Koralus & McCord 2026; C02, McCord & Koralus 2026).
  • The Transparent Choice Test and Candid Aim Test. Transparent Choice: would the product "survive… of users who fully understand how the product works and could easily select an alternative?" — failing if its economics depend on the informed-choice gap. Candid Aim: "Is the stated aim key to the best explanation for how the system actually behaves…?" — a red flag when a contrary aim (e.g. "maximizing clicks") explains more. The framework is "analytically neutral" (could "design effective propaganda as easily as effective education"), and "no funding model is a panacea" (A02, Koralus & McCord 2026; C02, McCord & Koralus 2026).
  • Decentralisation over centralised control — of the "collective mind," supporting science's and markets' self-correcting processes, with a "marketplace of agents and inquiry complexes" and open-source as a governance dimension (C01, Koralus 2025; A02, Koralus & McCord 2026). Koralus's eight design criteria for autonomy-preserving AI: (i) erotetic equilibrium with minimal cognitive burden; (ii) individualized inquiry complexes; (iii) privacy as a cornerstone; (iv) security & trustworthiness; (v) decentralized control & ownership; (vi) mutual educability; (vii) a marketplace of agents; (viii) modularity for innovation (C01, Koralus 2025).
  • Fidelity, value-drift, and "measure what matters." Institutions should "(1) serve their stated purposes, and (2) sustain the conditions under which those purposes can be interpreted and revised without capture" (C04, Edelman 2026). Down-fidelity: purpose must translate faithfully to metrics/incentives/lived experience (Facebook's "Meaningful Social Interaction" critiqued); up-fidelity: interpretation must stay "live… contestable… and revisable" without "populist whims or elite capture" (C04, Edelman 2026). Track "the values they purport to serve rather than reasonless behavioral proxies"; "behavioral, universal, and quantitative" metrics cannot see what matters; keep decision-makers "close to consequences" with "skin in the game," resist capture, avoid "value substitution loops" (C03, Edelman 2026).
  • Six criteria for a public value, Thick Models of Value, stewardship. A public value should be concretely realizable, relevant, broadly legible, personally embraceable, entrepreneurial, and nonreductive (C04, Edelman 2026). Thick Models of Value are "precise enough to hold institutions accountable, yet pluralistic enough to accommodate disagreement," and revisable. A stewardship principle holds public-commitment assets "on behalf of a purpose," revocable for breach, with constraints to avoid politicisation: in rem, justiciable mandates, maturity and reliance, protected zones, and ripeness (C04, Edelman 2026). Candidate values are Freedom, Fairness, Fidelity, with a meta-question whether existing frameworks "suffice or must be supplemented for AI agents" (A02, Koralus & McCord 2026; C04, Edelman 2026).
  • A "good digital society": equity, resilience, sustainability and "good for whom." Three indispensable dimensions — equity (collective and differential), resilience ("collective wellbeing"), sustainability (environmental) (P18, Hale et al. 2024). Prefer doing good with/by/as communities over paternalistic for, foregrounding "digital good for whom?"; offer capabilities/technomoral-virtue/data-justice lenses; favour sufficiency/regenerative design; propose a "Digital Good Index" that is "not a simplistic checklist" (P18, Hale et al. 2024).
  • Reach as a criterion for systemic risk. A reach criterion must align with the AI Act (Recitals 110–111; Annex XIII "10 000 registered business users"; Art. 3(65)), be provider-checkable, minimal/proportionate, "maximise coverage across different GPAI models," and treat risk as arising from widespread use "regardless of whether [models] are at the technological frontier" — shifting the basis "from capability alone to societal exposure" (P09, Röttger et al. 2025). Usage threshold N>1; Model Reports documenting bias evaluations are "compatible with risks resulting from model reach"; acknowledged tension that emphasising reach-risks "could… dilute the Act's focus" away from frontier risks (P09, Röttger et al. 2025).
  • Mapping current law to AI-mediated opinion (and its gaps). Existing EU law is "insufficient" (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026): Art. 53 GPAI documentation "unclear whether [providers] must test/prevent nudging"; Art. 55/51 systemic-risk duties may not classify "inducing bias into a user's text" as systemic; Annex III(8)(b) election-influence may not apply; Art. 50(4) watermarking "does not extend to AI-generated text"; DSA Art. 34(1)(a–d) frames systemic risk around illegal content, expression, civic/electoral processes, gender-based violence, public health/minors. The implicit criterion: AI mediating opinion should preserve the user's original opinion/meaning without directional bias (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026).
  • Alignment as the fair treatment of claims (public justification). "When a technology has profound societal effects it ought to be regulated by principles… amenable to public rather than private justification"; alignment should track principles from fair processes with "no undue favouring of any one party," via a tetradic stakeholder model (AI systems, users, developers, society) and "six modes of misalignment" (P31, Gabriel & Keeling 2025). Criteria: Scanlonian reasonable-rejection, Rawlsian public reason, inclusion/"voice" un-mediated, and the all-affected principle with variable scope; rights "resist aggregation"; legal compliance is "only a minimum standard"; principles are forward-looking, contestable, iteratively re-aligned (P31, Gabriel & Keeling 2025).
  • Participatory / bottom-up legitimacy and collective ownership. Legitimacy "might depend… on participatory processes that reflect a broad societal consensus," with at minimum a procedural consensus that systems be "transparent, socially accountable, open to revision… and decentralized" (P28, Marchal et al. 2026). Participation is "a matter of justice" requiring being "asked and being heard," with representativeness, individualisation/steerability, pre-aggregation transparency about disagreement, documentation of annotator artefacts, welfare evaluation "attentive to out-group and minority welfare," and alignment data as a public good (P16, Kirk et al. 2024). STELA argues norm-elicitation should "centre" marginalised communities in an ethics of care (P34, Bergman et al. 2024); scaling participation invokes "all should have a say in how the power that governs them is exercised," with collective ownership, fair attribution, and Kalluri's "how it shifts power" (P44, Feng et al. 2026).
  • Symmetric-risk and proportionality framings. Acknowledge both market failure and "the symmetric risk of government failure"; "proof-of-human is no panacea" (endangers dissidents), favouring "verified-yet-anonymous posting" (P36, Schroeder et al. 2026). Regulation should "target actual threats and avoid backfiring / chilling effects" (P26, Simon & Altay 2025); a voluntary code of conduct can pair with binding regulation (EU AI Act; California AB-2013; US EO 14110) (P48, Fisher et al. 2025); platforms "closely regulated by democratic governments" (P02, Dunleavy & Margetts 2026); AI firms held accountable with reputational/trust safeguards (P26, Simon & Altay 2025); limiting manipulation "will likely curtail beneficial… applications" (P40, Bengio et al. 2026). The International AI Safety Report models institutional independence — full discretion, no specific policy recommendations, no endorsement of a regulatory approach (P40, Bengio et al. 2026).
  • Concrete normative targets for feed and ecosystem design. News quality (domain-level reliability/fact-checker ratings) is the yardstick — "design social media that actively rewards credible reporting" — defending the metric's neutrality against the partisan-bias objection (S11, Mosleh et al. 2025); "pro-user media" should "prioritize user well-being and epistemic health over… viral engagement" (P36, Schroeder et al. 2026).
  • Avoiding techno-solutionism and determinism. "Neither exuberant optimism nor despairing pessimism" (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025); a "middle of the road" view recognising human agency over tool use (P26, Simon & Altay 2025); valid evaluation requires interdisciplinarity, avoiding speculation that "amplifies unfounded fears" (S01, Jungherr 2023); avoid paternalism (don't assume Global-South/non-WEIRD populations "more gullible") and respect "open vigilance / epistemic autonomy" (P26, Simon & Altay 2025).
  • Conditions on historical-data reliance and electoral uncertainty. AI is legitimate only where "the past must provide a useful template for the future" — often violated in politics; preserve "organized uncertainty" and broad sharing of economic gains (S01, Jungherr 2023); whether "strong institutions… can buffer societies… and if so, for whom" is a legitimacy/equity question (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026); informational equality to "underserved communities" is a positive goal (P26, Simon & Altay 2025).

13. Methodological and evaluative norms

↑ top
  • Human-interaction (not output-only) evaluations. Persuasiveness "can only be reliably measured by quantifying change in the attitudes of real, diverse, and dynamic human populations" (P08, Hackenburg et al. 2025), since "only 9.1% of current LLM ethical/social-risk evaluations empirically examine human-AI interaction" (P25, Hackenburg et al. 2025). Behavioural (not just attitude) outcomes, because attitude-only conclusions "may be misleading" (P22, Hackenburg et al. 2026); knowing the threshold-crossing matters (P08, Hackenburg et al. 2025); evaluations must specify target population and goal (P22, Hackenburg et al. 2026). Shift "from static benchmarks to dynamic, real-world tests," sandboxes, red-teaming, and RCTs (P32, Gabriel et al. 2025; P23, Summerfield et al. 2025); "real (not simulated) human-AI interactions in natural contexts" (P15, Kirk et al. 2025); assess "in the social contexts where deployed" (S07, Cheng et al. 2026); continuous empirical monitoring over speculative scenarios (S01, Jungherr 2023); policy on "empirical socio-technical evaluation rather than 'popular concern'" (P25, Hackenburg et al. 2025).
  • Need proprietary and open-source evaluation; reproducibility. Cover both closed and open models (P25, Hackenburg et al. 2025); target post-training/prompting not just scale, since techniques can "bypass developer safeguards" (P21, Hackenburg et al. 2025), and open models could be biased by bad actors (P46, Fisher et al. 2025). Reproducibility is itself a value — public release of code, data, messages, replication materials, pre-registration (P08, Hackenburg et al. 2025; P21, Hackenburg et al. 2025; P22, Hackenburg et al. 2026; P25, Hackenburg et al. 2025; S02, Salvi et al. 2024).
  • Ecological validity and realism. Measure with prompts that "mirror real LLM usage," not artificial multiple-choice (P04, Röttger et al. 2025), using realistic templates and authentic-discourse issues "such that they do not introduce polarity" (P06, Lim & Röttger 2026); embed contextual/cultural elements since direct opinion questions are inadequate (P14, Rystrøm et al. 2025); real-world open-endedness with "no single ground truth" (P45, Jiang et al. 2025).
  • Robustness, fine-grained and stratified measurement. Many templates per issue with threshold-robustness/downsampling checks (P04, Röttger et al. 2025); issue-level over aggregate (P06, Lim & Röttger 2026); per-language/per-context with parallel/balanced design (U.S. and China; English and Chinese) (P06, Lim & Röttger 2026); aggregate distributions plus consistency controls since individual-response evaluations are "unstable/unreliable" (P14, Rystrøm et al. 2025). Stratified judge evaluation per model×language×task with length/position-bias neutrality, faithfulness, helpfulness/safety decoupling, and calibration — which judges violate under language shift (P17, Padarha et al. 2025). Human-centred evaluation "whenever possible" over automatic (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024; P39, Jarrett et al. 2025; P45, Jiang et al. 2025).
  • Validity, bias-auditing, scoping cautions. Selection bias and the information effect "must be checked before trusting AI-mediated counts" (P11, Belisario et al. 2025). Distinguish "direct epistemic effects… as a political research tool" from "broader equilibrium effects of AI availability"; search/AI "are not neutral information intermediaries"; an "epistemic health assessment" adds indicators (trust, private beliefs, extremity) beyond accuracy (P24, Luettgau et al. 2026). The personalisation question is "whether citizens retain exposure to diverse viewpoints and qualitative information" (P26, Simon & Altay 2025).
  • Limits of automated value evaluation. Extracting values "involves significant interpretation," is "inherently open-ended," and using Claude to evaluate Claude "may create biases" (P49, Huang et al. 2025); whose feedback debiases models is itself normative, with "caution when investigating the psychology of LLMs" (S06, Atari et al. 2023).
  • Mitigation via user education and AI literacy "preferable to (or alongside)" architecture/training changes (P46, Fisher et al. 2025); disclaimers and inoculation-style literacy help users "anticipate and resist over-affirmation" (S07, Cheng et al. 2026).
  • Research-ethics governance as an enacted normative model. A demonstrated standard for studying AI harms responsibly: ethics approval, informed consent, welfare monitoring, "Report Harm" button, off-boarding, OSF pre-registration (P13, Kirk et al. 2026); DSIT "Responsible Research Framework," non-logging, debrief, mental-health signposting, Helsinki compliance (P24, Luettgau et al. 2026; P27, Luettgau et al. 2026); UK AISI Research Assurance Board, petitions "submitted but not stored" (P22, Hackenburg et al. 2026); EPFL HREC, "controlled, harmless setting" (S02, Salvi et al. 2024); Royal Holloway ethics, screening (P25, Hackenburg et al. 2025); IRB "benign behavioral intervention" exemption, opt-out debriefing, "limit any lasting effects" in future biased-model work (P46, Fisher et al. 2025); independent review, no PII, compensation "above the regional living wage" (P39, Jarrett et al. 2025); consent with demand effects minimised (S14, Piccardi et al. 2025); "safe, responsible, and transparent" research (P38, Tacchetti et al. 2025). Equitable access, shared benchmarks, capacity-building, and long-term affordability/maintainability are named evaluative dimensions (P11, Belisario et al. 2025).

14. Recurrent cross-cutting trade-offs and cautions

↑ top
  • Distortion / influence may be an inherent cost — entangled properties mean "some degree of distortion may be an inherent cost of AI writing assistance" (P05, Röttger et al. 2026), generalised: limiting manipulation "will likely curtail beneficial… applications" (P40, Bengio et al. 2026).
  • Accuracy vs pluralism; truth-definition hazards — accuracy-driven defaults cause homogeneity (P20, Yang et al. 2026); defining "truth" can "backfire by rewarding subtler deception" (P40, Bengio et al. 2026).
  • Disclosure / transparency is necessary but insufficient — "labelling bias does not remove its influence," and a bare fact can read as endorsement (P48, Fisher et al. 2025); explanations can "paradoxically cause over-reliance" (P28, Marchal et al. 2026).
  • Pluralism's twin failure modes — false balance / "both-sidesism" vs "silencing minority views" (P48, Fisher et al. 2025; P50, Sorensen et al. 2024; P28, Marchal et al. 2026).
  • Individual autonomy/personalisation vs societal stability and shared discourse (P10, Kirk et al. 2024; P15, Kirk et al. 2025; C01, Koralus 2025).
  • Incommensurability and the limits of preference-utilitarianism — some values "cannot be traded off" (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024; P47, Jiang et al. 2025), with PRISM/the roadmap questioning whether averaged-preference utilitarianism aligns with wellbeing and whether some feedback is (il)legitimate (P16, Kirk et al. 2024; P50, Sorensen et al. 2024).
  • No funding model is a panacea; analytic frameworks are value-laden — "the BBC subsequently developed other pathologies, and any funding model creates its own selection pressures" (C02, McCord & Koralus 2026); the recurrent meta-caution that the analyst's normative priors must be made explicit and values set by a fair, participatory, contestable process is where this literature both begins and ends (C02, McCord & Koralus 2026; P31, Gabriel & Keeling 2025; P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026).

Aggregation and filtering of opinionated political content

↑ top
On this section (1)

    • 2. Framing as the mechanism: same facts, partisan emphasis
    • 3. Refusal as a hard filter on political content
    • 4. Selective fact-checking and verdict asymmetries on political claims
    • 5. Persuasion via selective marshalling of political information
    • 6. Sycophancy, personalisation, and partisan filter bubbles
    • 7. Steering and reranking of the user's own political stance via writing assistance
    • 8. Representational bias and WEIRD/Western skew
    • 9. Inferring, selecting, and distributionally representing which political opinion a person (or population) holds
