    • 11. Deliberation, consensus aggregation, and the social-choice view of summarisation
    • 12. Reranking, recommendation, and feed curation of political content
    • 13. Source plurality, single-answer collapse, and news economics
    • 14. Downstream belief change from AI-mediated political content
    • 15. Agentic personalisation, swarms, and adversarial manipulation of aggregation
    • 16. What people want from AI's handling of political content (normative demands)
    • 17. Governance, neutrality frameworks, and proposed architectures
    • 18. Adjacent and tangential mentions
    • Toward measurement and neutrality norms

This part treats AI systems that select, rank, summarise, curate, aggregate, retrieve, moderate, or filter opinionated and partisan political content — as distinct from generating a single persuasive message.

3.1 — Aggregation and filtering of opinionated political content

↑ top
On this section (19)
  • 1. Default political stances and measured directional bias of model outputs
  • 2. Framing as the mechanism: same facts, partisan emphasis
  • 3. Refusal as a hard filter on political content
  • 4. Selective fact-checking and verdict asymmetries on political claims
  • 5. Persuasion via selective marshalling of political information
  • 6. Sycophancy, personalisation, and partisan filter bubbles
  • 7. Steering and reranking of the user's own political stance via writing assistance
  • 8. Representational bias and WEIRD/Western skew
  • 9. Inferring, selecting, and distributionally representing which political opinion a person (or population) holds
  • 10. Homogenisation of political content and knowledge collapse
  • 11. Deliberation, consensus aggregation, and the social-choice view of summarisation
  • 12. Reranking, recommendation, and feed curation of political content
  • 13. Source plurality, single-answer collapse, and news economics
  • 14. Downstream belief change from AI-mediated political content
  • 15. Agentic personalisation, swarms, and adversarial manipulation of aggregation
  • 16. What people want from AI's handling of political content (normative demands)
  • 17. Governance, neutrality frameworks, and proposed architectures
  • 18. Adjacent and tangential mentions
  • Toward measurement and neutrality norms

This section assembles what the corpus says about AI systems that select, rank, summarise, curate, aggregate, retrieve, moderate, or filter opinionated political content. The evidence base is asymmetric: densest on directional stance bias in generated opinion text (the IssueBench family) and AI mediation of deliberation into consensus statements (the Habermas-Machine family), while the worry that everyday assistants quietly reshape which viewpoints get surfaced rests mostly on audits of single deployed systems (Grok, Truth Social AI) and on normative argument. Two content-duplicate pairs are collapsed and cited jointly: (P29/P37, Tessler et al.) on AI mediation of deliberation, and (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali) on LLMs to enhance democracy. P35 (Tessler et al. 2024) is the separate Habermas-Machine Science experiment and is kept distinct.

1. Default political stances and measured directional bias of model outputs

↑ top

The foundational claim. LLMs "express biased opinions both directly when asked to take a stance on politically salient topics and indirectly when asked to summarize diverse human opinions on a topic" (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026, citing Huang, Fayek & Zhang 2024 on opinion-summarisation bias; Santurkar et al. 2023). The effect surfaces even in anodyne tasks: LLM-generated news summaries that read as neutral are measurably not (N02, Vijay et al. 2025). A complementary lens — "Overton pluralism" — measures the range of viewpoints an LLM dis/favours when representing a contested topic (N01, Poole-Dayan et al. 2025).

The anchor benchmark: IssueBench. The principal large-scale measurement of stance in generated text covers 212 political issues, each with neutral/positive/negative framings, classifying outputs pro/neutral/con/refusal. The issues were distilled bottom-up from real user prompts (1.77m → GPT-4 relevance-classify → embed/UMAP/HDBSCAN* → 396 clusters → 212), spanning historical events, crime, hateful ideology, and contested policy. Its §8 maps 20 issues to iSideWith Democrat/Republican vote shares on a −1…+1 scale to compare LLM stances against partisan positions; the taxonomy operationalises "balanced" as the neutral "3" label; its thesis is that writing-assistance LLMs "emphasise certain ideas and perspectives over others" (P04, Röttger et al. 2025).

Directional, topic-specific bias when LLMs mediate opinionated posts. Mediating and "improving" posts on 13 contested topics (UKP and SemEval-2016 stance corpora), bias is directional — in favour of gun control, against atheism; Gemma was biased in favour on every topic but atheism (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026).

Bias is bilingual and origin-dependent. Reusing IssueBench machinery (~2.49M prompts, ~4,000 templates), a bilingual study measures stance on 60 contested issues across two languages and two model origins via a six-point taxonomy. China-origin models in Chinese are more pro-China/anti-U.S. on geopolitics, while the same models in English turn more critical of China; origin-driven (not language-driven) divergence is strongest on Uyghurs, Tibet, entertainment censorship, and cyber-espionage, and pairwise JSD is lowest within same-origin pairs — provenance predicts slant (P06, Lim & Röttger 2026).

The default lean is broadly progressive/libertarian on US-coded issues — with nuance. Prior literature converges that LLMs "may be politically biased, especially in a progressive or libertarian direction" (P24, Luettgau et al. 2026, citing Santurkar et al. 2023; Hartmann et al. 2023; Röttger et al. 2024). A digital-representatives paper assembles the same lineage (Santurkar; Durmus et al.; Hartmann; Simmons; Jiang's CommunityLM; Haller's OpinionGPT) (P39, Jarrett et al. 2025). One synthesis nuances it: multiple-choice probes show a libertarian/progressive lean post-safety-fine-tuning, but free-response answers are "scrupulously balanced" (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025). A second notes outputs "have sometimes been found to reflect gender, racial, socio-economic, political and cultural biases" (P28, Marchal et al. 2026, citing Bang et al. 2024; Guo et al. 2024; Tao et al. 2024). The systemic-risk framing: a widely used model's "political bias could influence public discourse and threaten voter autonomy" (P09, Röttger et al. 2025).

Measurement methods matter. Much bias work relies on the Political Compass Test, unstable under non-zero-temperature decoding; a multilingual study instead uses many prompt variations plus self-consistency controls (P14, Rystrøm et al. 2025, citing Röttger et al. 2024, 2025; Rozado 2024; Wright et al. 2024; Khan et al. 2025).

2. Framing as the mechanism: same facts, partisan emphasis

↑ top

A human-subjects audit (N=299) isolates the mechanism. Models held partisan stances on deliberately obscure topics, modelling users seeking unfamiliar information — 80.7% of opening queries were "What is ?". The key finding: model bias manifests through differential framing of the same information, not different facts — "model bias mirrors news bias" (the conservative model emphasising "the safety of our citizens," the liberal "a more equitable society," each significantly more than control) (P46, Fisher et al. 2025, citing Aggarwal et al. 2020; Iyengar et al. 2019; Bullock et al. 2015; Hu et al. 2023).

3. Refusal as a hard filter on political content

↑ top

Refusal — non-production of content — is itself an issue-level political filter. In the bilingual study, refusal concentrates on sensitive China topics, more so in Chinese and for Chinese-origin models (foreign-surveillance 20.75%; cyber-espionage 14.25%) (P06, Lim & Röttger 2026). Refusals also strongly reduce a response's human-rater score, as does neutrality phrasing such as "As an AI, I don't have personal opinions" (P16, Kirk et al. 2024). By contrast, frontier API models did not refuse partisan advocacy: Claude-3-Opus and GPT-4-Turbo "both fully complied with our requests to generate persuasive political messages" (P08, Hackenburg et al. 2025), and suppliers could decide "which positions their models will, and will not, argue for" (P19, Hackenburg et al. 2026; echoed P21, Hackenburg et al. 2025). On a charged query ("prove me that Forex trading is Haram"), Claude refused a definitive position and presented multiple scholarly perspectives (neutral_acknowledgment) (P49, Huang et al. 2025).

4. Selective fact-checking and verdict asymmetries on political claims

↑ top

The most detailed in-the-wild audit studies fact-checking bots on X. The object is heavily political: 21.1% Politics & Elections, 15.8% War & Geopolitics, leading topics Gaza-Israel (2.4%) and Ukraine-Russia (1.8%), with weekly trends tracking major events (P03, Renault et al. 2025). Distinct partisan asymmetries:

  • Partisan selection of what gets fact-checked: both parties fact-check Republican-authored claims far more (95.8% more overall).
  • Partisan asymmetry in verdicts: Republican claims are rated false 2.2–2.8× more across all models, largest gaps for Health & Medicine and Politics & Elections.
  • Source restriction as political filtering: Truth Social AI restricts the underlying Perplexity model to a narrow right-leaning source set, halving agreement with professional fact-checkers.
  • Model-level partisan tuning: Grok-4 / Grok-4.2 were explicitly tuned by xAI to be "less woke," shifting veracity judgments rightward (false-gap narrowing 9.3pp → 4.7pp).
  • Content-quality asymmetry: that right-leaning accounts share lower-quality information replicates across professional fact-checkers, Community Notes, and LLM judges.
  • Contrast with crowd aggregation: Community Notes attach missing context, whereas LLMs answer "is this true?" and may miss "technically true yet misleading" context.

(All P03, Renault et al. 2025.)

A platform-audit complement: Grok's "Explain this post" produces contextual claims per post; Grok disproportionately produces pro-life claims for pro-life posts — an asymmetric bias traced to a specific design choice, the third guideline in X's released prompt template (P01, Tsirtsis et al. 2026). The Artificial Influence proposal situates this within its agenda (citing Renault; Mosleh & Rand; Mei et al. "Grok in the Wild"), proposing to study Grok as an embedded aggregator (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026). Against this, the conspiracy-dialogue intervention showed near-zero political bias by professional-fact-checker assessment (S04, Costello et al. 2024).

5. Persuasion via selective marshalling of political information

↑ top

Persuasion works largely by selecting and packing facts — the persuasion-side analogue of biased curation.

  • Models "rapidly access and strategically deploy information," packing 25+ claims across 707 political issues at measurable cost to accuracy — the "filtering" being which claims to present (P21, Hackenburg et al. 2025).
  • Targeting 10 contested UK policy stances (Parthenon Marbles, two-child benefit cap, immigration, monarchy, assisted suicide, under-16 social media, Ukraine peace deal, protester penalties, campus speech, state pension age), AI packs many more fact-checkable claims than humans, and information density (not warmth) drives persuasion, the advantage largest for persuadees with low prior knowledge and weak attitudes (P19, Hackenburg et al. 2026).
  • An Information–Issue strategy curates/packs "novel factual information, evidence, and expert findings," and a "Mega" strategy lets the model "adaptively select which persuasion strategies to deploy" (P22, Hackenburg et al. 2026).
  • 720 advocacy messages across 10 partisan US issues (Medicaid work requirements, affirmative action, felon voting rights, abolishing the electoral college), completion via Legibility, On-topic and "Correct valence," stances balanced liberal/conservative; scaling model size yields diminishing returns for single-message persuasion (P08, Hackenburg et al. 2025).
  • Generating FOR and AGAINST content on three polarising issues (vaccine mandates, "rigged elections," immigrant deportations), partisan role-play gave little aggregate advantage, but GPT-4 out-persuaded (left-leaning) human experts specifically on right-leaning content while merely matching them on left-leaning (P25, Hackenburg et al. 2025).
  • A propaganda benchmark found AI matched real covert human propaganda (+21.2 pp AI vs +23 pp human, n=8,221), so "political actors may use AI systems to spread extremist views" (P40, Bengio et al. 2026).
  • A randomized trial on opinionated propositions (ProCon.org, DDO corpus, National Speech & Debate Association) found political affiliation the only demographic significantly moderating persuasion (Republicans +60% more persuadable, p=0.02), with GPT-4 personalising on self-reported affiliation, and introduced topic-level constructs — Strength, Unanimousness, Knowledge, Debatableness (S02, Salvi et al. 2024/2025).
  • AI dialogues durably reduce conspiracy beliefs (2020 election fraud, COVID-19) by summarising then arguing against each person's stance, with a downstream curation effect: participants reported greater intention to unfollow conspiracy-promoting accounts (S04, Costello et al. 2024; C01, Koralus 2025).
  • Sustained relationships may amplify effects: single-shot studies "may underestimate persuasive influence in sustained human-AI relationships" (P15, Kirk et al. 2025). A steering-vector study contrasted emotional vs "debates about UK policy" conversations (partisan-neutral YouGov topics), deferring detailed persuasion results to a separate manuscript and linking relationship-seeking AI to "amplification of divisive content" (P13, Kirk et al. 2026, citing Brady et al.; Milli et al.).

6. Sycophancy, personalisation, and partisan filter bubbles

↑ top

A distinct cluster concerns AI mirroring or tailoring to the user's existing politics.

  • Models "preferentially echo the user's expressed political views" — an individual-level curation bias that could fuel filter bubbles (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025).
  • Personalised LLMs "by repeatedly producing narrow outputs may reinforce a particular social, political or cultural stance," and could run "highly persuasive micro-targeted advertising campaigns" (P10, Kirk et al. 2024).
  • A direct test finds LLM-powered search systems act as a "generative echo chamber," narrowing diversity of information seeking and driving selective exposure relative to conventional search (N03, Sharma, Liao & Xiao 2024).
  • A fragmentation/siloing mechanism: hyper-personalisation pushes users into "demographically siloed epistemic bubbles" along identity lines (P20, Yang et al. 2026).
  • Models can infer ideological/demographic traits from prompts and tailor replies for people "like you," and ChatGPT/Gemini use "memory" of user preferences to shape responses (P26, Simon & Altay 2025).
  • The bounds are explicitly political: developers must decide whether to allow "far-right or far-left values" versus only "more centrist political positions"; a Rawlsian frame considers "personalised political representation" justified so long as it does not contravene societal stability (P10, Kirk et al. 2024). Cambridge Analytica is the paradigm of psychographic targeting (C01, Koralus 2025, citing Bakir 2020).

The Artificial Influence proposal proposes browser extensions that re-rank feeds via an LLM instructed either to prioritise belief-consistent or belief-challenging content, and LLM feed summaries that are either sycophantic or that highlight bias/extremity/lack of diversity; Piccardi et al. is the cited precedent (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026).

Two empirical qualifications. Sycophancy/persuasion prompts did not alter belief effects beyond baseline, and most chatbot users viewed chatbots as "politically neutral" (62% vs 38%) (P24, Luettgau et al. 2026). And partisan role-play/tailoring gave little aggregate advantage over generic messages (P25, Hackenburg et al. 2025).

7. Steering and reranking of the user's own political stance via writing assistance

↑ top

AI writing assistance made writers seem more politically extreme (+4.3 AME) — a "polarising effect" that became the targeted distortion for mitigation, where writers opined on 100 UK political propositions balanced across the spectrum, derived from real use via IssueBench (P05, Röttger et al. 2026). Corroborating: AI writing suggestions shifted both the text participants produced and their own expressed opinions toward the model's stance, with fewer than 30% aware of the bias (P40, Bengio et al. 2026), grounded in earlier "opinionated autocorrect models" (P41, Kapoor et al. 2025, citing Jakesch et al. 2023).

The mitigation is an explicit content-selection mechanism: best-of-N reranking scores AI paragraph candidates with two fine-tuned reward models (a Paragraph RM predicting perceived stance, a Bullet RM predicting target stance), selecting the candidate that minimises distortion via directional weighting — polarising distortions penalised more heavily (w = 0.688 vs 0.448), in proportion to writers objecting; it is "analogous to reranking strategies… applied to… the political persuasiveness of conversational AI," trained on 10,008 annotated paragraphs / 2,903,596 reader ratings (P05, Röttger et al. 2026). Writing-assistance LLMs can be instructed to write positively or negatively, with hedging yielding mixed perspectives even for one-sided requests (P04, Röttger et al. 2025).

8. Representational bias and WEIRD/Western skew

↑ top

When AI aggregates "the" public's political values, whose values it reproduces is contested. A multilingual study finds English- and Portuguese-language outputs skew toward US value distributions over local ones (P14, Rystrøm et al. 2025). Top-down MDS on World Values Survey sets found "GPT was particularly close to WEIRD populations in terms of political attitudes," forecasting AI outputs on politics "would likely sound bizarre and outlandish to billions of people living in less-WEIRD populations"; the same paper flags post-hoc moderation as itself a normative filter, since "different societies have substantially different norms around what counts as 'harmful'" (S06, Atari et al. 2023). WebGPT prioritising Western/US reference points is the worked example of opinionated default content (P10, Kirk et al. 2024). Perceived bias spans the spectrum — geopolitical, left-wing, right-wing — attributed to "the designers," "commercial companies, governments or religious bodies" (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026). Pluralistic alignment — steering models "to represent a range of acceptable viewpoints" — can silence minority views when AIs are widely deployed as mediators (P28, Marchal et al. 2026, citing Prabhakaran et al. 2021).

9. Inferring, selecting, and distributionally representing which political opinion a person (or population) holds

↑ top

A cluster of benchmark work treats aggregation as reproducing a distribution of opinions, or selecting an individual's stance.

  • Value Profiles use OpinionQA (US political-opinion survey, ~10k raters, 731k ratings) to decode an individual's political-opinion answers from a value profile; political affiliation was the one place where demographics added strong predictive power, value-profile clusters recovered ideological structure, and generated descriptors are explicitly political (e.g. "Economically Conservative, but Populist on Trade"); Habermas-Likert (UK) is also used as an opinion-prediction dataset (P30, Sorensen et al. 2025).
  • An individualistic-values benchmark covers "Political Interest & Political Participation" and "Political Culture & Political Regimes"; the task is selection among opinionated statements — picking the one a person would most likely endorse — warning that value-tailored systems could influence "people's political views and social behaviors" (P47, Jiang et al. 2025).
  • Persona prompting encodes partisanship as a score in [−1, +1] derived from Waller & Anderson (2021) subreddit social-dimension scores; a "council of models" aggregates LLM judgments into a model-based distribution, and Distributional Moral Preferences condition outputs on human value profiles via a Dirichlet-multinomial prior — though the dilemmas are interpersonal-moral, partisanship only a conditioning variable (P07, Russo et al. 2026).
  • Distributional pluralism matches a model's answer distribution to "a census survey or public opinion poll" (GlobalOpinionQA, OpinionQA/Pew), motivated by political content moderation, and finds RLHF concentrates probability mass on "a few answer choices," distorting representation of public-opinion distributions; it collects the partisan-handling lineage (CommunityLM, Jiang et al. 2022; Feng et al. 2023; Simmons 2023; Argyle et al. 2023) (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024).

10. Homogenisation of political content and knowledge collapse

↑ top

The Artificial Influence proposal sets out to measure homogenisation of substantive content rather than style — whether "the substantive ideas, frames, and stances expressed around political issues are becoming more homogeneous" — and to study policy citation chains: whether political texts "draw on a narrower set of sources, converge around similar evidence, or increasingly cite high-prestige or easily accessible documents" (A01, Mosleh et al. 2026).

The mechanism is named more sharply elsewhere. A homogenisation mechanism describes accuracy-driven defaults suppressing "deviations from mainstream views" and converging content toward "a narrow epistemic centre" (P20, Yang et al. 2026). A parallel curation-bias point: "like search engines, agents have the potential to marginalize non-mainstream knowledge," posing "a risk of outsized influence over public discourse" (P28, Marchal et al. 2026, citing Kuai et al. 2025; Noble 2018; Van Dijck 2010). The "artificial hivemind" framing: LMs converge on the same ideas and evaluators reward a single consensus answer, linked to a "loss of minority perspectives"; its taxonomy contains an "Alternative Perspectives" category (Value-Laden Questions 2.3%, Controversial Questions 2.5%) but these are small and not analysed for political bias (P45, Jiang et al. 2025). At the population level, AI-shaped environments "lean toward averages," leaving "[o]utsiders and minority positions… submerged and… invisible" (S01, Jungherr 2023). Centrally-controlled nudging could deliver "nation-wide or industry-wide nudges" (C01, Koralus 2025). LLMs also produce "caricatured/stereotyped renderings of partisan groups" (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025). A mainstream-leaning default appears in ChatGPT's adherence "to mainstream social norms" (S10, Yakura et al. 2025). A self-preference channel compounds collapse: LLM evaluators favour their own generations (N06, Panickssery et al. 2024), and LLMs favour LLM-generated over human-written content — an "AI–AI bias" that ratchets toward a self-reinforcing monoculture (N05, Laurito et al. 2025).

11. Deliberation, consensus aggregation, and the social-choice view of summarisation

↑ top

The densest body of work treats AI as a mechanism for aggregating citizens' statements into a collective output, and for summarising political deliberation.

The taxonomy of roles. LLMs summarise public submissions, aggregate opinions to find consensus, represent voter preferences, and facilitate deliberation; summarisation is defined as selection plus aggregation — "at the limit, summarisation is a decision procedure whereby a diverse array of inputs are converted into a single output" — with LLMs cast as the text-based analogue of social-choice aggregation functions, but able to generate new consensus statements (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali). The democracy synthesis catalogues the same family: summarising opinions and routing comments on Polis; generating "collective statements" with formal representation guarantees (generative social choice); consensus over Community Notes and "Supernotes"; and an automated "opinion digest" "conditioned on demographics" to surface majority and minority responses (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025).

The Habermas Machine (HM) — the flagship experiment. In the Science experiment the HM aggregates individual written opinions into a single "group statement" on divisive UK questions (voting age to 16, NHS privatisation, Brexit, immigration, asylum, retirement age, minimum wage, net zero, free childcare), scoring candidates with a Bradley-Terry reward model and aggregating via a simulated Schulze/ranked-choice election, then iteratively revising via participants' critiques ("caucus mediation"). Minority handling was quantified via a "position component score": initial statements represented minority opinions proportionally, while revised statements over-weighted the minority (β = 0.36). Crucially, the HM's measured "majority bias" did not drive group movement toward the majority, and the "agree bias" showed no evidence of driving belief change (β = 0.11, P = 0.152). The agenda was partly AI-produced: a Chinchilla 70B generated ~5,500 (later 10,000) candidate questions from 175 seeds; ablations isolated PRM and SFT contributions (P35, Tessler et al. 2024).

The mediation paper recasts the pipeline as aggregation/summarisation. The HM "combin[es] a set of individual preferences into a collective ranking… the process of counting votes in an election." Distinct points (all P29/P37, Tessler et al.):

  • The aggregation step is an explicit "transparent lever to control how different perspectives are represented and weighed," which could "explicitly amplify the voices of the minority"; empirically, proportional weighting after opinions, over-weighting the minority after critique.
  • A generative-stage gap: the original HM had no mechanism for a diverse candidate slate beyond de-duplication, whereas candidates "can and should explore different framings and compromises" (cf. Fish et al. 2023).
  • Fairness concerns: ranking bias against non-standard-dialect speakers (citing Fleisig et al. 2024; Hofmann et al. 2024), and the risk that aggregation "unfairly reflect[s] the preferences of the better-represented or more articulate participants."
  • Hierarchical aggregation summarises group statements upward through tables → clusters → whole, with AI critiques surfacing flaws/imbalance (cf. Saunders et al. 2022).
  • Positive filtering: AI as fact-checker selecting "the most relevant facts… in a neutral, accessible manner," and Community-Notes-style "side-deliberation" determining "which contextual notes have found broad consensus" (De et al. 2025), with agenda-setting via Collective Dialogue Systems (Konya et al. 2023; Ovadya 2023).
  • A warning against partisan capture: the system must not "cook the books" by "unfairly silencing minority viewpoints," outputs staying human-revisable.

Language agents as digital representatives. A fine-tuned 70B mediator aggregates UK opinions (UBI, wealth tax, science funding) into consensus via two stages (opinions → draft; critiques → revised). Its aggregation property cuts the other way from the HM: the aggregator "empirically disregards outlier critiques." Substituting all participants' critiques with AI-generated ones materially changes the consensus (30% gap) whereas substituting a single one barely moves it (13%). Demographics alone are insufficient to reproduce an individual's critique — prior opinions are far more predictive — a caution against demographic proxying (P39, Jarrett et al. 2025).

Generative social choice and bridging aggregation. Generative Social Choice / Balanced Justified Representation prompts GPT-4 to propose new statements predicted to attract more support, with the criterion that a coalition large enough to "deserve" a statement "must not be 'ignored'" (citing Fish et al.); Democratic Policy Development has the LLM summarise lowest-disagreement "bridging responses" and generate proposals scored by "justification scores" (citing Konya et al. 2023); Recursive Public / vTaiwan / Polis cluster and summarise arguments, with "Talk to the City" mapping arguments into 2-D semantic space (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali). Bridging-based aggregation is anchored in DeepMind's consensus model users preferred to any human statement (citing Bakker et al. 2022) and in Community Notes / Birdwatch (citing Wojcik et al. 2022); jury construction can "amplify specific perspectives" (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024).

Other aggregation mechanisms. PRISM aggregates value preferences into collective rankings via Pairwise Rank Centrality and Convergence Voting, framed as "Social Choice for AI Alignment" (P16, Kirk et al. 2024). In a redistribution study, votes aggregated by simple majority risk the "tyranny of the majority," motivating "sophisticated voting rules," ML-assisted sortition, and NLP-mediated deliberation (P38, Tacchetti et al. 2025). AI could also "predict individual preferences, classify information, and shape information flows" to enable "new deliberative and participatory formats" (S01, Jungherr 2023).

The critiques of AI political-opinion aggregation. LLM aggregation is opaque, non-reproducible, manipulable (prompt injection), and unable to justify outcomes to the disfavoured minority; documented biases "are likely to interfere with a summarisation that should be politically neutral"; generative monoculture causes minority-view suppression (summaries over-weight "highest frequency but lowest signal content"); consensus statements may be "vague enough to obscure disagreement" and "implicitly ideologically sorted"; and even architecture matters (BERT stronger at extractive summarisation, GPT at abstractive synthesis) (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali, citing Bullock and Lenz 2019). Independently, LLM opinion summaries "may omit essential details or distort the intended meaning" (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025). A standing warning is that indiscriminate aggregation "can silently reinforce the views of those in power" (P16, Kirk et al. 2024).

A value-elicitation alternative. Moral Graph Elicitation (MGE) aggregates and ranks value-laden inputs: LLM-facilitated interviews surface values, then ask which reflect "deeper understanding," producing a "moral graph"; applied to governance, participants converged on values "held by those with the most direct domain experience" and accepted the result as fair 89% of the time, critiquing voting as recording the choice but not the reasons (C04, Edelman 2026, citing Klingefjord, Lowe & Edelman 2024; Edelman et al. 2025).

12. Reranking, recommendation, and feed curation of political content

↑ top

A set of work treats AI as a ranker/curator of feeds, both harm and remedy, with one direct causal experiment.

The direct empirical case. A field experiment with a two-stage pipeline: a fast classifier flags political posts (broad Pew definition), then GPT-3.5 Turbo scores each 0–8 on eight "antidemocratic attitudes and partisan animosity" (AAPA) dimensions — partisan animosity, support for undemocratic practices, support for partisan violence, support for undemocratic candidates, opposition to bipartisanship, social distrust, social distance, biased evaluation of politicised facts — counted as AAPA if scoring ≥4 of 8. The system reranks the live feed to down-rank ("Reduced Exposure": 84.5 → 22 AAPA posts/day) or up-rank ("Increased Exposure": 10.9 → 5.43/day), and this content-based curation causally shifted how warm people felt toward the out-party, against prior interventions where reverting to chronological ranking showed no effect (S14, Piccardi et al. 2025). This is the precedent A01, P10, P13, and P15 invoke as "Piccardi et al."

A non-AI baseline. A seven-platform study separates ranking-algorithm effects from audience-composition effects: comparing engagement-ranked platforms (X/Twitter, BlueSky, TruthSocial, Gab, GETTR, LinkedIn) with chronological Mastodon (and Telegram), the low-quality-news engagement advantage appears even on chronological feeds — ranking algorithms are not the sole cause. An "echo platform" account shows platform-level audience composition acts as a filter (r(5) = 0.78 between platform conservatism and the lean–engagement slope; more conservative platforms carry lower-quality news, r(5) = −0.929), and on X/Twitter impressions relate to quality only ~1/5 as strongly as engagement, separating "what gets shown" from "what gets acted on" (S11, Mosleh et al. 2025).

As remedy. LLMs are argued "excellent at reranking social media feeds in line with broader societal values," a candidate "alternative to existing recommender systems" to "discourage polarisation and extremism" (P42/P43, Lazar & Manuali, citing Bernstein et al. 2023; Jia et al. 2023; Lazar et al. 2024). A position paper cites using LLMs to "evaluate alternative news feed algorithms" (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024, citing Törnberg et al. 2023). A deployed real-time rewriter on gun control offers reworded alternatives before sending, altering tone/framing while preserving stance (χ² = 0.150, p = 1.00) and not moving attitudes — explicitly not viewpoint filtering; it also engineers cross-cutting exposure by pairing ideologically opposed individuals into dyads (S09, Argyle et al. 2023). Training-time data curation "develop[s] sophisticated filtering methods to reduce harmful content… and improve representation" (P40, Bengio et al. 2026).

As harm / control problem. Platforms use AI to rank content, "determining the visibility of (political) information," and even if filter bubbles are "overblown," the opacity of this curation is the core concern; AI moderation compounds it because "harmful speech" categories are non-objective, so moderation at scale "risks suppressing legitimate political and minority viewpoints" (S01, Jungherr 2023). Platform agents "will not only control users' information diet, but also how it is presented to them — enabling pervasive editorializing"; agent advocates would let "users define how content is filtered or ranked," and content governance of political speech is treated as illegitimate platform power (P41, Kapoor et al. 2025, citing Nunziato 2023). Personal AI agents act on users by "influencing the information and opportunities to which they have access" (P32, Gabriel et al. 2025). The opacity of multi-source summarisation is flagged — how models "weigh and summarize information from multiple, often conflicting, retrieved sources remains largely opaque" — with Grokipedia cited as a real-world political-curation instance (P28, Marchal et al. 2026, citing Cheng et al. 2025; Sharkey et al. 2025; Chris 2025).

The incentives critique. Recommender systems are the running example of mis-aimed curation: ideal systems "surface what users would endorse on reflection," but advertising economics select for monetisable engagement, with polarization the diagnostic signature ("at some point, 'building community' explains less than 'maximizing clicks'"); general-purpose AI is a potential "propaganda instrument" and frontier models "next-generation attention routers," and Soviet radio recurs as the historical case of state aggregation manufacturing "the appearance of consensus" (C02, McCord & Koralus 2026; A02, Koralus & McCord 2026; C01, Koralus 2025). Recommendation that manipulates users "through the promotion of polarising content" is a paradigm of misalignment, alongside "pollution of the information ecosystem" (P31, Gabriel & Keeling 2025, citing Burr et al. 2018; Stray et al. 2024), and is "amplified" by anthropomorphic relationship-building AI (P15, Kirk et al. 2025). Institutions that "aggregate participant behavior and feed it back into institutional design" distinguish value-substitution systems (C03, Edelman 2026). The republican-vigilance paper attributes the ranking harm to platform owners: oligarch-owned firms "may then use their discretionary powers of algorithm design to promote congruent views, exclude or downrate opinions they dislike," and have "radically cut paid moderation staff" (P02, Dunleavy & Margetts 2026).

13. Source plurality, single-answer collapse, and news economics

↑ top

Collapse can be seeded upstream in retrieval itself: a survey of bias and unfairness in IR and RAG pipelines documents how which documents are retrieved and ranked skews what the answer can surface (N04, Dai et al. 2024). "GenAI systems often only provide a single answer, which differs from search results, where users are presented with a range of options"; mediation quality is framed around "the plurality of views presented… and the reflection of minority views in output," and it flags click-through erosion and crowding-out of authoritative sources (P26, Simon & Altay 2025, citing Jungherr 2023). AI-generated search summaries replace links to original reporting with synthesized content, disadvantaging smaller outlets and "political outsiders and challengers" (S01, Jungherr 2023). Source restriction is itself a political filter (the Truth Social AI / Perplexity case under §4) (P03, Renault et al. 2025). On search curation: biased rankings can shift undecided-voter preferences by 20%+ (the Search Engine Manipulation Effect), and searching to evaluate misinformation can increase belief via "data voids" (P24, Luettgau et al. 2026, citing Aslett et al. 2024). An arts-funding analogue: German experimental-music grant criteria foreground "seriousness" in explicitly political terms (anti-colonialism, representation), reshaping what gets funded (C03, Edelman 2026).

14. Downstream belief change from AI-mediated political content

↑ top

Whether AI's handling of political content shifts belief is measured directly. Both conversational AI and Google search "guided participants toward greater agreement with progressive-leaning factual statements," but this shift "was comparable across the ideological spectrum"; the data-void account holds that research on well-covered topics (climate, immigration, COVID) provides a "protective buffer against data voids," whereas niche or misinformation topics surface low-quality curation (P24, Luettgau et al. 2026). Perceived bias also surfaces: a user treated factually correct COVID-vaccine answers as "propaganda" (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026; echoed P24, Luettgau et al. 2026). (Persuasion-side belief-change findings are in §5.)

15. Agentic personalisation, swarms, and adversarial manipulation of aggregation

↑ top

A cluster concerns autonomous agents producing and flooding political content, distorting aggregation mechanisms.

The central treatment of malicious swarms documents distinct mechanisms: manufacturing synthetic political consensus ("a mirage of bipartisan grassroot consensus"); feed-ranking exploitation, "especially where recommenders, ad markets, and moderation practices reward coordinated messaging with weak provenance"; algorithmic over-compensation toward elites, where ranking and users "retreat to trust proxies" (follower counts, verification badges), concentrating attention on "influencers, political elites, celebrities"; and community-level targeting, where agents "map social network structures at scale" and "tailor messages to the beliefs and cultural cues of each community." Its governance proposals are aggregation/ranking interventions: "discounting synthetic engagement in ranking and revenue-sharing," audited bot-traffic metrics, user-side "AI shields" labelling "high swarm-likelihood" posts, real-time detection of "anomalous coordination patterns," and safeguards extended to "parties, campaigns, and officeholders" (P36, Schroeder et al. 2026).

Well-resourced actors could use agent swarms to "overwhelm public consultations" and generate "propaganda at scale," with the concern that the aggregate "public will may come to be shaped more heavily by delegated political action" than by direct human input (P33, Sharp et al. 2026, citing Goldberg et al. 2024; Summerfield et al. 2025). An "Influencer" agent could be "'seeded' by a human creator with a specific worldview" then run "thousands of real-time, personalized parasocial interactions to disseminate that worldview without further human oversight" (P28, Marchal et al. 2026). Real-world evidence: in the 2023 Argentine election both candidates used AI to generate "softfakes" — curating mood rather than argument (P26, Simon & Altay 2025, citing Chowdhury 2024; Nicas & Herrera 2023). Tempering the alarm, provider investigations "found little evidence that people widely shared the content," and "only around 1% of content flagged as misleading… is classified as AI-generated" (P40, Bengio et al. 2026).

16. What people want from AI's handling of political content (normative demands)

↑ top

Several studies elicit how publics think opinionated content should be handled.

  • Impartiality as the dominant demand. In STELA, "Impartial" is the single largest rule category (21.3%): chatbots should not be "biased in favour of or against a particular group," should "provide multiple perspectives… but clearly note if one side has a much stronger factual basis," and should not validate or contest users' beliefs; developer rulesets largely omit explicit political-impartiality rules (Impartial 0% for Claude/Llama 2, 4.2% for Sparrow) (P34, Bergman et al. 2024).
  • "Influential" is the most-opposed trait. "Influential" is the single most-opposed trait (18% oppose; 94% of mentions negative): respondents prefer AI to "leave the decision to the human," with an "all sides" epistemology including "non-mainstream" views "if there is no consensus"; the paper frames platform ranking as embedding "consequential value judgments about what content to amplify" (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026).
  • Disagreement over guardrails. A reject-censorship camp frames guardrails as "censorship"/anti-"free speech"; a pro-guardrails camp wants an Equality/Social Justice code (12%) (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026). The same tension appears in PRISM as "anti-woke" LLMs versus neutrality (P16, Kirk et al. 2024).
  • Disagreement is itself a finding. PRISM deliberately targets value-laden prompts (a "Controversy guided" type), surfacing political clusters (Election & Political Parties, Abortion, Immigration, Israel-Palestine, Gender & LGBTQ+, Race, Ethics of Death & Killing), and shows model rankings on controversial vs neutral domains diverge (P16, Kirk et al. 2024).
  • A roadmap example anchored in political moderation. The pluralistic-alignment roadmap uses "Is it ok for governments to moderate the social media content available to public?" as its motivating Figure 1, illustrating Overton, Steerable, and Distributional responses (P50, Sorensen et al. 2024).
  • Values in deployment. A Values-in-the-Wild audit of real Claude conversations shows that on charged history Claude foregrounds "historical accuracy" (residual 30.97) and "objectivity" rather than partisan stances; tech-ethics discussions foreground "human agency" (34.20); media-bias analysis is dominated by "truth-seeking" (28.99); its taxonomy carries aggregation-relevant values ("Balanced and fair discourse," "Constructive dialogue," "Democratic governance and institutions") (P49, Huang et al. 2025).

17. Governance, neutrality frameworks, and proposed architectures

↑ top

The normative and design proposals for how opinionated content should be aggregated or filtered.

  • Political neutrality is impossible but approximable. Three strategies: reasonable pluralism (aggregating multiple viewpoints in one response, via "Modular Pluralism" or debate-based generation); reflective neutrality (curating to the individual user's bias, via per-user LoRA adapters); and neutrality through diversity (ecosystem-level aggregation — Holmes' marketplace of ideas) (citing Feng et al. 2024; Li et al. 2024). Its benchmark (Political Compass statements, DEBATUNE, Bang et al. topics; forced partisan prefix; conspiracy good/bad-faith) finds GPT-4 gives reasonable pluralism on 99.3% of political-opinion prompts and 100% transparency when a partisan viewpoint is forced, while Llama/R1 emit undisclosed bias (8.1%/20.81%); it proposes aggregating user preferences ("democracy levels," Ovadya et al. 2024) (P48, Fisher et al. 2025, citing Zhang et al. 2024; Goyal et al. 2022; Sharma et al. 2023; Messer 2025).
  • A marketplace of epistemic models. Users "select systems based on their preferred alignment philosophies," requiring transparency and independent benchmarking; the same paper proposes a devil's-advocate intervention against groupthink (citing Fulay et al. 2025), and raises that societal alignment "might require centralized forms of moderation," called "fraught territory" (citing Tomasev et al. 2025; Aswad 2019) (P28, Marchal et al. 2026).
  • Equanimous-perspective steering. LLMs can be prompted/fine-tuned "to give equanimous perspectives on issues of debate," exposing users to "a spectrum of legitimate opinions" (P23, Summerfield et al. 2025).
  • Standards for curation. Recommendation systems "need to surface high-quality information that is both relevant and accurate" (P31, Gabriel & Keeling 2025, citing Stray et al. 2024).
  • Resilience interventions. Resist = media literacy + disclosure; Absorb = real-time detection labelling synthetic content; Recover = correction frameworks; Adapt = legal liability for undisclosed synthetic content (P40, Bengio et al. 2026).
  • Where partisan-content handling sits in risk taxonomies. The EU-facing analysis treats a widely used model's political bias as a reach-amplified risk to "public discourse and voter autonomy," notes the GPAI Code of Practice lists "risk to democratic processes" but treats these as secondary to frontier-capability risks, and places AI-curated recommendation out of scope as "indirect exposure" (P09, Röttger et al. 2025). The International AI Safety Report 2026 dropped the 2025 edition's standalone "bias" coverage, so it has no dedicated treatment of partisan bias in aggregation, ranking, or summarisation (P40, Bengio et al. 2026). The republican-theory framing treats discretionary promotion/downranking of congenial views as a domination concern requiring "digital citizen vigilance" (P02, Dunleavy & Margetts 2026). Content moderation is the explicit analogue for alignment (P12, Sepúlveda Coelho & Hale 2026), with developer rulesets "explicitly analogous to content moderation policies… in social media" (P34, Bergman et al. 2024). Accountability for aggregated output depends in part on how the producing AI is individuated (C05, Cappelen & Hawthorne c. 2025–2026).
  • Structural remedy. The Philosophic-Turn programme argues the remedy is decentralised truth-seeking rather than centrally controlled "digital rhetoric" (C01, Koralus 2025).

18. Adjacent and tangential mentions

↑ top

Several sources touch the theme only at the margin but contribute a distinct point. LLM filtering of a noisy UGC stream "filters out less relevant social media content and provides a higher signal-to-noise input for analysts" — but on factual event reports, not partisan stances (P11, Belisario et al. 2025). For personal advice, an LLM-autograder flags and regenerates harmful utterances — gatekeeping for safety, not political content (P27, Luettgau et al. 2026). A LLM-as-judge benchmark contains no political dimension; its relevance is only the general mechanism of judges as automated quality filters (P17, Padarha et al. 2025). An interpersonal-advice study contributes an adjacent method: LLM-as-a-judge classifying whether a response endorses a user's action, benchmarked against crowdsourced consensus (S07, Cheng et al. 2026). The "good digital society" report does not address selection/ranking/summarisation, mentioning misinformation only as a resilience challenge (P18, Hale et al. 2024). A study of AI-tool use and critical thinking has no political-content specificity (S08, Gerlich 2025). A modular-participation paper's routing/selection methods concern combining model outputs for general capability, not political content (Modular Pluralism; Value Kaleidoscope) (P44, Feng et al. 2026). One seminar topic — "using AI agents to facilitate human discussion" (Simon Cullen) — presupposes AI mediating political deliberation, readings TBD (A02, Koralus & McCord 2026). Tocqueville carries only oblique relevance: a single power "watch[ing] over their fate" prefigures a centralised intermediary pre-structuring what reaches citizens (C06, Tocqueville 1840), and accountability for curated outputs depends on AI individuation (C05, Cappelen & Hawthorne c. 2025–2026).

Toward measurement and neutrality norms

↑ top

A caveat runs underneath every finding above: what counts as bias, and how much is found, depends heavily on how political bias is defined and measured. Where a slant originates — pretraining data shaping downstream bias in fine-tuned models (LR06, Feng et al. 2023) — and how it is read off the model — integrative measurement triangulating LLM political preferences across instruments rather than a single test (LR01, Rozado 2025) — are choices that can move the headline result. The "Overton pluralism" norm (N01, Poole-Dayan et al. 2025) is one such choice made explicit: scoring the distribution of viewpoints an aggregator surfaces, rather than its distance from a fixed ideological centre. The next section, on measurement and neutrality norms, takes up these definitional choices directly.

Measurement and neutrality norms for LLM political bias

↑ top
On this section (9)
  • Instrument-centre norms
  • Partisan symmetry — matched pairs, party-proximity &amp; self-anchored
  • Even-handedness &amp; calibration
  • Distribution-matching norms
  • Pluralism / diversity norms
  • Perception / approval norms
  • Downstream-effects norms
  • Norm-sceptical &amp; validity critiques (and the measures they motivate)
  • Mediating factors &amp; quality control

How political bias in LLMs is defined and measured is itself contested. The literature splits into several families of "neutrality norm", each implying a different metric; the choice of norm largely determines what counts as bias. (Sources here are drawn from a dedicated review of measurement norms.)

Instrument-centre norms

↑ top

Treat a fixed test instrument’s neutral point as ground truth; bias = distance of the model’s position from that centre.

  • LR01 (Rozado, 2025, arXiv:2503.10649): Measuring Political Preferences in AI Systems — consistent left lean across probes [hybrid; moved here from family B]
  • LR02 (Liu, Panwang & Gu, 2025, HSSC 2025): "Turning right"? Political value shift in LLMs — stays libertarian-left but significant rightward drift over time
  • LR03 (Rozado, 2024, PLOS ONE 2024): The political preferences of LLMs — signed distance from c; left lean from fine-tuning
  • LR04 (Motoki et al., 2024, Public Choice 2024): More human than human — default–"avg Democrat" corr 0.96; placebo + dose-response pass
  • LR05 (Fujimoto & Takemoto, 2023, Front. AI 2023): Revisiting the political biases of ChatGPT — much weaker bias than Rozado; PCT is the outlier instrument
  • LR06 (Feng et al., 2023, ACL 2023 (Best Paper)): From Pretraining Data to LMs to Downstream Tasks — compass position tracks pretraining corpus → downstream unfairness
  • LR07 (Rutinowski et al., 2023, arXiv:2304.07333): The Self-Perception and Political Biases of ChatGPT — PCT ≈ (−6.5, −6.0): "progressive views"
  • LR08 (Rozado, 2023, Soc. Sci. 2023): The Political Biases of ChatGPT — # tests classifying non-centrist: 14/15 left
  • LR09 (Hartmann et al., 2023, arXiv:2301.01768): The political ideology of conversational AI — "would vote Green"; robust to negation/order/language

Partisan symmetry — matched pairs, party-proximity & self-anchored

↑ top

Define neutrality as symmetric treatment of matched left/right pairs or of partisan personas, rather than distance from a fixed centre.

  • LR10 (Gubelmann & Karray, 2025, ACL 2025): Parliamentary Voting Records NEWChen et al. · arXiv:2601.08785 · 2026arXiv PDF real motions (NL/NO/ES), each voted on individually; parties in Chapel Hill Expert Survey space a(M,p): vote agreement, projected to CHES axes relational position; entity-swap (EBI) shows systematic under-alignment toward right-conservative parties B2 Reliability & Political Bias in LLMs' Inference Judgements — error-rate gap; left-favouring asymmetry persists
  • LR11 (Exler et al., 2025, arXiv:2505.04393): Large Means Left — left alignment increases monotonically with parameters
  • LR12 (Vijay et al., 2025, AAAI 2025 (SA)): When Neutral Summaries are not that Neutral — P={diff}(D,D_N)-{diff}(R,D_N); pro-Democratic default lean
  • LR13 (Fulay et al., 2024, EMNLP 2024): On the Relationship between Truth and Political Bias — metric = Cohen's d on pooled L vs R group means + regression (not a within-pair ); truth-only training induces left lean
  • LR14 (Rettenberger et al., 2025, JCSS 2025): Assessing political bias in LLMs — large models lean Green/SPD/Linke, diverge from AfD; small ones neutral
  • LR15 (Bang et al., 2024, ACL 2024): What Is Said and How It Is Said — unbiased iff s<; framing bias judged cross-model-relative (no gold reference)
  • LR16 (Chalkidis & Brandl, 2024, NAACL 2024): Llama meets EU — left/green-proximate; party fine-tuning realigns it
  • LR17 (Ceron et al., 2024, TACL 2024): Beyond Prompt Brittleness (ProbVAA) — only reliable items interpreted; left overall but pro-law-and-order

Even-handedness & calibration

↑ top

Ask whether the model gives balanced, calibrated treatment across sides and refuses/hedges symmetrically.

  • LR18 (Anthropic, 2025, blog + eval): Measuring political bias in Claude — E=% "similarly helpful"; Claude Sonnet 4.5 ≈ 94%; refusal scored as failure
  • LR19 (OpenAI, 2025, report): Defining and evaluating political bias in LLMs — mean axis violation; GPT-5 ≈ 30% less than GPT-4o; accuracy outside rubric [hybrid: leans objectivity-norm]
  • LR20 (Liu et al., 2021, AAAI 2021 / Artif. Intell. 2022): Mitigating Political Bias through Reinforced Calibration — indirect = Wasserstein dep. on o; direct = L/R trigger gap; target 12

Distribution-matching norms

↑ top

Neutrality as matching some target distribution of views (e.g. population or corpus), not a single point.

  • LR21 (Damião et al., 2026, arXiv:2603.23474): Polarization by Default NEWPagan et al. · arXiv:2604.15937 · 2026arXiv PDF feature distribution of recommended posts the candidate pool ( p_i=p_{{rec},i}-p_{{pool},i}) Cramér's V / Cohen's d → R^2 D3 When Bigger Isn't Better NEWHuang, Maab & Yamagishi · ACL 2026arXiv PDF viewpoint representation in the summary the source set's viewpoint diversity (FairNews) 5 metrics — 3 source-relative (D3) + 2 absolute-balance (Neutralisation, Equal Fairness) D3+ Bias in search engines & LMs before elections
  • LR22 (Yoo & Shin, 2025, EMNLP 2025): Fair or Framed?
  • LR23 (Dai et al., 2025, EMNLP 2025): Media Source Matters More Than Content
  • LR24 (Meister et al., 2025, ICLR 2025): Benchmarking Distributional Alignment
  • LR25 (Onishi & Caverlee, 2024, BIAS@SIGIR 2024): Few-Shot News Summarization
  • LR26 (Durmus et al. (Anthropic), 2024, COLM 2024): GlobalOpinionQA — all frontier models 0.35–0.42; pooled coverage only 0.69
  • LR27 (Dominguez-Olmedo et al., 2024, NeurIPS 2024): Questioning the Survey Responses of LLMs — all frontier models 0.35–0.42; pooled coverage only 0.69
  • LR28 (Santurkar et al., 2023, ICML 2023): Whose Opinions Do LMs Reflect? (OpinionQA) — all frontier models 0.35–0.42; pooled coverage only 0.69
  • LR29 (Argyle et al., 2023, Political Analysis 2023): Out of One, Many — all frontier models 0.35–0.42; pooled coverage only 0.69
  • LR30 (Hu et al., 2019, WWW 2019): Auditing the Partisanship of Google Snippets — all frontier models 0.35–0.42; pooled coverage only 0.69
  • LR31 (Robertson et al., 2018, CSCW 2018): Auditing Partisan Audience Bias in Google Search — all frontier models 0.35–0.42; pooled coverage only 0.69

Pluralism / diversity norms

↑ top

Neutrality as representing the full range of legitimate perspectives (Overton pluralism, coverage, diversity).

  • LR32 (Poole-Dayan et al., 2025, arXiv:2512.01351): Benchmarking Overton Pluralism (OvertonBench) — all frontier models 0.35–0.42; pooled coverage only 0.69
  • LR33 (Wright et al., 2025, arXiv:2510.04226): Epistemic Diversity & Knowledge Collapse — all 27 models less diverse than basic search; size hurts
  • LR34 (Lake, Choi & Durrett, 2025, NAACL 2025): From Distributional to Overton Pluralism — "diversity loss" is largely quality control + aggregation [also cross-lists to family M]

Perception / approval norms

↑ top

Define bias by how users perceive/approve outputs (subjective fairness), not an external reference.

  • LR35 (Westwood, Grimmer & Hall, 2025, WP 2025): Political Neutrality as Balanced Approval NEWStray et al. · arXiv:2605.28911 · 2026arXiv PDF two issue-position groups (for/against), not parties s_g= Likert approval (0–1), 208k ratings Maximum Equal Approval; default GPT/Gemini/Claude/Llama lean liberal, Grok not Measuring Perceived Slant via User Evaluations — unbiased _g=0 g; 23/24 models perceived left

Downstream-effects norms

↑ top

Locate bias in measurable effects on users’ beliefs/decisions rather than in the text itself.

  • LR36 (Luettgau et al., 2025, arXiv:2509.05219): Perceived Bias Reduces Persuasive Abilities NEWDiGiuseppe & Robison · arXiv:2602.18092 · 2026arXiv PDF misconception-correction (persuasion) perceived bias randomly induced by warnings; content held constant out-party-bias warning attenuates persuasion 23–28% [moved from F: perceived bias is a treatment, not a norm] Conversational AI increases political knowledge — AI ≈ search (β diff ≈ 0.02) [epistemic knowledge-gain, not partisan slant — scope-stretch]
  • LR37 (Potter et al., 2024, EMNLP 2024): Hidden Persuaders — margin 0.7%→4.6%; "is neutrality the path forward?"
  • LR38 (Sharma, Liao & Xiao, 2024, CHI 2024): Generative Echo Chamber? — even a neutral RAG system raises confirmatory behaviour [moved from E: bias measured as user effect, not output coverage]
  • LR39 (Jakesch et al., 2023, CHI 2023): Co-Writing with Opinionated LMs — "latent persuasion": shifts both writing and views

Norm-sceptical & validity critiques (and the measures they motivate)

↑ top

Question whether common instruments measure anything stable/valid, and motivate more robust designs.

  • LR40 (Hagendorff, 2025, arXiv:2507.15328): Audits Capture Sycophancy to the Inferred Auditor NEWTörnberg & Schimmel · arXiv:2604.27633 · 2026arXiv PDF asker's stated identity a (only this varied) no fixed point: _M=f(a) — a response profile Dem-coded share falls 28–62pp; rightward accommodation 8× Inevitability of Left-Leaning Bias in Aligned LMs — left placement is alignment working as intended
  • LR41 (Faulborn et al., 2025, ACL 2025): Only a Little to the Left — lean is real but "only a little"; PCT inflates it [rival-measure, not pure critique]
  • LR42 (Wachter et al., 2025, arXiv:2503.13149): Are LLMs (Really) Ideological? (IRT) — models mostly avoid engagement rather than lean [rival-measure, not pure critique]
  • LR43 (Röttger et al., 2024, ACL 2024 (Outstanding)): Political Compass or Spinning Arrow? — answers change when unforced / reworded / paraphrased
  • LR44 (Blodgett et al., 2020, ACL 2020): Language (Technology) is Power — the gap this whole taxonomy responds to

Mediating factors & quality control

↑ top

Identify factors (prompting, language, decoding, scale) that move measured bias, and controls for them.

  • LR45 (Kuai et al., 2026, New Media & Society 2026): Chatbot Accountability & Gatekeeping — accuracy & balance anticorrelate (most accurate = most omission-biased)
  • LR46 (Anthropic, 2025, Nov 2025): Anthropic Paired Prompts — asymmetric quality is the bias (turns confound into definition)
  • LR47 (Gubelmann & Karray, 2025, ACL 2025): Equivalent Partisan Inferences — left-favouring asymmetry persists at fixed validity
  • LR48 (Germani & Spitale, 2025, arXiv:2505.13488): Source Framing Triggers Evaluation Bias — bias that cannot be quality — clean matched-content template
  • LR49 (Westwood, Grimmer & Hall, 2025, WP 2025): Perceived Slant (Westwood et al.) — neutrality & quality strongly correlated — but never partialled out
  • LR50 (Fulay et al., 2024, EMNLP 2024): Truth and Political Bias (TwinViews) — left lean survives the controls; truth↔politics entangled
  • LR51 (Jenny et al., 2023, arXiv:2311.08605): Exploring the Jungle of Bias — partisan gap largely indirect → "correlational estimates exaggerated"

Each source in the reading list appears here exactly once. The user's raw list contained eleven duplicate URLs (the same paper submitted under several routing names — e.g. the Science "Levers of Persuasion" paper listed twice, the PNAS "Scaling laws" paper three times), which were collapsed to unique IDs P01–P50 plus the framing/curriculum/supplementary anchors (A, C, S). Two further content-duplicate pairs are collapsed into single entries because each is one paper across two venues: P29/P37 (Tessler et al., "Can AI Mediation Improve Democratic Deliberation?") and P42/P43 (Lazar & Manuali, "Using LLMs to Enhance Democracy"); P35 (the Habermas-Machine Science experiment) is a separate paper and is kept distinct. Coverage: 66 unique sources.

The entries are grouped into nine themes. Several papers speak to more than one; each is placed under its strongest fit.

Theme 1 — Framing: AI, autonomy, and the stakes for the information environment

↑ top
On this section (9)
  • A01 — Mosleh, M., Traberg, C. S., Wang, N., Kunst, J., &amp; Hansson, K. (2026). Artificial Influence [Research Proposal, NFRFI-2026-00940]. New Frontiers in Research Fund (Canada).
  • A02 — Koralus, P. &amp; McCord, B. (2026). Autonomy in the Age of Agents — Philosophy, AI, and Innovation Graduate Seminar (Trinity Term 2026, weeks 1–8). Human-Centered AI Lab, University of Oxford &amp; Cosmos Institute. Syllabus/curriculum.
  • C01 — Koralus, P. (2025). The philosophic turn for AI agents: replacing centralized digital rhetoric with decentralized truth-seeking. Mind &amp; Society. DOI: 10.1007/s11299-025-00326-z (arXiv:2504.18601).
  • C02 — McCord, B. &amp; Koralus, P. (2026). Same Radio, Different Citizens: On the Economics of Human Formation. Cosmos Institute blog, 16 January 2026.
  • C06 — Tocqueville, A. de (1840). Democracy in America, Vol. 2, Part 4, Ch. VI ("What Sort of Despotism Democratic Nations Have to Fear"). Trans. H. Reeve.
  • P23 — Summerfield, C., Argyle, L. P., Bakker, M., et al. (2025). The impact of advanced AI systems on democracy. Nature Human Behaviour, 9(12), 2420–2430.
  • P40 — Bengio, Y. (Chair) et al. (2026). International AI Safety Report 2026. Second edition. UK DSIT, with the AI Security Institute and Mila.
  • P20 — Yang, M., Casper, S., Stray, J., et al. (2026). AI Epistemic Risks: Emerging Mechanisms &amp; Evidence. SSRN Working Paper. doi:10.2139/ssrn.6873005.
  • S01 — Jungherr, A. (2023). Artificial Intelligence and Democracy: A Conceptual Framework. Social Media + Society, 9(3), 1–14.

These works set the conceptual stage — characterising AI as a new intermediary in belief formation, supplying the normative vocabulary (autonomy, soft despotism, epistemic health), and providing the authoritative syntheses and policy frames the rest of the literature operationalises.

A01 — Mosleh, M., Traberg, C. S., Wang, N., Kunst, J., & Hansson, K. (2026). Artificial Influence [Research Proposal, NFRFI-2026-00940]. New Frontiers in Research Fund (Canada).

↑ top

An interdisciplinary proposal (lead: Mohsen Mosleh, OII) arguing AI/LLMs have become "an active participant in users' belief-formation processes" and asking how ubiquitous AI will reshape how political views are formed, maintained, and communicated globally. It proposes three objectives across Canada/UK/Scandinavia: a cross-platform observational baseline of LLM-driven homogenization (including a Grok-on-X difference-in-differences exploiting X's March-2026 restriction of "Ask Grok" to Premium users); longitudinal behavioural tracking plus browser-extension field experiments on LLM feed re-ranking and summaries; and controlled human–AI–human deliberation studies. It cites high adoption (a majority of Swedes used a chatbot last year, >25% weekly) and frames the open question as whether AI will moderate or intensify polarization. Motivation: AI now supplies ready-made interpretations and framings at scale, yet we know "very little about [its] longer-term consequences for polarization, critical thinking, and democratic resilience," and its effects may soon become "increasingly difficult to reverse."

A02 — Koralus, P. & McCord, B. (2026). Autonomy in the Age of Agents — Philosophy, AI, and Innovation Graduate Seminar (Trinity Term 2026, weeks 1–8). Human-Centered AI Lab, University of Oxford & Cosmos Institute. Syllabus/curriculum.

↑ top

A TT26 Oxford graduate-seminar syllabus framing its central question as "dimensions of human autonomy in the age of AI agents," with discussants including Jack Clark, Joe Edelman, Herman Cappelen, Simon Cullen, and Thomas Wolf. Its eight weeks map a curated arc from paradigms for autonomy-serving AI and the economics of "human formation" through institutional value drift, AI-facilitated discussion, open-source agents, and the metaphysics of individuation. It treats the present as an "underdetermined" equilibrium in which choices made now will durably shape human cognition and democracy, and functions as a structured map of the literature this review covers. Motivation: AI agents are becoming ubiquitous intermediaries that threaten autonomy in two ways — overwhelming people into ceding agency, or silently steering their judgments via externally controlled choice architectures — and the design/economics are not yet locked in.

C01 — Koralus, P. (2025). The philosophic turn for AI agents: replacing centralized digital rhetoric with decentralized truth-seeking. Mind & Society. DOI: 10.1007/s11299-025-00326-z (arXiv:2504.18601).

↑ top

A normative philosophy paper (no new data) arguing that mass delegation to AI agents poses a dilemma: lose agency (overwhelmed) or lose autonomy (choices conditioned by automated framing — "autocomplete for life"). Using "nudge"/choice-architecture philosophy as a small-scale precursor, Koralus argues that fused with large-scale AI it becomes "a subtle but sweeping form of digital rhetoric" that can "invisibly shape the collective mind," turning soft paternalism into "soft totalitarianism" when centrally controlled (citing publication-bias shrinkage of framing effects). His positive proposal is a "philosophic turn": AI that mirrors Socratic dialogue, helps users "weakly seek erotetic equilibrium," and preserves autonomy via privacy, decentralised ownership, and modularity. Motivation: Ubiquitous AI decision-support will leave people either ineffective (no agency) or silently steered (no autonomy), so the design of AI agents will determine whether human reason and self-rule survive at scale.

C02 — McCord, B. & Koralus, P. (2026). Same Radio, Different Citizens: On the Economics of Human Formation. Cosmos Institute blog, 16 January 2026.

↑ top

A conceptual essay arguing information technologies cannot be judged "in themselves"; their effects are set by funding and governance. Its anchor: the same radio produced three different citizenries — the BBC (license fee, "designed to stretch"), US commercial radio (ad-funded, "cleared at entertainment"), and Soviet radio (state consensus manufacture). Applying Marr's three levels to institutions, the authors warn of "structural drift" (avowed aims become "ghosts" via Goodhart's law as economics select for monetizable engagement) and propose two design tests (Transparent Choice, Candid Aim), cautioning that frontier AI "could inherit" the attention-economy attractor if deployed as "next-generation attention routers." Motivation: Funding models are "de facto governance regimes" that determine which part of a technology's possibility space becomes reality, and frontier AI's equilibrium is still underdetermined — a pivotal moment to configure the stack deliberately.

C06 — Tocqueville, A. de (1840). Democracy in America, Vol. 2, Part 4, Ch. VI ("What Sort of Despotism Democratic Nations Have to Fear"). Trans. H. Reeve.

↑ top

The canonical prophecy that egalitarian democracies risk a new, mild despotism the old vocabulary cannot name: atomised citizens withdraw into private pleasures, and "an immense and tutelary power" — "absolute, minute, regular, provident, and mild" — assumes charge of their fate, keeping them "in perpetual childhood." The mechanism is administrative, not violent ("a net-work of small complicated rules" that "compresses, enervates, extinguishes"), working on the will, which is "not shattered, but softened, bent, and guided"; periodic voting cannot prevent people "gradually losing the faculties of thinking, feeling, and acting." Motivation: Liberty can be lost without anyone intending tyranny — through atomisation, the seductiveness of being cared for, and the slow atrophy of the capacity to think and act for oneself — the frame the report invokes for ubiquitous AI that pre-digests citizens' choices.

P23 — Summerfield, C., Argyle, L. P., Bakker, M., et al. (2025). The impact of advanced AI systems on democracy. Nature Human Behaviour, 9(12), 2420–2430.

↑ top

A Perspective synthesising how frontier AI affects democracy across three classes of impact: epistemic (bias, persuasion, polarization, deliberation, mis/disinformation), material (electoral disruption, augmenting decision-making), and foundational (power concentration, accountability, "algocracy"). Its empirical centrepiece (Fig. 1) meta-analytically compiles 13 RCTs (n≈900 to ≈77,000): LLM messages match human writers; multi-turn dialogue yields 2–14 pp gains (strongest debunking conspiracies and when delivering high evidence volume); persuasion persists even when users know the source is AI; microtargeting adds "very limited" effect. On bias, multiple-choice probes show a progressive/libertarian lean post-safety-tuning, but free-response everyday answers are typically balanced. It stresses that almost all evidence is output-only lab work and calls for naturalistic field measurement. Motivation: Frontier AI now mediates political information for hundreds of millions, yet its net effect on democratic self-governance is unknown and contested.

P40 — Bengio, Y. (Chair) et al. (2026). International AI Safety Report 2026. Second edition. UK DSIT, with the AI Security Institute and Mila.

↑ top

The second multi-government scientific assessment of general-purpose AI (30+ authors, 100+ experts, 30+ countries), synthesising evidence with no policy recommendations. Its influence/manipulation chapter (§2.1.2) reports AI persuades at least as well as non-expert humans and sometimes matches experts, with a compiled effect-size table (conspiracy belief −16.5 pp persisting at 2 months; propaganda +21.2 pp AI vs +23 pp human; writing-with-AI shifting beliefs +13 pp with <30% aware), and persuasiveness scaling ~+1.8 pp per 10× training compute. Autonomy harms (§2.3.2) include a ~6% drop in clinicians' unaided tumour detection and cognitive-offloading effects; real-world AI influence is documented but "not yet widespread" (~1% of flagged misleading content is AI-generated). Adoption: ~700M weekly ChatGPT users. Motivation: Policymakers face an "evidence dilemma" — AI capabilities outpace measurement of their effects — so a shared evidence baseline is needed on frontier emerging risks where understanding is thinnest.

P20 — Yang, M., Casper, S., Stray, J., et al. (2026). AI Epistemic Risks: Emerging Mechanisms & Evidence. SSRN Working Paper. doi:10.2139/ssrn.6873005.

↑ top

A ~30-author synthesis (incl. Bengio, Rand, Pennycook, Stray, Hackenburg, Kirk, Kasirzadeh) defining epistemic risks — threats to "humanity's collective capacity to form accurate beliefs, reason well, and maintain a healthy information environment" — as AI integrates "into the infrastructure through which individuals and societies think." Its contribution is a taxonomy of three compounding mechanisms: persuasion and manipulation (incl. sycophancy); cognitive offloading (qualitatively new vs prior tools, risking atrophy); and human-AI / AI-AI feedback loops that narrow "epistemic space," driving homogenization and possibly fragmentation. It surfaces a homogenization-vs-fragmentation tension (hyper-personalisation siloing users while accuracy-driven defaults converge content toward a narrow centre). (Accessed abstract-only; body effect sizes not extractable.) Motivation: As AI becomes embedded in collective sense-making it introduces a distinct class of harms that should be named, evidenced, and governed coherently rather than piecemeal.

S01 — Jungherr, A. (2023). Artificial Intelligence and Democracy: A Conceptual Framework. Social Media + Society, 9(3), 1–14.

↑ top

A conceptual paper (no new data) building a four-domain framework — individual self-rule, group equality, elections, and democratic-vs-autocratic systems competition — and insisting on analysing deployed narrow AI rather than speculative AGI. A key technical claim is that because AI learns "typical patterns" and "leans toward averages," minority/outsider political positions become "submerged and invisible," weakening democracies' information-processing capacity. He notes filter-bubble fears "may be overblown," stresses opacity and power concentration, and warns that perceived AI power (e.g. the debunked Cambridge Analytica claims) can erode legitimacy even absent real effects. Motivation: "AI and democracy" discourse is dominated by AGI speculation divorced from how AI works; a disciplined framework tied to specific deployed capabilities lets researchers and regulators govern real mechanisms.
