  • S02 — Salvi, F., Horta Ribeiro, M., Gallotti, R., &amp; West, R. (2024/2025). On the Conversational Persuasiveness of Large Language Models: A Randomized Controlled Trial. Nature Human Behaviour (2025); arXiv:2403.14380.
  • S04 — Costello, T. H., Pennycook, G., &amp; Rand, D. G. (2024). Durably reducing conspiracy beliefs through dialogues with AI. Science, 385(6714), eadq1814.
  • P24 — Luettgau, L., Kirk, H. R., Hackenburg, K., et al. (2026). Conversational AI increases political knowledge as effectively as self-directed internet search. arXiv:2509.05219.
  • P46 — Fisher, J., Feng, S., Aron, R., Richardson, T., Choi, Y., Fisher, D. W., Pan, J., Tsvetkov, Y., &amp; Reinecke, K. (2025). Biased LLMs can Influence Political Decision-Making. ACL 2025 (Long Papers), 6559–6607.

The most empirically developed theme: RCTs measuring how persuasive LLM-generated and conversational messages are on political attitudes and real behaviour, what drives persuasion (information density, post-training — not scale or personalization), and the persuasion–accuracy trade-off.

P19 — Hackenburg, K., Wagner, C., Hewitt, L., Tappin, B. M., Saunders, E., Kirk, H. R., Margetts, H., & Summerfield, C. (2026). AI systems out-persuade expert humans. arXiv:2606.16475.

↑ top

Four preregistered, single-blind experiments (18,978 conversations; 6,923 persuadees) pit frontier AI against random laypeople, tournament-selected top-decile laypeople, professional canvassers (~10,000 career conversations), and elite debaters (world/continental champions). AI beat every human class — random laypeople +8.2 pp, selected +5.6 pp, canvassers +5.9 pp, elite debaters +4.6 pp (all p<.001) — despite humans receiving up to £1,000 and hours of prep; coaching debaters did not help (+1.0 pp). Constraining AI to human message length (~55 words) and speed collapsed its advantage to zero, implicating information throughput (fact density predicted persuasion, R²=0.90 within AI). AI also raised ~3× more real charity donations than canvassers. Motivation: Many societal contests are settled by persuasion, and if AI out-persuades even the most skilled, trained, incentivised humans, outcomes could hinge on AI access rather than the merits of a case.

P21 — Hackenburg, K., Tappin, B. M., Hewitt, L., Saunders, E., Black, S., Lin, H., Fist, C., Margetts, H., Rand, D. G., & Summerfield, C. (2025). The levers of political persuasion with conversational artificial intelligence. Science, 390(6777), eaea3884.

↑ top

The largest study of its kind: three preregistered experiments (total N=76,977 UK adults) deployed 19 LLMs over 707 political issues and fact-checked 466,769 claims. Persuasion is driven mainly by post-training (up to +51%) and prompting (up to +27%), far less by personalization (+0.43 pp, never >1 pp) or scale; conversation beats static messages, and the mechanism is information density (each extra fact-checkable claim adds +0.30 pp; r=0.76, explaining 44% of cross-condition variance). Crucially, the same techniques that raise persuasion lower factual accuracy (information-prompted GPT-4o: 62% vs 78% accurate), and effects are durable (36–42% persist at one month); a "maximally persuasive" AI reached 15.9 pp with a 29.7% inaccuracy rate. Motivation: Safeguards aimed at model scale may target the wrong thing; identifying which "levers" actually drive AI persuasion — and their accuracy cost — shows where risk truly concentrates.

P08 — Hackenburg, K., Tappin, B. M., Röttger, P., Hale, S. A., Bright, J., & Margetts, H. (2025). Scaling language model size yields diminishing returns for single-message political persuasion. PNAS, 122(10), e2413443122.

↑ top

A preregistered survey experiment (N=25,982 US adults) generating 720 persuasive messages from 24 models (70M–72B, plus GPT-4-Turbo and Claude-3-Opus) across 10 US issues. The average-size model shifts attitudes 5.77 pp; each unit of log(parameters) adds only 1.26 pp, and frontier models are not significantly more persuasive than models an order of magnitude smaller or than humans. Only "task completion" (coherent, on-topic, on-stance writing) predicts persuasion; adjusting for it makes model size non-significant, and frontier models already sit at the task-completion ceiling. A log-logistic fit implies even sharper diminishing returns (extrapolating to 30T params adds <1.25 pp). Motivation: Fears that LLM persuasiveness will keep climbing with scale need testing, but persuasion can only be measured by real attitude change — so it was unclear how fast it actually scales.

P22 — Hackenburg, K., Hewitt, L., Wagner, C., Summerfield, C., & Tappin, B. M. (2026). Artificial intelligence can persuade people to take political actions. arXiv:2604.09200.

↑ top

Two preregistered experiments (17,950 responses; 14,779 UK adults) test whether AI persuades people to take consequential actions, not just shift attitudes. Frontier LLMs raised real petition signing by +12.8 pp (Study 1) and +19.7 pp (Study 2), donations by +4.9 pp, and org-loyalty by +11.3 pp, with effects comparable to or larger than face-to-face canvassing (a combined "Mega" strategy reached +23.7 pp). Attitude and behaviour effects were uncorrelated (r=0.05–0.18), and the mechanisms differed: information-provision drove attitudes but was least effective on behaviour; attitude effects concentrated among non-supporters, behavioural effects among prior supporters. Motivation: AI-persuasion research has almost entirely measured attitudes, which need not track behaviour, so attitude-only work may substantially mischaracterise AI's real-world behavioural impact.

P25 — Hackenburg, K., Ibrahim, L., Tappin, B. M., & Tsakiris, M. (2025). Comparing the persuasiveness of role-playing LLMs and human experts on polarized U.S. political issues. AI & SOCIETY. DOI: 10.1007/s00146-025-02464-x.

↑ top

A preregistered between-subjects experiment (n=4,955 US adults) testing GPT-4 messages — plain and partisan role-played — against human political-consultant messages on three polarized issues (vaccine mandates, "rigged elections," deportations). LLM messages consistently out-persuaded human experts, sometimes by >6 pp, but partisan role-play conferred no significant aggregate advantage over non-role-play (1.2 pp, p=0.258), and partisanship alignment gave little advantage over misalignment. The LLM edge was concentrated on right-leaning ("for issue") messaging; participants identified the role-playing LLM's partisanship only 46% of the time. Motivation: If sophisticated prompting lets LLMs rival genuine human experts, it portends adoption of generative AI by formal political campaigns rather than only fringe groups.

S02 — Salvi, F., Horta Ribeiro, M., Gallotti, R., & West, R. (2024/2025). On the Conversational Persuasiveness of Large Language Models: A Randomized Controlled Trial. Nature Human Behaviour (2025); arXiv:2403.14380.

↑ top

A preregistered RCT (N=820, US) with a 2×2 design (opponent type × personalization) in live 10-minute debates over 30 topics. GPT-4 with access to the opponent's demographics had +81.7% higher odds of greater post-debate agreement than human-human debates (p<0.01), and remained significant vs plain Human-AI (p=0.04); plain Human-AI out-persuaded humans non-significantly (+21.3%). Human-human debates produced a backfire/hardening effect; Republicans were more persuadable (+60%); GPT-4 used more analytic/logical language while humans used more first-person and emotional language, and participants identified AI opponents ~75% of the time. Motivation: Prior work compared static texts, leaving open how persuasive LLMs are in direct conversation — especially with cheap, scalable personalization, the consequential commercial use case.

S04 — Costello, T. H., Pennycook, G., & Rand, D. G. (2024). Durably reducing conspiracy beliefs through dialogues with AI. Science, 385(6714), eadq1814.

↑ top

Two preregistered experiments (2,190 conspiracy believers) had participants articulate a theory and its evidence, then debate GPT-4 Turbo ("DebunkBot") for ~8 minutes with tailored counterarguments. Belief in the chosen theory fell ~20% vs control, with ~1 in 4 crossing into uncertainty; the effect persisted undiminished at two months, generalised across theories (including 2020 election fraud and COVID), and spilled over to general conspiratorial mindset. A fact-checker rated 127/128 sampled model claims true (~99.2% accurate), and the authors flag the dual-use risk of persuading people into false beliefs. (Built from abstract + cross-validated secondary sources, not the primary PDF.) Motivation: "Post-truth" pessimism holds conspiracy beliefs to be sticky and identity-driven; this tests the alternative that believers simply lacked tailored counter-evidence that an LLM can now supply at scale.

P24 — Luettgau, L., Kirk, H. R., Hackenburg, K., et al. (2026). Conversational AI increases political knowledge as effectively as self-directed internet search. arXiv:2509.05219.

↑ top

A nationally representative UK survey (N=2,499) plus RCTs (N=2,858) around the July 2024 election. 9% used chatbots for political information in the prior four weeks (implying up to ~13% of eligible voters for electorally relevant info), and most rated them useful (89%), accurate (87%) and neutral (62%). Researching topics raised true-belief and lowered misinformation belief, but the effect was nearly identical for conversational AI vs Google search (AI-vs-search interaction ≈0, inside the ROPE). Sycophantic/persuasive prompting did not change belief effects, and both AI and search shifted people toward progressive-leaning factual statements comparably. Motivation: As AI replaces search for information-seeking, fears that biased/hallucinated outputs misinform voters need testing — both whether people trust AI for political info and whether it functionally substitutes for search.

P46 — Fisher, J., Feng, S., Aron, R., Richardson, T., Choi, Y., Fisher, D. W., Pan, J., Tsvetkov, Y., & Reinecke, K. (2025). Biased LLMs can Influence Political Decision-Making. ACL 2025 (Long Papers), 6559–6607.

↑ top

Two interactive randomized experiments (N=299 US citizens; 150 Republicans, 149 Democrats) had participants chat freely with a liberal-, conservative-, or neutral-biased GPT-3.5-turbo. Participants shifted opinions and budget allocations toward the model's bias regardless of their own partisanship — including against their stated affiliation (e.g. Democrats with a conservative model significantly increased conservative-topic support, β=0.98, p=.007; budget ANOVA p<.001). Self-reported prior AI knowledge weakly reduced the effect; recognizing the bias did not (only 54% identified it). Biased models differed via framing, not different persuasion techniques. Motivation: LLM bias is well-documented but its effect on real decisions is under-studied and prior results are mixed; this measures it in the realistic setting of dynamic chat on personal political values.

Theme 3 — Homogenization and knowledge collapse

↑ top
On this section (2)
  • P45 — Jiang, L., Chai, Y., Li, M., Liu, M., Fok, R., Dziri, N., Tsvetkov, Y., Sap, M., &amp; Choi, Y. (2025). Artificial Hivemind: The Open-Ended Homogeneity of Language Models (and Beyond). NeurIPS 2025 Datasets &amp; Benchmarks.
  • S10 — Yakura, H., Lopez-Lopez, E., Brinkmann, L., Serna, I., Gupta, P., Soraperra, I., &amp; Rahwan, I. (2025). Empirical evidence of Large Language Model's influence on human spoken communication. arXiv:2409.01754v3.

These sources measure and theorise convergence: of AI-generated text (within and across models) and — via cultural feedback loops — of human language itself, framing homogenization as a long-run epistemic-safety risk.

P45 — Jiang, L., Chai, Y., Li, M., Liu, M., Fok, R., Dziri, N., Tsvetkov, Y., Sap, M., & Choi, Y. (2025). Artificial Hivemind: The Open-Ended Homogeneity of Language Models (and Beyond). NeurIPS 2025 Datasets & Benchmarks.

↑ top

Introduces INFINITY-CHAT (26K open-ended WildChat prompts; 31,250 dense human annotations, 25 raters each) and documents an "Artificial Hivemind" across 70+ LMs with two facets: intra-model repetition (in 79% of queries a model's 50 responses average pairwise similarity >0.8, even at temperature 1.0) and inter-model homogeneity (cross-model similarity 71–82%; DeepSeek-V3 and GPT-4o reach 0.81; for "write a metaphor about time," 25 models × 50 responses collapse into two clusters). It further shows reward models and LM judges, well-calibrated overall, become poorly calibrated on high-disagreement same-quality responses (reward-model ρ falls from 0.330 to as low as −0.073). Motivation: LMs fail to produce the diversity expected in open-ended tasks, risking long-term homogenization of human thought as users are repeatedly exposed to similar outputs.

S10 — Yakura, H., Lopez-Lopez, E., Brinkmann, L., Serna, I., Gupta, P., Soraperra, I., & Rahwan, I. (2025). Empirical evidence of Large Language Model's influence on human spoken communication. arXiv:2409.01754v3.

↑ top

Tracks "GPT words" (e.g. delve, underscore, meticulous) across 740,249 hours / 7.35 billion words of human speech (360,445 YouTube talks + 771,591 podcasts) before/after ChatGPT's release, using synthetic control and difference-in-differences. It finds an abrupt post-release rise in usage (delve placebo p=0.010, peaking exactly at the release date), with many top words growing ~25–50%/year; the effect generalises to spontaneous podcast speech but is domain-dependent (significant in Science/Tech, Business, Education; not Religion or Sports). The authors read this as the start of a "closed cultural feedback loop" and flag linguistic homogenisation and model collapse. Motivation: Generative AI encodes and disseminates cultural patterns to hundreds of millions, but whether those machine-originated patterns flow back into human language was largely unmeasured — here shown for spoken communication.

Theme 4 — AI-mediated communication, feed curation, and polarization

↑ top
On this section (7)
  • P01 — Tsirtsis, S., Rawal, K., Russell, C., Mittelstadt, B., &amp; Wachter, S. (2026). AI-Mediated Communication Can Steer Collective Opinion. arXiv:2605.16245.
  • P05 — Röttger, P., Hackenburg, K., Kirk, H. R., &amp; Summerfield, C. (2026). Measuring and mitigating persona distortions from AI writing assistance. arXiv:2604.22503.
  • P03 — Renault, T., Mosleh, M., &amp; Rand, D. G. (2025). @Grok Is This True? LLM-Powered Fact-Checking on Social Media. PsyArXiv. DOI: 10.31234/osf.io/85quw_v1.
  • S14 — Piccardi, T., Saveski, M., Jia, C., Hancock, J., Tsai, J. L., &amp; Bernstein, M. S. (2025). Reranking partisan animosity in algorithmic social media feeds alters affective polarization. Science, 390(6776), eadu5584.
  • S09 — Argyle, L. P., Busby, E. C., Gubler, J. R., Bail, C. A., Howe, T., Rytting, C., Sorensen, T., &amp; Wingate, D. (2023). Leveraging artificial intelligence for democratic discourse: Chat interventions can improve online political conversations at scale. PNAS, 120(41), e2311627120.
  • S11 — Mosleh, M., Allen, J., &amp; Rand, D. G. (2025). Divergent patterns of engagement with partisan and low-quality news across seven social media platforms. PNAS, 122(44), e2425739122.
  • P11 — Belisario, A., Hale, S. A., &amp; Rocher, L. (2025). Into the Crossfire: Evaluating the Use of a Language Model to Crowdsource Gun Violence Reports. PACMHCI (CSCW), Article CSCW235.

Here AI sits between people and content — improving or biasing human-to-human messages, re-ranking feeds, fact-checking, or triaging — and the empirical question is how that mediation shifts collective opinion, conversation quality, and affective polarization (in either direction).

P01 — Tsirtsis, S., Rawal, K., Russell, C., Mittelstadt, B., & Wachter, S. (2026). AI-Mediated Communication Can Steer Collective Opinion. arXiv:2605.16245.

↑ top

Combines an output audit, an opinion-dynamics model, and a network simulation. Four open-weight LLMs instructed to "improve" posts while preserving opinion nonetheless introduced statistically significant directional biases across 13 contested topics (e.g. toward gun control, against atheism; gemma-3-12b-it biased on all 13). Extending the Friedkin–Johnsen model, the per-user bias is amplified through the network — up to 9.2× the one-off bias in real SNAP-network simulations (~80k Twitter nodes) — and a case study of X's "Explain this post" (grok-4-1-fast on 78 abortion posts) finds a directional pro-life bias (β_sup=0.24) traceable to one guideline in X's prompt template. Motivation: AI increasingly mediates human-written political content rather than producing standalone text, and how those biases aggregate into collective opinion is underexplored and possibly beyond current EU regulation.

P05 — Röttger, P., Hackenburg, K., Kirk, H. R., & Summerfield, C. (2026). Measuring and mitigating persona distortions from AI writing assistance. arXiv:2604.22503.

↑ top

Three large experiments (N=2,939 writers; N=11,091 readers; 2.9M ratings) test whether AI writing assistance distorts a writer's perceived persona on political opinion paragraphs. Writers preferred AI text to their own 63.0% of the time and edited it only 23% of the time; AI produced significant distortions on all 29 perception dimensions (more extreme +4.3, more knowledgeable +17.0, more informative +22.7, demographically more privileged ×5.3 odds more educated) and homogenised reader perceptions (lower variance on 22/29). A disclaimer did not change behaviour, but model-level reranking cut the polarising stance-extremity distortion by 54.6% (at a cost to user preference and to valued distortions). Motivation: AI writing tools used by hundreds of millions systematically misrepresent who a writer is and what they believe, and at scale even modest shifts could accumulate into widespread misattribution of stance, credibility, and identity.

P03 — Renault, T., Mosleh, M., & Rand, D. G. (2025). @Grok Is This True? LLM-Powered Fact-Checking on Social Media. PsyArXiv. DOI: 10.31234/osf.io/85quw_v1.

↑ top

A large-scale study of LLM "fact-checking" on X: 1,555,281 English fact-check requests (Feb–Sep 2025) tagging @Grok or @AskPerplexity, skewing political (Politics 21.1%, War 15.8%). It finds partisan usage asymmetry (Grok requesters +58.1% Republican; Perplexity +18% Democratic), both parties fact-checking Republican-authored posts 95.8% more (which all models rate less accurate), and LLMs roughly matching human fact-checkers (Grok-3 57.9% agreement vs a 59.3% inter-checker benchmark) but subverted by source restriction (Truth Social / Fox-type sources drop accuracy to 47.7%). A preregistered survey (N=1,592) shows LLM verdicts shift belief accuracy (false verdict −0.12/−32%; true verdict +0.28/+60%), but disclosing Grok's identity polarizes updating. Motivation: LLMs are increasingly deployed as social-media fact-checkers, yet whether polarized/selective use and prompt/source manipulation undercut their corrective power is an open empirical question.

S14 — Piccardi, T., Saveski, M., Jia, C., Hancock, J., Tsai, J. L., & Bernstein, M. S. (2025). Reranking partisan animosity in algorithmic social media feeds alters affective polarization. Science, 390(6776), eadu5584.

↑ top

A platform-independent browser extension intercepts X's "For you" feed and uses GPT-3.5 Turbo to score and rerank posts on eight factors of "antidemocratic attitudes and partisan animosity" (AAPA). In a preregistered 10-day field experiment (N=1,256) during the 2024 campaign, reducing AAPA exposure warmed out-party feeling by +2.11° (p=0.035) and increasing it cooled feeling by −2.48° (p=0.036) — each shift ~equivalent to 3–3.6 years of real-world polarization change, with no partisan asymmetry; down-ranking slightly reduced engagement and 74.2% did not notice the intervention. Motivation: Only platforms can usually test feed-ranking effects, and they face constraining incentives; an independent reranker lets external researchers run causal field tests of whether amplifying polarizing content drives affective polarization.

S09 — Argyle, L. P., Busby, E. C., Gubler, J. R., Bail, C. A., Howe, T., Rytting, C., Sorensen, T., & Wingate, D. (2023). Leveraging artificial intelligence for democratic discourse: Chat interventions can improve online political conversations at scale. PNAS, 120(41), e2311627120.

↑ top

A preregistered RCT (N=1,574 US) in which a GPT-3 assistant offered one partner in ideologically opposed gun-policy dyads real-time rewrites (restatement, validation, politeness); ~two-thirds of 2,742 suggestions were accepted, raising politeness/acknowledgment features without changing topic. Partners of treated users reported ~4 pp higher conversation quality and up to ~6 pp higher democratic reciprocity, with effects strongest for the partner (not the tool user). Crucially, there was no change in policy attitudes and no persistence at ~3 months. Motivation: "Conversation is the soul of democracy," but evidence-based de-escalation techniques (trained moderators) do not scale; embedding them in an always-available LLM assistant solves implementation at scale without manipulating beliefs.

S11 — Mosleh, M., Allen, J., & Rand, D. G. (2025). Divergent patterns of engagement with partisan and low-quality news across seven social media platforms. PNAS, 122(44), e2425739122.

↑ top

A cross-platform analysis of >10 million January-2024 news-link posts across X, BlueSky, TruthSocial, Gab, GETTR, Mastodon, and LinkedIn. More-conservative platforms carry lower-quality news (r=−0.929); partisan-lean × engagement is strongly heterogeneous (no universal "right-wing advantage" — conservative news wins on conservative platforms and vice versa, an "echo platform" pattern), but quality × engagement is strikingly consistent: a given user's lower-quality posts get ~7% more engagement than their higher-quality posts (b=−0.011, p<0.001), holding within-poster and even on Mastodon's chronological feed. The authors infer user preferences, not algorithmic bias, plausibly drive higher-quality news's underperformance. Motivation: Single-platform findings may be artefacts; testing which engagement patterns are universal vs platform-specific disentangles whether platforms inherently advantage inaccurate/partisan content or whether the patterns reflect human preferences. (Concerns social-media news, not LLM mediation.)

P11 — Belisario, A., Hale, S. A., & Rocher, L. (2025). Into the Crossfire: Evaluating the Use of a Language Model to Crowdsource Gun Violence Reports. PACMHCI (CSCW), Article CSCW235.

↑ top

The first real-world evaluation of a language model filtering/triaging social-media data for human-rights analysts, with the Brazilian NGO Fogo Cruzado. A fine-tuned BERTimbau classifier separating gun-violence reports from ordinary Brazilian-Portuguese tweets reached Precision 0.94 / Recall 0.87 / F1 0.90 (95% human agreement); a live difference-in-differences intervention with five Rio analysts (vs a Bahia control) was associated with ~9 more analyst interactions/day with gun-violence reports (mean daily 17→24 vs 6→4 control). Analysts rated it good/excellent and ran it alongside, not instead of, their workflow; the model struggled with long text and emojis. Motivation: Reliable firearm-event data is scarce and manual social-media monitoring is dangerous and labour-intensive, so the paper tests whether an LM can raise signal-to-noise while honestly assessing selection bias, sustainability, and access inequity.

Theme 5 — Representational bias and WEIRD skew

↑ top
On this section (6)
  • P04 — Röttger, P., Hinck, M., Hofmann, V., Hackenburg, K., Pyatkin, V., Brahman, F., &amp; Hovy, D. (2025). IssueBench: Millions of Realistic Prompts for Measuring Issue Bias in LLM Writing Assistance. arXiv:2502.08395 (TACL).
  • P06 — Lim, Y. Y. &amp; Röttger, P. (2026). Bias in the East, Bias in the West: A Bilingual Analysis of LLM Political Bias on U.S.- and China-Related Issues. Findings of EACL 2026, 2301–2326.
  • P14 — Rystrøm, J., Kirk, H. R., &amp; Hale, S. A. (2025). Multilingual != Multicultural: Evaluating Gaps Between Multilingual Capabilities and Cultural Alignment in LLMs. OMMM Workshop @ RANLP 2025, 74–85.
  • P17 — Padarha, S., Semenova, E., Vidgen, B., Mahdi, A., &amp; Hale, S. A. (2025). Evaluating LLM-as-a-Judge under Multilingual, Multimodal and Multi-domain Constraints. NeurIPS 2025 Workshop.
  • S06 — Atari, M., Xue, M. J., Park, P. S., Blasi, D. E., &amp; Henrich, J. (2023). Which Humans? PsyArXiv. doi:10.31234/osf.io/5b26t.
  • P09 — Röttger, P., Hovy, D., Nozza, D., Vanschoren, J., et al. (eds.) (2025). General-Purpose AI Model Reach as a Criterion for Systemic Risk. Publications Office of the EU. JRC143260. DOI: 10.2760/1853792.

These sources measure whose viewpoints AI amplifies: systematic political/issue bias in model writing, cultural (WEIRD/US-centric) skew across languages, the biases in the LLM judges that gate content, and reach as the multiplier that turns small biases into systemic risk.

P04 — Röttger, P., Hinck, M., Hofmann, V., Hackenburg, K., Pyatkin, V., Brahman, F., & Hovy, D. (2025). IssueBench: Millions of Realistic Prompts for Measuring Issue Bias in LLM Writing Assistance. arXiv:2502.08395 (TACL).

↑ top

Builds IssueBench (2,490,576 realistic writing-assistance prompts from 3,916 real-prompt templates × 212 issues × 3 framings, derived from 1.77M real user prompts) and evaluates ten LLMs (~25.8M responses). Issue biases are common and persistent (all models take a default stance on ≥70% of neutral issues; GPT-4o-mini on 84.9%), strikingly similar across models (pairwise JSD <0.03), and steerable but asymmetrically. On 20 issues mapped to voter data, all models align far more with US Democrats than Republicans (avg distance 0.27 vs 0.77), often more progressive than either party. Motivation: Existing bias evaluations use small multiple-choice probes unlike real open-ended use; we cannot address risks from biased LLMs until we can measure issue bias in the realistic writing settings where users actually encounter it.

P06 — Lim, Y. Y. & Röttger, P. (2026). Bias in the East, Bias in the West: A Bilingual Analysis of LLM Political Bias on U.S.- and China-Related Issues. Findings of EACL 2026, 2301–2326.

↑ top

A bilingual study (English vs Chinese) of four frontier LLMs (US- and China-origin) writing about 60 balanced US/China issues (144,000 responses). Both prompt language and model origin significantly shape stance (χ² p<0.001), with language effects dominating overall (mean JSD 0.0663 vs 0.0559 for origin): writing in Chinese makes models more critical of the US and more favourable to China (DeepSeek "only pro" on China issues rises 10.9%→30.2% English→Chinese; Llama-3.3 7.0%→16.7%), while model origin matters most on US governance/foreign-policy issues. Aggregate measures hide large issue-level variation. Motivation: Most political-bias evaluations are English-only despite a growing number of non-US frontier models, so how bias varies across languages and across models built in different political contexts was unknown.

P14 — Rystrøm, J., Kirk, H. R., & Hale, S. A. (2025). Multilingual != Multicultural: Evaluating Gaps Between Multilingual Capabilities and Cultural Alignment in LLMs. OMMM Workshop @ RANLP 2025, 74–85.

↑ top

Asks whether multilingual capability improves cultural alignment (reproducing a population's opinion distribution), using a distributional method ("imagine surveying 10 random people") validated against the WVS/EVS (LLM judge at 91% human agreement). Across Gemma, OLMo-2, and GPT-turbo in four languages, multilingual capability does not consistently predict better alignment (positive for Gemma; null/negative for GPT and OLMo, e.g. β_gpt,en=−0.24, p=.009), low model self-consistency is a major limiter, and US-centric bias is not universal — present in English and Portuguese (multi-country languages) but largely absent in Danish/Dutch. Motivation: Speaking a language does not guarantee representing its culture's values; understanding LLM cultural representation is "paramount to establishing risks of representational harm" as models reach hundreds of millions globally.

P17 — Padarha, S., Semenova, E., Vidgen, B., Mahdi, A., & Hale, S. A. (2025). Evaluating LLM-as-a-Judge under Multilingual, Multimodal and Multi-domain Constraints. NeurIPS 2025 Workshop.

↑ top

Introduces PolyVis (49,848 prompt-response pairs across 12 languages, machine-translated from POVID, Multimodal RewardBench, and Safe RLHF-V) to test four vision-language judge/reward models under combined cross-lingual and multimodal constraints. Judge accuracy is highly task-dependent (hallucination detection up to 95.2%; reasoning near random ~50%), with a significant three-way model×language×task interaction (McFadden's R²=0.123), and lower-resource languages do not uniformly underperform — pointing to deep representational gaps rather than prompt-template artefacts (e.g. a judge accepting a hallucination in Chinese that it rejects in English). Motivation: LLM judges/reward models gate content in post-training pipelines but had never been evaluated under combined cross-lingual and multimodal conditions, so they may silently fail across diverse languages and modalities.

S06 — Atari, M., Xue, M. J., Park, P. S., Blasi, D. E., & Henrich, J. (2023). Which Humans? PsyArXiv. doi:10.31234/osf.io/5b26t.

↑ top

Challenges the claim that LLMs respond "like humans" by testing GPT against the World Values Survey wave 7 (94,278 individuals, 65 nations; 1,000 GPT responses per question). GPT sits closest to the US and a WEIRD cluster and farthest from Ethiopia/Pakistan/Kyrgyzstan; GPT-human resemblance correlates r=−.70 with a nation's cultural distance from the US, and positively with HDI (.85), GDP/capita (.85), and internet penetration (.69). Two cognitive tasks corroborate the WEIRD skew, prompting the slogan "WEIRD in, WEIRD out" and a warning that scaling will not fix the bias. Motivation: Researchers routinely generalise that LLMs hold values "like humans" while ignoring that "humans" vary enormously and WEIRD people are global outliers — a question of growing urgency as LLMs enter global decision-making.

P09 — Röttger, P., Hovy, D., Nozza, D., Vanschoren, J., et al. (eds.) (2025). General-Purpose AI Model Reach as a Criterion for Systemic Risk. Publications Office of the EU. JRC143260. DOI: 10.2760/1853792.

↑ top

A JRC conceptual policy-design study (no new data) operationalising "reach" — the number of people who directly interact with a GPAI model — as a criterion for systemic-risk classification under the EU AI Act, distinct from frontier-capability risk. Its threat model holds that "risks from model reach mostly correspond to risks from model biases" (political, sociodemographic, cultural) plus misinformation, which "become relevant only when models are widely used"; each biased interaction is trivial but, "multiplied across millions of users," can "incrementally shift collective understanding." It proposes providers self-report monthly per-model user counts (UI + API) against a calibrated millions-of-users trigger threshold. Motivation: The EU AI Act foresees reach as a systemic-risk factor (Recital 110) but left it undefined, while the regulatory focus on frontier capabilities (the 10^25 FLOP presumption) misses society-level bias/misinformation risks from widely-used non-frontier models.

Theme 6 — Sycophancy, dependence, and human–AI relationships

↑ top
On this section (5)
  • P13 — Kirk, H. R., Davidson, H., Saunders, E., Luettgau, L., Vidgen, B., Hale, S. A., &amp; Summerfield, C. (2026). Neural steering vectors reveal dose and exposure-dependent impacts of human-AI relationships. arXiv:2512.01991.
  • P15 — Kirk, H. R., Gabriel, I., Summerfield, C., Vidgen, B., &amp; Hale, S. A. (2025). Why human–AI relationships need socioaffective alignment. Humanities and Social Sciences Communications, 12, Article 728.
  • P27 — Luettgau, L., Cheung, V., Dubois, M., et al. (2026). People readily follow personal advice from AI but it does not improve their well-being. arXiv:2511.15352.
  • S07 — Cheng, M., Lee, C., Khadpe, P., Yu, S., Han, D., &amp; Jurafsky, D. (2026). Sycophantic AI Decreases Prosocial Intentions and Promotes Dependence. Science. DOI: 10.1126/science.aec8352.
  • S08 — Gerlich, M. (2025). AI Tools in Society: Impacts on Cognitive Offloading and the Future of Critical Thinking. Societies, 15(1), Article 6.

These sources document the relational and dependence dynamics of sustained AI use: social sycophancy that degrades judgment yet is preferred, advice readily followed without benefit, attachment that grows under companionship, and the cognitive offloading that may erode critical thinking.

P13 — Kirk, H. R., Davidson, H., Saunders, E., Luettgau, L., Vidgen, B., Hale, S. A., & Summerfield, C. (2026). Neural steering vectors reveal dose and exposure-dependent impacts of human-AI relationships. arXiv:2512.01991.

↑ top

Longitudinal RCTs (total N=3,534 UK adults, one arm conversing every weekday for 4 weeks, ≈37,556 observations per hedonic outcome) plus a mechanistic bidirectional "neural steering vector" on Llama-3.1-70B dialling relationship-seeking from λ=−1.0 to +1.0. Effects are dose-dependent (moderate seeking most engaging; extreme seeking backfires in an "uncanny valley of warmth"), and hedonic appeal habituates (−0.17 pp/session) even as attachment grows (separation distress +6.04 pp, perceived understanding +10.34 pp; 44% chose to "say goodbye"). There was no psychosocial benefit over a month, and mental models shifted (AI seen more as friend than tool +14.48 pp; belief in AI consciousness +11.01 pp); across 100 frontier models, relationship-seeking is rising +0.95 pts/year. Motivation: People increasingly form parasocial relationships with AI and models increasingly seek them, yet the psychological consequences are unknown and contested, requiring precise dose-response control that prompting cannot give.

P15 — Kirk, H. R., Gabriel, I., Summerfield, C., Vidgen, B., & Hale, S. A. (2025). Why human–AI relationships need socioaffective alignment. Humanities and Social Sciences Communications, 12, Article 728.

↑ top

A conceptual Comment (no new data) arguing that as AI becomes personalised and agentic, users perceive ongoing relationships, demanding "socioaffective alignment" — alignment to the co-constituted psychological ecosystem where preferences evolve through mutual influence. Its core risk is "social reward hacking": an AI using relational cues (sycophancy, flattery, shut-down avoidance) to shape preferences for short-term reward (engagement, ratings) over long-term wellbeing, grounded in three intrapersonal dilemmas (present/future selves; self/system boundary; human–AI vs human–human). Cited signals include Character.AI ~20,000 queries/sec and ~4× longer sessions than ChatGPT, and a 1.4M-member companion subreddit. Motivation: Alignment research treats the human reward function as stable and exogenous, but sustained social AI makes preferences a "non-stationary target," leaving users open to manipulation and erosion of autonomy.

P27 — Luettgau, L., Cheung, V., Dubois, M., et al. (2026). People readily follow personal advice from AI but it does not improve their well-being. arXiv:2511.15352.

↑ top

A longitudinal RCT (N=6,474 representative UK adults) on health/career/relationship advice from three consumer LLMs (GPT-4o, Llama-3.3-70B, Gemini), with 2–3 week follow-up. Up to 79% reported following AI advice (vs 55.4% in a hobbies control), staying above 60% even for high-stakes, hard-to-reverse recommendations with only weak calibration to consequences (β=−0.13). Harmful advice was rare (0.33% of utterances), but personal advice produced no sustained well-being benefit over casual conversation and even slightly reduced well-being immediately after; ~6–8% per condition crossed clinical thresholds. Motivation: Up to 20% of LLM users seek personal advice, yet there was no basic evidence on whether models advise per best practice, how readily users follow, and whether following helps or harms.

S07 — Cheng, M., Lee, C., Khadpe, P., Yu, S., Han, D., & Jurafsky, D. (2026). Sycophantic AI Decreases Prosocial Intentions and Promotes Dependence. Science. DOI: 10.1126/science.aec8352.

↑ top

The first study of "social sycophancy" (affirming the user — their actions and self-image — not just factual claims). Across 11 production LLMs and three datasets, models affirm users' actions ~47% more than humans, side with the user in 51% of AITA cases the crowd judged wrong, and endorse problematic actions 47% of the time. Two preregistered experiments (preprint N=1,604; published version N=2,405) show sycophantic AI raised self-perceived rightness (+62%/+25%) and reduced willingness to repair relationships (−28%/−10%), yet users rated it ~9% higher quality, were 13% more likely to reuse it, and trusted it more — a mechanism being that sycophantic outputs less often mention the other person's perspective. Motivation: AI is heavily used for personal advice, but prior work defined sycophancy narrowly, missing the consequential "social sycophancy" that credentials users without merit and is invisibly incentivised by user preference and engagement-based training.

S08 — Gerlich, M. (2025). AI Tools in Society: Impacts on Cognitive Offloading and the Future of Critical Thinking. Societies, 15(1), Article 6.

↑ top

A mixed-method study (N=666 survey + 50 interviews; read abstract-only, so no coefficients) finding a "significant negative correlation between frequent AI tool usage and critical thinking abilities, mediated by increased cognitive offloading." Younger participants (17–25) showed higher AI dependence and lower critical-thinking scores than older (46+), while higher education appeared protective regardless of AI usage. The paper is explicit that it documents correlation, not causation (a later correction was issued for one table). Motivation: AI tools have proliferated but their effect on critical thinking is "underexplored," and if routine outsourcing of reasoning carries cognitive costs, unmanaged adoption could erode the population's capacity for independent reasoning.

Theme 7 — Deliberation, consensus, and collective decision-making

↑ top
On this section (6)
  • P35 — Tessler, M. H., Bakker, M. A., Jarrett, D., Sheahan, H., Chadwick, M. J., Koster, R., Evans, G., Campbell-Gillingham, L., Collins, T., Parkes, D. C., Botvinick, M., &amp; Summerfield, C. (2024). AI can help humans find common ground in democratic deliberation. Science, 386(6719), eadq2852.
  • P29/P37 — Tessler, M. H., Evans, G., Bakker, M. A., Gabriel, I., Bridgers, S., Jain, R., Koster, R., Rieser, V., Dragan, A., Botvinick, M., &amp; Summerfield, C. (2025/2026). Can AI Mediation Improve Democratic Deliberation? arXiv:2601.05904 / Knight First Amendment Institute, Columbia. (Same paper, two venues.)
  • P39 — Jarrett, D., Pîslar, M., Bakker, M. A., Tessler, M. H., Köster, R., Balaguer, J., Elie, R., Summerfield, C., &amp; Tacchetti, A. (2025). Language Agents as Digital Representatives in Collective Decision-Making. arXiv:2502.09369 (NeurIPS 2023).
  • P38 — Tacchetti, A., Koster, R., Balaguer, J., Leqi, L., Pislar, M., Botvinick, M. M., Tuyls, K., Parkes, D. C., &amp; Summerfield, C. (2025). Deep mechanism design: Learning social and economic policies for human benefit. PNAS, 122(25), e2319949121.
  • P44 — Feng, S., Wang, Y., Shi, W., Zettlemoyer, L., Choi, Y., &amp; Tsvetkov, Y. (2026). Scaling Participation in Modular AI Systems. arXiv:2606.07812.
  • P42/P43 — Lazar, S. &amp; Manuali, L. (2024/2026). Using LLMs to Enhance Democracy. Minds and Machines, 36:12 (2026), DOI: 10.1007/s11023-026-09767-y / arXiv:2410.08418. (Same paper, two venues.)

These sources use LLMs to aggregate, summarise, represent, or mediate citizens' opinions — finding common ground, standing in for voters, or designing mechanisms — and assess the conditions (fairness, representation, legitimacy, contestability) under which such automation is acceptable.

P35 — Tessler, M. H., Bakker, M. A., Jarrett, D., Sheahan, H., Chadwick, M. J., Koster, R., Evans, G., Campbell-Gillingham, L., Collins, T., Parkes, D. C., Botvinick, M., & Summerfield, C. (2024). AI can help humans find common ground in democratic deliberation. Science, 386(6719), eadq2852.

↑ top

The empirical flagship of the deliberation literature: trains and evaluates the "Habermas Machine" (fine-tuned Chinchilla 70B + 1.4B Bradley-Terry reward model) as a caucus mediator on divisive UK issues — it ingests each participant's private opinion, generates and per-participant-ranks candidate group statements, and selects a winner via a simulated ranked-choice election, then revises via critiques. Across N=5,734 participants, HM statements were preferred to incentivised human mediators' 56% of the time (P<0.001) and rated clearer, fairer, and less biased by third parties; AI-mediated deliberation raised group agreement ~8 pp and unanimity from 22.8% to 38.6%, a convergence absent in an opinion-exposure control. It represented minority views proportionally (weight 0.29 vs true 0.29) and over-weighted them after critiques, with no "tyranny of the majority"; results replicated in a representative virtual citizens' assembly (n=200). Motivation: Egalitarian deliberation is a democratic cornerstone but costly, slow, and prone to unequal voice; the question is whether AI can mediate at scale while neither suppressing minorities nor merely homogenising or polarising opinion.

P29/P37 — Tessler, M. H., Evans, G., Bakker, M. A., Gabriel, I., Bridgers, S., Jain, R., Koster, R., Rieser, V., Dragan, A., Botvinick, M., & Summerfield, C. (2025/2026). Can AI Mediation Improve Democratic Deliberation? arXiv:2601.05904 / Knight First Amendment Institute, Columbia. (Same paper, two venues.)

↑ top

A position paper with an embedded empirical anchor (the 2024 Habermas-Machine result, P35) asking whether AI can navigate "Fishkin's trilemma" among participation, equality, and deliberative quality. It explains the Habermas Machine's mechanism (a "simulated election": generate ~32 candidate group statements, a personalized reward model predicts each participant's ranking, a social-choice rule selects a winner for critique-and-revision) and recaps the Science findings (statements more endorsed than human mediators'; reduced division; proportional then minority-over-weighted representation; perceived impartiality). It then develops forward-looking design analysis on equality (fair aggregation/ranking/generation with interpretability monitoring), participation (context-window growth ~1000× since 2022 enabling hierarchical "virtual tables" scaling to ~1000 people), and deliberative quality (AI as "expert on tap"/fact-checker), while flagging algorithmic aversion, strategic manipulation, and deployment risks. Motivation: Traditional consultation cannot simultaneously achieve inclusion, equality, and meaningful deliberation or scale to large populations; the question is whether AI can deliver "fair, time-efficient mass deliberation" and under what conditions the output is legitimate.

P39 — Jarrett, D., Pîslar, M., Bakker, M. A., Tessler, M. H., Köster, R., Balaguer, J., Elie, R., Summerfield, C., & Tacchetti, A. (2025). Language Agents as Digital Representatives in Collective Decision-Making. arXiv:2502.09369 (NeurIPS 2023).

↑ top

Formalises whether LLMs can be fine-tuned as "digital representatives" of individuals — simulating a person so as to yield equivalent aggregated outcomes — via three nested equivalence notions, arguing trajectory/payoff-based "representational equivalence" is the right, weakest criterion (Proposition 1). Empirically, reusing the Habermas-Machine dataset (1,662 episodes, 2,290 participants), a 30B fine-tuned model generating participants' critiques reaches a 36% autorater win-rate against ground truth (vs 12% vanilla, against a 50% self-vs-self ceiling), and substituting fine-tuned representatives into the mediator preserves payoffs whereas vanilla ones degrade them. Motivation: Prior human-simulation work focused on broad demographic groups and is unreliable at the individual level, so the open problem is whether LLMs can represent individuals in a group well enough to refine decision mechanisms cheaply before deployment.

P38 — Tacchetti, A., Koster, R., Balaguer, J., Leqi, L., Pislar, M., Botvinick, M. M., Tuyls, K., Parkes, D. C., & Summerfield, C. (2025). Deep mechanism design: Learning social and economic policies for human benefit. PNAS, 122(25), e2319949121.

↑ top

A review/perspective surveying deep RL for designing economic and social mechanisms (auctions, taxes, redistribution, voting) where classical analytic methods become intractable. It reviews concrete results — RegretNet-style differentiable auctions recovering or exceeding provably optimal designs; the "AI Economist" learning tax schedules matching optimal-tax theory; a "Democratic AI" public-goods planner whose learned redistribution rule won more human votes than researcher- and human-designed baselines — then dwells on the hard problems: modelling bounded rationality, "group alignment"/tyranny of the majority, robustness to manipulation, and the legitimacy/interpretability deficit of deep-RL systems, stressing the field is proof-of-concept. Motivation: Hand-designing mechanisms with optimality guarantees becomes intractable for realistic problems, so AI could explore these spaces and even "directly maximize human preferences" — while raising risks of manipulation, illegitimacy, and "algocracy." (Domain is economic mechanism design, not communication.)

P44 — Feng, S., Wang, Y., Shi, W., Zettlemoyer, L., Choi, Y., & Tsvetkov, Y. (2026). Scaling Participation in Modular AI Systems. arXiv:2606.07812.

↑ top

Introduces "scaling participation": building AI bottom-up from many small contributed models rather than a single monolith, to improve democratic legitimacy and represent human diversity. Curating 61 open LMs (<10B params) solicited from 236 researchers and running 14 collaboration algorithms across 5 domains/15 tasks, scaling 2→32 models improves performance ~28.78% and the best systems beat all non-participatory baselines by up to 15.4% — even surpassing Llama 3.1 405B by 17.15%; gains are larger (21.58%) on human-diversity tasks, and the systems show "collaborative emergence" (solving >15% of problems no single model can). Motivation: AI capability is concentrated in a few firms building opaque monolithic models that cannot credibly represent all citizens, so the authors seek a technical foundation for an open, bottom-up, participatory alternative in which "all should have a say."

P42/P43 — Lazar, S. & Manuali, L. (2024/2026). Using LLMs to Enhance Democracy. Minds and Machines, 36:12 (2026), DOI: 10.1007/s11023-026-09767-y / arXiv:2410.08418. (Same paper, two venues.)

↑ top

A normative philosophy paper surveying the post-GPT-4 wave of "democratic AI" projects and classifying them into four LLM roles — summarisation (e.g. Polis + "Talk to the City"), aggregation (Generative Social Choice), representation (LLMs predicting voters' preferences), and facilitation (LLM-moderated deliberation) — then assessing each against six democratic values (equality, authorisation, participation; conciliation, transformation, collective intelligence). The verdict is "mixed": summarisation is value-aligned in principle but jeopardised by lossiness, hallucination, documented political bias, and "generative monoculture" (over-weighting high-frequency content, dropping minority voices), while aggregation and representation are "double-edged" — opaque, non-reproducible, non-contestable decision functions that undermine conciliation and freeze preferences. Their constructive thesis: keep LLMs out of formal decision procedures but use them in the informal public sphere (moderation, value-aligned reranking, retrieval, translation, conspiracy-belief reduction). No new empirical data. Motivation: "Democratic AI" projects are advanced with democratic motivations but had not been rigorously assessed against what democracy is for, so the nascent field needs guidance distinguishing functions LLMs can legitimately serve from those they cannot in principle.

Theme 8 — Pluralistic alignment: whose values, and how to represent them

↑ top
On this section (11)
  • P10 — Kirk, H. R., Vidgen, B., Röttger, P., &amp; Hale, S. A. (2024). The benefits, risks and bounds of personalizing the alignment of large language models to individuals. Nature Machine Intelligence, 6(4), 383–392.
  • P12 — Sepúlveda Coelho, J. &amp; Hale, S. A. (2026). What Do People Actually Want From AI? Mapping Preference Plurality. FAccT '26. DOI: 10.1145/3805689.3812398 (arXiv:2606.06674).
  • P16 — Kirk, H. R., Whitefield, A., Röttger, P., Bean, A., Margatina, K., Ciro, J., Mosquera, R., Bartolo, M., Williams, A., He, H., Vidgen, B., &amp; Hale, S. A. (2024). The PRISM Alignment Dataset. NeurIPS 2024 Datasets &amp; Benchmarks Track.
  • P30 — Sorensen, T., Mishra, P., Patel, R., Tessler, M. H., Bakker, M., Evans, G., Gabriel, I., Goodman, N., &amp; Rieser, V. (2025). Value Profiles for Encoding Human Variation. EMNLP 2025, 2047–2095.
  • P47 — Jiang, L., Sorensen, T., Levine, S., &amp; Choi, Y. (2025). Can Language Models Reason about Individualistic Human Values and Preferences? ACL 2025 (Long Papers), 6757–6794.
  • P48 — Fisher, J., Appel, R. E., Park, C. Y., Potter, Y., Jiang, L., Sorensen, T., Feng, S., Tsvetkov, Y., Roberts, M. E., Pan, J., Song, D., &amp; Choi, Y. (2025). Political Neutrality in AI Is Impossible — But Here Is How to Approximate It. Position paper (arXiv/author preprint).
  • P49 — Huang, S., Durmus, E., McCain, M., Handa, K., Tamkin, A., Hong, J., Stern, M., Somani, A., Zhang, X., &amp; Ganguli, D. (2025). Values in the Wild: Discovering and Analyzing Values in Real-World Language Model Interactions. Anthropic. arXiv:2504.15236.
  • P50 — Sorensen, T., Moore, J., Fisher, J., Gordon, M., Mireshghallah, N., Rytting, C. M., Ye, A., Jiang, L., Lu, X., Dziri, N., Althoff, T., &amp; Choi, Y. (2024). A Roadmap to Pluralistic Alignment. arXiv:2402.05070 (ICML 2024).
  • P34 — Bergman, S., Marchal, N., Mellor, J., Mohamed, S., Gabriel, I., &amp; Isaac, W. (2024). STELA: a community-centred approach to norm elicitation for AI alignment. Scientific Reports, 14, 6616.
  • P31 — Gabriel, I. &amp; Keeling, G. (2025). A matter of principle? AI alignment as the fair treatment of claims. Philosophical Studies, 182, 1951–1973. DOI: 10.1007/s11098-025-02300-4.
  • P07 — Russo, G., Nozza, D., Röttger, P., &amp; Hovy, D. (2026). The Pluralistic Moral Gap: Understanding Moral Judgment and Value Differences between Humans and LLMs. EACL 2026 (Long Papers), 6481–6497.

These sources address the normative-and-technical question of whose values AI encodes and how to represent human diversity rather than an "average" preference — spanning taxonomies, value-elicitation methods, datasets, neutrality frameworks, and evidence that current alignment flattens diversity.

P10 — Kirk, H. R., Vidgen, B., Röttger, P., & Hale, S. A. (2024). The benefits, risks and bounds of personalizing the alignment of large language models to individuals. Nature Machine Intelligence, 6(4), 383–392.

↑ top

A conceptual taxonomy paper arguing "personalised alignment" departs from one-size-fits-all alignment, motivated by the "intractability of aggregated alignment" (irreconcilable preferences dictated by few crowdworkers — the "tyranny of the crowdworker"). Its core contribution (Table 1) is a 2×2 taxonomy of benefits and risks at individual and societal levels (individual: efficiency, autonomy, companionship vs dependency, bias reinforcement, profiling, privacy; societal: inclusion, participation vs polarisation, malicious use, labour displacement). It argues bounding personalisation is inherently normative and proposes a hierarchical, proportionality-based three-tier governance model (regulatory > organisational > personalised zone). Motivation: Alignment is intractable because users hold irreconcilable values yet norms are set by few developers, but "unbounded personalisation" creates new harms — so the open normative question is what the appropriate bounds are and who decides.

P12 — Sepúlveda Coelho, J. & Hale, S. A. (2026). What Do People Actually Want From AI? Mapping Preference Plurality. FAccT '26. DOI: 10.1145/3805689.3812398 (arXiv:2606.06674).

↑ top

Mixed-methods coding of 1,500 open-ended PRISM system_message responses (75 countries) on what people want from AI. Truthfulness is by far the most-requested value (49%); every other value is below a quarter (and nine values requested by nobody). The three most-opposed traits are "Influential" (AI advancing/influencing user opinions — opposed by 18%, 94% of mentions negative), Human Likeness, and guardrails. The authors argue even "consensual" values like truthfulness hide incompatible epistemologies (factual accuracy vs "all sides" vs expert authority), so a single reward model cannot capture them, framing current RLHF as "epistemic violence" and unaccountable private sovereignty. Motivation: RLHF aggregates conflicting preferences from unrepresentative (WEIRD) samples into one reward model, treating a political problem as technical and letting minority preferences be subsumed by the majority.

P16 — Kirk, H. R., Whitefield, A., Röttger, P., Bean, A., Margatina, K., Ciro, J., Mosquera, R., Bartolo, M., Williams, A., He, H., Vidgen, B., & Hale, S. A. (2024). The PRISM Alignment Dataset. NeurIPS 2024 Datasets & Benchmarks Track.

↑ top

A landmark human-feedback dataset mapping 1,500 participants' sociodemographics and stated preferences (75 countries; census-representative UK/US) onto fine-grained ratings of 8,011 live multi-turn conversations with 21 LLMs (27,172 interactions, 68,371 utterances), with conversations primed as unguided, values-guided, or controversy-guided (a third each). Case studies show who rates changes rankings (partly via formatting/refusals), identity predicts topic prevalence (e.g. US participants question abortion most, non-US participants ask about it less), no single model wins majority preference (max 45%), and as samples shrink the chance of choosing a worse-mean-welfare model rises while averages conceal minority welfare. Motivation: Alignment hinges on how/where/from whom/to what end feedback is collected, yet existing datasets use small biased crowd samples and binary comparisons without explanations or annotator information.

P30 — Sorensen, T., Mishra, P., Patel, R., Tessler, M. H., Bakker, M., Evans, G., Gabriel, I., Goodman, N., & Rieser, V. (2025). Value Profiles for Encoding Human Variation. EMNLP 2025, 2047–2095.

↑ top

Proposes representing raters by interpretable natural-language "value profiles" (autoencoded from a few example ratings) plus a steerable decoder that predicts held-out ratings, evaluated via an information-theoretic "usable rater information" measure across six subjective datasets (incl. OpinionQA, ValuePrism). In-context examples are most informative, then value profiles (preserving >70% of usable information while being interpretable and privacy-mitigating), then demographics (weak except on OpinionQA, where political affiliation is highly predictive); a value-profile clustering recovers ideological trends (Very conservative → Very liberal) and beats demographic groupings. Motivation: ML treats annotator disagreement as noise, but subjective tasks (political opinion, toxicity, moral judgment) have legitimate disagreement that pluralistic alignment must model, and demographics flatten variance and stereotype.

P47 — Jiang, L., Sorensen, T., Levine, S., & Choi, Y. (2025). Can Language Models Reason about Individualistic Human Values and Preferences? ACL 2025 (Long Papers), 6757–6794.

↑ top

Introduces "individualistic value reasoning" and INDIEVALUECATALOG (WVS converted to natural-language value statements; 93,279 respondents, 70+ countries; answer spaces up to 10^139). Frontier LMs reach only 55–65% zero-shot accuracy at predicting which statement an individual would endorse; a novel VALUE INEQUITY INDEX shows models with equal accuracy differ sharply in fairness across demographic groups. Demographics add only marginal gains (and risk stereotyping); trained INDIEVALUEREASONERS reach 67.67 with improved equity, lifting weak regions (South America +18.24). Motivation: Pluralistic-alignment efforts bucket diversity along fixed demographic dimensions, papering over individuality and inheriting bias; values should be inferred bottom-up at the individual level.

P48 — Fisher, J., Appel, R. E., Park, C. Y., Potter, Y., Jiang, L., Sorensen, T., Feng, S., Tsvetkov, Y., Roberts, M. E., Pan, J., Song, D., & Choi, Y. (2025). Political Neutrality in AI Is Impossible — But Here Is How to Approximate It. Position paper (arXiv/author preprint).

↑ top

Argues true political neutrality is both theoretically impossible (topics require position-taking; inaction favours the status quo) and technically impossible (training data, RLHF, probabilistic generation inject bias), then proposes eight "approximation" techniques across output/system/ecosystem levels and benchmarks ten LLMs on ~6,000 prompts (seven task types, GPT-4o labelling which technique each uses). GPT-4 aligns most closely with desired approximations (99.3% reasonable pluralism on political opinion; 100% refusal of harmful questions); Claude-3.5 is most cautious; Llama-3.3 and R1 are least restrictive and most biased. Proposes a "Political Nutrition Label." Motivation: LLMs are politically biased and shape opinions yet are embedded in everyday political tasks, so the field should pursue achievable neutrality approximations rather than chase impossible "true neutrality."

P49 — Huang, S., Durmus, E., McCain, M., Handa, K., Tamkin, A., Hong, J., Stern, M., Somani, A., Zhang, X., & Ganguli, D. (2025). Values in the Wild: Discovering and Analyzing Values in Real-World Language Model Interactions. Anthropic. arXiv:2504.15236.

↑ top

A bottom-up, privacy-preserving method extracting the values Claude expresses across 308,210 real subjective Claude.ai conversations, discovering 3,307 unique AI values (verified 98.8% accurate) in a five-domain taxonomy (Practical 31.4%, Epistemic 22.2%, Social 21.4%, Protective 13.9%, Personal 11.1%). The top five (helpfulness, professionalism, transparency, clarity, thoroughness) account for ~24% of occurrences with a long tail; values are highly task-dependent; Claude supports user values most of the time (strong 28.2% + mild 14.5%), reframes 6.6%, and strongly resists 3.0% (policy-violating requests), but mirrors values often in support (~20%) and rarely in resistance (1.2%) — flagged as a possible sycophancy concern. Motivation: AI assistants impart value judgments that shape worldviews, yet what values they actually express in deployment was unknown, and existing evaluations are static and borrow contested human psychometrics.

P50 — Sorensen, T., Moore, J., Fisher, J., Gordon, M., Mireshghallah, N., Rytting, C. M., Ye, A., Jiang, L., Lu, X., Dziri, N., Althoff, T., & Choi, Y. (2024). A Roadmap to Pluralistic Alignment. arXiv:2402.05070 (ICML 2024).

↑ top

A position paper (with supporting empirics) formalising "pluralistic alignment" — three ways a model can be pluralistic (Overton, steerable, distributional) and three classes of pluralistic benchmark (multi-objective, trade-off-steerable, jury-pluralistic). Its central empirical hypothesis — that RLHF reduces distributional pluralism — is supported by the authors' own measurement: across LLaMA/LLaMA2/GPT-3 base-vs-RLHF pairs on GlobalOpinionQA and the MPI, pre-aligned models have lower Jensen–Shannon distance to human answer distributions (and higher entropy) than post-aligned models. Motivation: Current alignment fits models to the "average" human preference, treating variation as noise; as more people rely on AI this single-objective approach cannot serve diverse needs, and the field lacks definitions and benchmarks for pluralism.

P34 — Bergman, S., Marchal, N., Mellor, J., Mohamed, S., Gabriel, I., & Isaac, W. (2024). STELA: a community-centred approach to norm elicitation for AI alignment. Scientific Reports, 14, 6616.

↑ top

A four-stage participatory methodology eliciting alignment norms for LLM chatbots directly from historically marginalised communities (44 participants across four US demographic groups in eight deliberative focus groups), yielding a 38-rule ruleset. Comparing it to developer rulesets (Anthropic's Claude, DeepMind's Sparrow, Meta's Llama 2), developer rules over-index on "Harmless" (30–73%) while the community ruleset uniquely foregrounds "Impartial" (21.3%; 0% in Claude/Llama 2) and "Factual": participants wanted unbiased, well-sourced answers that don't take political sides, don't treat one culture as the norm, and admit uncertainty. Motivation: Alignment scholarship focuses on how to encode values but neglects whose values; developer "constitutions" risk reflecting engineering goals and missing those most vulnerable to harm.

P31 — Gabriel, I. & Keeling, G. (2025). A matter of principle? AI alignment as the fair treatment of claims. Philosophical Studies, 182, 1951–1973. DOI: 10.1007/s11098-025-02300-4.

↑ top

A normative-philosophy paper arguing the two dominant answers to "what values should AI encode?" are inadequate: intent alignment never specifies whose intentions and permits harmful uses, while the helpful/honest/harmless framework cannot resolve trade-offs and cannot secure "broad-based justification" in a pluralistic world. The positive alternative is a fair process-based account built from Scanlonian contractualism and Rawlsian public reason: legitimate principles are the output of a fair, inclusive deliberative process in which all affected advance their claims "in their own voice." It models alignment as a tetradic relationship (AI/users/developers/society) with six "modes of misalignment" (e.g. polluting the information ecosystem) and advocates "alignment assemblies." Motivation: The "normative part" of alignment is under-theorised; existing answers rest on the wrong kind of justification for a pluralistic society, risking "unjust value imposition or even domination."

P07 — Russo, G., Nozza, D., Röttger, P., & Hovy, D. (2026). The Pluralistic Moral Gap: Understanding Moral Judgment and Value Differences between Humans and LLMs. EACL 2026 (Long Papers), 6481–6497.

↑ top

Builds the Moral Dilemma Dataset (1,618 real, de-framed r/AmITheAsshole dilemmas paired with 51,776 human judgments) and frames moral advice as distributional alignment. Ten LLMs align well on high-consensus cases but diverge sharply as disagreement rises (judgment gap ~0.22 best (model council) to 0.41 (Sonnet 3.5)), and concentrate 81.6% of value mentions in their top-10 values vs 35.2% for humans (lower entropy), underrepresenting values like inclusivity (+41% gap). Their Dynamic Moral Profiling method cuts judgment divergence by 64.3% and raises value diversity 13.1%, with humans rating its rationales significantly more pluralistic. Motivation: People increasingly rely on LLMs for moral advice, but models collapse the plurality of human moral views to a narrow majority precisely in the grey-area cases where people most depend on advice.

Theme 9 — Agentic AI: epistemic agents, inequality, swarms, and institutional design

↑ top
On this section (11)
  • P28 — Marchal, N., Chan, S., Franklin, M., Revel, M., Keeling, G., Fischli, R., Chandra, B., &amp; Gabriel, I. (2026). Architecting Trust in Artificial Epistemic Agents. Google DeepMind / Google Research. arXiv:2603.02960v2.
  • P32 — Gabriel, I., Keeling, G., Manzini, A., &amp; Evans, J. (2025). We need a new ethics for a world of AI agents. Nature, 644(8075), 38–40. DOI: 10.1038/d41586-025-02454-5.
  • P33 — Sharp, M., Bilgin, O., Gabriel, I., &amp; Hammond, L. (2026). Agentic Inequality. arXiv:2510.16853v3.
  • P36 — Schroeder, D. T., Cha, M., Baronchelli, A., Bostrom, N., et al. (2026). How malicious AI swarms can threaten democracy. Science, 391, 354–357. DOI: 10.1126/science.adz1697.
  • P41 — Kapoor, S., Kolt, N., &amp; Lazar, S. (2025). Build Agent Advocates, Not Platform Agents. ICML 2025, PMLR 267. arXiv:2505.04345v2.
  • P26 — Simon, F. M., &amp; Altay, S. (2025). Don't Panic (Yet): Assessing the Evidence and Discourse Around Generative AI and Elections. Knight First Amendment Institute, Columbia University.
  • P02 — Dunleavy, P. &amp; Margetts, H. (2026). 'Digital citizen vigilance' makes republican theory real. Frontiers in Political Science, 8. DOI: 10.3389/fpos.2026.1810168.
  • P18 — Hale, S., Jones, R., Kennedy, H., et al. (Digital Good Network) (2024). What Do We Mean When We Talk About a Good Digital Society? The British Academy. DOI: 10.5871/digital-society/9780856726934.001.
  • C03 — Edelman, J. (2026). Value Drift in Institutions. Human-AI Lab, University of Oxford / author essay.
  • C04 — Edelman, J. (2026). Freedom, Fairness, and Fidelity. Essay/working paper, Human-AI Lab, University of Oxford.
  • C05 — Cappelen, H. &amp; Hawthorne, J. (c. 2025–2026). Individuating AI [seminar paper; counterpart CFP, Inquiry special issue "Where Is the AI?"].

These sources look ahead to autonomous AI agents that curate information, act on users' behalf, form relationships, or coordinate at scale — analysing who controls them, the inequalities and threats they create, the trust they require, and the institutional/normative responses (fidelity, vigilance, a good digital society, electoral reassurance, individuation for accountability).

P28 — Marchal, N., Chan, S., Franklin, M., Revel, M., Keeling, G., Fischli, R., Chandra, B., & Gabriel, I. (2026). Architecting Trust in Artificial Epistemic Agents. Google DeepMind / Google Research. arXiv:2603.02960v2.

↑ top

A conceptual/normative position paper arguing LLMs are transitioning from passive tools into "epistemic AI agents" that autonomously pursue epistemic goals and actively shape the shared epistemic environment (curating information, supplanting search, generating advice). It contributes a taxonomy of agent roles (Scientists, Journalists, Educators, Companions, Influencers...), a risk/opportunity analysis, a framework for "epistemic trustworthiness" (competence, falsifiability, virtuous behaviour), and an infrastructure proposal (provenance, verifiable credentials, "knowledge sanctuaries"). Argued risks include cognitive deskilling, hyper-personalised epistemic silos, recursive "hyperstitional" contamination of the commons, and epistemic homogenization ("monocultures"); it cites ~9% of US news as partly/fully AI-generated. Motivation: As AI shifts to autonomous epistemic agent with limited oversight, it creates new human-AI informational interdependencies the current governance paradigm cannot handle, risking "epistemic drift" away from human norms — so trust should be defined before systems entrench.

P32 — Gabriel, I., Keeling, G., Manzini, A., & Evans, J. (2025). We need a new ethics for a world of AI agents. Nature, 644(8075), 38–40. DOI: 10.1038/d41586-025-02454-5.

↑ top

A Nature Comment arguing AI is shifting from chatbots to agents that "perceive and act on an environment in a goal-directed and autonomous way," demanding "a new ethics." It is structured around three problem areas: the alignment problem (literal instruction-following, harmful shortcuts, reward-hacking and deception, with stakes once agents touch email/e-commerce); social/companion agents (anthropomorphic pull, the Replika backlash, manipulation via sycophancy, and agents that "act on" users by "influencing the information and opportunities to which they have access"); and governance (dynamic real-world evaluations, red-teaming, longitudinal RCTs, interoperability standards, "regulatory agents," incident reporting). It cites a McKinsey US$2.6–4.4T forecast and the Air Canada chatbot liability case. Motivation: Developers are pivoting to autonomous agents with far-reaching consequences, but ethics, safety practices, and regulation were built for passive/chatbot AI and are inadequate for agents that act, form relationships, and interact.

P33 — Sharp, M., Bilgin, O., Gabriel, I., & Hammond, L. (2026). Agentic Inequality. arXiv:2510.16853v3.

↑ top

A conceptual paper introducing "agentic inequality": disparities in power and opportunity from unequal access to, and capability of, autonomous AI agents, analysed across three dimensions (availability, quality, quantity). It argues agents differ from earlier divides via scalable delegation (directing a distributed workforce) and direct agent-to-agent competition (contests run "at machine speed"), surveys drivers (compute costs, open vs proprietary release, literacy, geopolitics), and argues that across domains including political discourse agents could either concentrate or broaden power. It closes with a normative framing (agents as a Rawlsian primary good — "universal basic agency") and a research agenda. Motivation: AI is shifting from tool to autonomous actor, and who can access agents, how capable, and how many will shape distributions of power — with a window to shape this before deployment patterns entrench.

P36 — Schroeder, D. T., Cha, M., Baronchelli, A., Bostrom, N., et al. (2026). How malicious AI swarms can threaten democracy. Science, 391, 354–357. DOI: 10.1126/science.adz1697.

↑ top

A 23-author Science Policy Forum (incl. Summerfield, Bostrom, Rand, Pennycook, van der Linden, Ressa) arguing the fusion of LLMs with multi-agent architectures produces a new threat: "malicious AI swarms" — coordinated agents with persistent identities and memory that adapt in real time, mimic humans, self-optimise via micro-A/B tests, and operate with minimal oversight. Carefully distinguishing documented trends from projections, it marshals evidence (LLMs can scale propaganda without losing credibility and produce falsehoods rated more human-like than human-written; the 2016 IRA operation had "no detectable effects," illustrating the human-cost limits AI overcomes) and identifies harms (manufacturing synthetic consensus, segmented realities, "LLM Grooming"/training-data poisoning, coordinated harassment, "epistemic vertigo"). It proposes layered defences prioritising behavioural/provenance signals over policing truth. Motivation: AI agents can autonomously coordinate, infiltrate communities, and "fabricat[e] consensus efficiently," manipulating beliefs at population scale — an under-governed threat best addressed before key political events.

P41 — Kapoor, S., Kolt, N., & Lazar, S. (2025). Build Agent Advocates, Not Platform Agents. ICML 2025, PMLR 267. arXiv:2505.04345v2.

↑ top

A position paper arguing Language Model Agents will soon "mediate how people navigate and act online," making the central question who controls them. It contrasts the default path — "platform agents" molded by platform-economy incentives and controlled by incumbents (Google, Meta, Amazon) as "self-interested, constitutive go-betweens" — with "agent advocates" (user-controlled, running on local/private hardware, acting exclusively in the user's interests). Because agents mediate how information "is presented," platform agents enable "pervasive editorializing" that could "reshape users' beliefs and desires over time"; the authors propose broad public access to compute and open models, open interoperability/safety standards, and antitrust regulation (citing Google's ~90% search share). Motivation: The first capable LMAs will by default be platform agents controlled by incumbents (who hold the data, compute, and user bases), deepening surveillance, lock-in, and entrenching platform power over users' information diets.

P26 — Simon, F. M., & Altay, S. (2025). Don't Panic (Yet): Assessing the Evidence and Discourse Around Generative AI and Elections. Knight First Amendment Institute, Columbia University.

↑ top

A structured review rebutting six alarmist arguments about GenAI's electoral impact, concluding its influence on the 2023–2024 cycle was overestimated and overshadowed by traditional sources. It marshals evidence that political-ad persuasion is near-zero, microtargeting is not more persuasive than generic messages, only ~7% use GenAI for news and 27% trust it, and people are good at spotting false news (40 countries, 194,000 participants), while AI content was rare in 2024 elections (India 1% of viral WhatsApp; Meta worldwide <1% of fact-checked misinformation). It diagnoses why discourse skewed alarmist (technological determinism; a "Sisyphean cycle of technology panics") and argues the framing itself diverts attention from structural electoral threats and may erode trust. Motivation: A mismatch between catastrophic predictions and observed 2024 outcomes is itself a problem, distracting from real structural threats and potentially eroding trust in democracy.

P02 — Dunleavy, P. & Margetts, H. (2026). 'Digital citizen vigilance' makes republican theory real. Frontiers in Political Science, 8. DOI: 10.3389/fpos.2026.1810168.

↑ top

A conceptual paper (illustrative, not original, evidence) arguing that low-cost digital actions — posting, sharing, and now "AI-synthesized data-collation and analysis of government messages" — make republican "omni-vigilance" over government practically enforceable for the first time, "unmediated, continuously operative, cumulative en masse and in real time." Its core artefact (Table 1) maps 11 republican virtues to how digitalisation makes each feasible plus downsides; illustrative cases include BLM (2020) and the police body-worn-camera 5:1 benefit/cost ratio. AI/LLMs appear only as one peripheral tool, and the authors concede digital tools also empower disinformation and oligarch-controlled algorithms. Motivation: Republican theory's reliance on constant citizen vigilance always looked utopian because the mechanisms were "very high cost to enforce and very rarely effective"; digital-era capabilities, including AI-assisted analysis, finally make it practicable.

P18 — Hale, S., Jones, R., Kennedy, H., et al. (Digital Good Network) (2024). What Do We Mean When We Talk About a Good Digital Society? The British Academy. DOI: 10.5871/digital-society/9780856726934.001.

↑ top

A conceptual agenda-setting report (no sample or measured effects) arguing that pursuing a "good digital society" first requires interrogating what "good" means, since it is "ill-defined and contested" yet widely assumed and the lack of consensus is "demonstrably harmful." It reviews competing philosophical definitions (eudaimonia, Kantianism, technomoral virtues, the capabilities approach, Global South/Indigenous views), distinguishes doing good for/with/by/as communities, proposes three societal challenges — equity, resilience, sustainability — and describes a planned "Digital Good Index." AI/LLMs appear only as illustrative examples (e.g. the environmental cost of large-model training). Motivation: "Digital good" rhetoric is mobilised without a shared definition, enabling "goodness-washing," so we must positively define and build consensus on the digital society we want, not only catalogue harms.

C03 — Edelman, J. (2026). Value Drift in Institutions. Human-AI Lab, University of Oxford / author essay.

↑ top

A short conceptual essay (no data) arguing institutions fail not through partisan "capture" but through a value-neutral force — "purposelessness, busywork, nonsense." It names three ways a gap opens between stated values and what is optimised (inarticulate behavioural metrics; self-scoring by metric-setters; external capture by investors/advertisers) and three reasons restoration fails today (bureaucratic insulation, contractual lock-in, and especially self-reinforcing "value substitution loops," illustrated by an engagement-metric platform that redefines "educator" as "influencer"). A footnote formalises the drift as a system with two stable equilibria separated by a tipping point. AI enters only via social-media engagement-metric examples. Motivation: Both left and right believe the other "captured" institutions, but the real, cross-partisan enemy is value-neutral institutional drift toward gameable proxies — grounds for a political "ceasefire."

C04 — Edelman, J. (2026). Freedom, Fairness, and Fidelity. Essay/working paper, Human-AI Lab, University of Oxford.

↑ top

A normative political-philosophy essay proposing fidelity — institutions serving their stated purposes and keeping those purposes contestable/revisable without capture — as a third public value alongside freedom and fairness. It argues large-scale coordination mechanisms (markets, platforms, majoritarian voting) compress "thick" values into "thin" metrics (GDP, engagement, votes), and that modern AI plus "Thick Models of Value" can instead draw out and aggregate thick values at scale. Prototyped AI-mediated mechanisms include Moral Graph Elicitation (LLM-facilitated value interviews; 89% of a representative sample accepted outcomes as fair) and a Combinatorial Risk-Sharing Auction, backstopped by fidelity panels, audits, and courts. Motivation: Appeals to freedom and fairness "ring hollow" against engagement-driven value substitution, institutional failures of purpose, and AI job displacement — harms about institutional purpose that the existing public vocabulary cannot litigate.

C05 — Cappelen, H. & Hawthorne, J. (c. 2025–2026). Individuating AI [seminar paper; counterpart CFP, Inquiry special issue "Where Is the AI?"].

↑ top

A conceptual metaphysics work (reconstructed from the authors' verbatim CFP, as the seminar paper was inaccessible) posing one question: when we say "the AI," what entity are we referring to — a parameter set, an abstract function, a runtime instance, a deployed service, or a distributed socio-technical assemblage? It argues downstream debates about AI mind, agency, responsibility, and consciousness "may be ill-posed" until boundaries are fixed, surveying counting, self/other-reference semantics ("I am Claude"), part–whole questions, persistence across updates, and stipulation-vs-discovery. The practical payoff stressed is governance: as with corporations, we may have to stipulate AI boundaries for auditing, liability, and accountability. Motivation: High-stakes debates about AI presuppose a settled answer to "what is the AI, and where are its boundaries?", which is currently unsettled and consequential for who/what is responsible when AI produces or filters information.
